### [1] [Filtered Reasoning Score: Evaluating Reasoning Quality on a Model's Most-Confident Traces](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.11996)
*Manas Pathak,Xingyao Chen,Shuozhe Li,Amy Zhang,Liu Leqi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper argues that relying only on accuracy to evaluate LLMs is inadequate and introduces the Filtered Reasoning Score (FRS), a metric that evaluates and aggregates reasoning-trace quality to better capture true reasoning capability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations focus mainly on whether a model’s final answer is correct, ignoring whether the underlying reasoning is sound. Models can get high accuracy by memorization, shortcut learning, or coincidental success, making it impossible to distinguish genuinely strong reasoners from models that guess or overfit benchmarks. The authors aim to develop metrics that reveal the quality and robustness of reasoning itself, beyond outcome-based accuracy.

Method: The authors first define a reasoning score that evaluates individual reasoning traces along several dimensions: faithfulness (alignment between reasoning and generation process), coherence (logical consistency and structure), utility (contribution of each step toward the final answer), and factuality (truthfulness of intermediate statements). They then address the problem of aggregating these per-trace scores. Instead of simply averaging scores over many sampled traces, they propose the Filtered Reasoning Score (FRS), which selects only the top-K% most confident traces for each instance and computes aggregate reasoning quality from these filtered traces. Confidence is used to avoid overcounting low-confidence, coincidentally correct traces, especially in long-horizon tasks with many possible trajectories.

Result: Using FRS on existing reasoning benchmarks, they show that models with nearly identical standard accuracy can have substantially different reasoning quality profiles. Furthermore, they find that models with higher FRS on one reasoning benchmark tend to perform better not only in reasoning quality but also in accuracy on other benchmarks, suggesting that FRS captures a form of transferable reasoning capability that accuracy alone misses.

Conclusion: Outcome-based accuracy is insufficient to assess LLM reasoning. The proposed Filtered Reasoning Score, which evaluates and aggregates reasoning-trace quality, distinguishes between models that look similar under accuracy and better predicts cross-benchmark performance. Therefore, FRS serves as a complementary metric to accuracy for measuring and comparing the genuine reasoning abilities of LLMs, and the authors provide an open-source codebase to facilitate its adoption.

Abstract: Should we trust Large Language Models (LLMs) with high accuracy? LLMs achieve high accuracy on reasoning benchmarks, but correctness alone does not reveal the quality of the reasoning used to produce it. This highlights a fundamental limitation of outcome-based evaluation: models may arrive at correct answers through flawed reasoning, and models with substantially different reasoning capabilities can nevertheless exhibit similar benchmark accuracy, for example due to memorization or over-optimization. In this paper, we ask: given existing benchmarks, can we move beyond outcome-based evaluation to assess the quality of reasoning itself? We seek metrics that (1) differentiate models with similar accuracy and (2) are robust to variations in input prompts and generation configurations. To this end, we propose a reasoning score that evaluates reasoning traces along dimensions such as faithfulness, coherence, utility, and factuality. A remaining question is how to aggregate this score across multiple sampled traces. Naively averaging them is undesirable, particularly in long-horizon settings, where the number of possible trajectories grows rapidly, and low-confidence correct traces are more likely to be coincidental. To address this, we introduce the Filtered Reasoning Score (FRS), which computes reasoning quality using only the top-K% most confident traces. Evaluating with FRS, models that are indistinguishable under standard accuracy exhibit significant differences in reasoning quality. Moreover, models with higher FRS on one benchmark tend to perform better on other reasoning benchmarks, in both accuracy and reasoning quality. Together, these findings suggest that FRS complements accuracy by capturing a model's transferable reasoning capabilities. We open source our evaluation codebase: https://github.com/Manas2006/benchmark_reproducibility.

</details>


### [2] [Self-Distillation Zero: Self-Revision Turns Binary Rewards into Dense Supervision](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12002)
*Yinghui He,Simran Kaur,Adithya Bhaskar,Yongjin Yang,Jiarui Liu,Narutatsu Ri,Liam Fowl,Abhishek Panigrahi,Danqi Chen,Sanjeev Arora*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces SD-Zero, a self-distillation method that converts binary rewards into dense token-level supervision, improving sample efficiency and outperforming RL- and distillation-based baselines on math and code benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing verifiable post-training approaches either use RL with sparse binary rewards (sample-inefficient) or distillation requiring costly external teachers or high-quality demonstrations. There is a need for a method that is as broadly applicable as RL, uses only binary rewards, but achieves dense supervision and higher sample efficiency without external data or models.

Method: SD-Zero trains a single model in two roles: Generator and Reviser. The Generator produces an initial response; the Reviser conditions on the prompt, the generator’s response, and its binary reward to produce an improved response. The model is then updated via on-policy self-distillation, using the Reviser’s token-level distributions (conditioned on the generator’s output and reward) as supervision for the Generator. Periodic synchronization ensures the generator inherits the reviser’s improving revision capability, turning sparse binary rewards into dense token-level learning signals.

Result: On math and code reasoning benchmarks using Qwen3-4B-Instruct and Olmo-3-7B-Instruct, SD-Zero yields at least 10% performance gains over base models and surpasses strong baselines such as Rejection Fine-Tuning, GRPO, and Self-Distillation Fine-Tuning under identical question sets and training sample budgets. Ablations validate the importance of the proposed self-distillation design and reveal new behaviors such as token-level localization and iterative improvement.

Conclusion: SD-Zero offers a sample-efficient alternative to RL-based verifiable training that needs only binary rewards and no external teacher. By jointly training generator and reviser roles within one model and distilling revision behavior back into generation, the method effectively converts sparse rewards into dense supervision. This leads to better performance in math and code reasoning tasks and exhibits useful properties like token-level self-localization and iterative self-evolution, suggesting a promising direction for scalable post-training without expensive annotations or teacher models.

Abstract: Current post-training methods in verifiable settings fall into two categories. Reinforcement learning (RLVR) relies on binary rewards, which are broadly applicable and powerful, but provide only sparse supervision during training. Distillation provides dense token-level supervision, typically obtained from an external teacher or using high-quality demonstrations. Collecting such supervision can be costly or unavailable. We propose Self-Distillation Zero (SD-Zero), a method that is substantially more training sample-efficient than RL and does not require an external teacher or high-quality demonstrations. SD-Zero trains a single model to play two roles: a Generator, which produces an initial response, and a Reviser, which conditions on that response and its binary reward to produce an improved response. We then perform on-policy self-distillation to distill the reviser into the generator, using the reviser's token distributions conditioned on the generator's response and its reward as supervision. In effect, SD-Zero trains the model to transform binary rewards into dense token-level self-supervision. On math and code reasoning benchmarks with Qwen3-4B-Instruct and Olmo-3-7B-Instruct, SD-Zero improves performance by at least 10% over the base models and outperforms strong baselines, including Rejection Fine-Tuning (RFT), GRPO, and Self-Distillation Fine-Tuning (SDFT), under the same question set and training sample budget. Extensive ablation studies show two novel characteristics of our proposed algorithm: (a) token-level self-localization, where the reviser can identify the key tokens that need to be revised in the generator's response based on reward, and (b) iterative self-evolution, where the improving ability to revise answers can be distilled back into generation performance with regular teacher synchronization.

</details>


### [3] [LLMs Struggle with Abstract Meaning Comprehension More Than Expected](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12018)
*Hamoud Alhazmi,Jiachen Jiang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates how well models understand abstract words using the SemEval-2021 ReCAM task and proposes a bidirectional attention classifier that significantly boosts fine-tuned models’ performance on abstract meaning comprehension.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Abstract words are hard for both humans and machines because they lack direct, concrete referents and involve high-level semantics. Existing work shows that even strong LLMs still struggle with this type of meaning. The authors want to systematically test models’ ability to understand abstract concepts and to design a method that better captures abstract semantics, moving beyond surface-level pattern matching.

Method: They use SemEval-2021 Task 4 (ReCAM), where models must choose the correct abstract word to fill a blank in a passage from five options. They benchmark various large language models (e.g., GPT-4o) under zero-shot, one-shot, and few-shot prompting, and compare them with fine-tuned encoders like BERT and RoBERTa. Building on this, they propose a bidirectional attention classifier that models attention both from passage to options and from options to passage, mimicking human-like reading strategies. This classifier is trained as a downstream component on top of fine-tuned encoders.

Result: They find that most LLMs, including GPT-4o, underperform on the ReCAM abstract meaning tasks when only prompted (zero/one/few-shot), whereas fine-tuned encoder models like BERT and RoBERTa achieve higher accuracy. Adding the proposed bidirectional attention classifier to these fine-tuned encoders yields performance gains of 4.06 percentage points on Task 1 and 3.41 percentage points on Task 2 compared with strong baselines.

Conclusion: Prompted LLMs are not yet reliable for abstract concept comprehension in cloze-style tasks, while supervised fine-tuning of encoder models remains more effective. Incorporating a cognitively inspired bidirectional attention mechanism further improves abstract meaning understanding, indicating that structured attention over both context and candidate options is a promising direction for enhancing semantic comprehension in NLP models.

Abstract: Understanding abstract meanings is crucial for advanced language comprehension. Despite extensive research, abstract words remain challenging due to their non-concrete, high-level semantics. SemEval-2021 Task 4 (ReCAM) evaluates models' ability to interpret abstract concepts by presenting passages with questions and five abstract options in a cloze-style format. Key findings include: (1) Most large language models (LLMs), including GPT-4o, struggle with abstract meaning comprehension under zero-shot, one-shot, and few-shot settings, while fine-tuned models like BERT and RoBERTa perform better. (2) A proposed bidirectional attention classifier, inspired by human cognitive strategies, enhances fine-tuned models by dynamically attending to passages and options. This approach improves accuracy by 4.06 percent on Task 1 and 3.41 percent on Task 2, demonstrating its potential for abstract meaning comprehension.

</details>


### [4] [Benchmarking Deflection and Hallucination in Large Vision-Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12033)
*Nicholas Moratelli,Christopher Davis,Leonardo F. R. Ribeiro,Bill Byrne,Gonzalo Iglesias*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a dynamic, retrieval-dependent benchmark (VLM-DeflectionBench) to test how Large Vision-Language Models respond when multimodal evidence is conflicting or insufficient, focusing especially on their ability to appropriately deflect instead of hallucinating answers.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current knowledge-intensive multimodal QA benchmarks for LVLMs fail to account for two key issues: (1) they rarely contain cases where visual and textual evidence conflict, or where retrieved knowledge is incomplete, so they under-test a model’s ability to say "I don’t know"; and (2) they quickly become obsolete because ever-larger LVLM training corpora allow models to answer many benchmark questions directly from memorized parametric knowledge without retrieval. This limits our ability to assess robustness and reliability of retrieval-augmented LVLMs in realistic settings.

Method: The authors design a dynamic data curation pipeline that continuously filters and constructs benchmark items that remain genuinely retrieval-dependent, even as LVLMs improve. Using this pipeline, they build VLM-DeflectionBench, a 2,775-sample benchmark covering diverse multimodal retrieval scenarios where evidence can be conflicting, noisy, or incomplete. They also propose a fine-grained evaluation protocol with four scenarios to disentangle what a model has memorized (parametric knowledge) from how robustly it uses retrieval, and whether it can correctly deflect when it cannot reliably answer.

Result: When evaluating 20 state-of-the-art Large Vision-Language Models on VLM-DeflectionBench, they find that current systems frequently fail to produce appropriate deflections in the face of noisy, misleading, or insufficient evidence. Instead, they tend to provide confident but unreliable answers, revealing a substantial gap in current methods’ ability to handle uncertainty and conflict in multimodal retrieval settings.

Conclusion: VLM-DeflectionBench and the associated dynamic curation pipeline provide a reusable, extensible way to maintain challenging, retrieval-dependent evaluation for LVLMs over time. The experiments show that today’s LVLMs are poor at deflecting when they lack trustworthy evidence, underscoring that future work must focus not just on increasing what models know, but on improving their behavior and reliability when they do not know or when the available evidence is conflicting or incomplete.

Abstract: Large Vision-Language Models (LVLMs) increasingly rely on retrieval to answer knowledge-intensive multimodal questions. Existing benchmarks overlook conflicts between visual and textual evidence and the importance of generating deflections (e.g., Sorry, I cannot answer...) when retrieved knowledge is incomplete. These benchmarks also suffer from rapid obsolescence, as growing LVLM training sets allow models to answer many questions without retrieval. We address these gaps with three contributions. First, we propose a dynamic data curation pipeline that preserves benchmark difficulty over time by filtering for genuinely retrieval-dependent samples. Second, we introduce VLM-DeflectionBench, a benchmark of 2,775 samples spanning diverse multimodal retrieval settings, designed to probe model behaviour under conflicting or insufficient evidence. Third, we define a fine-grained evaluation protocol with four scenarios that disentangle parametric memorization from retrieval robustness. Experiments across 20 state-of-the-art LVLMs indicate that models usually fail to deflect in the presence of noisy or misleading evidence. Our results highlight the need to evaluate not only what models know, but how they behave when they do not, and serve as a reusable and extensible benchmark for reliable KB-VQA evaluation. All resources will be publicly available upon publication.

</details>


### [5] [Think Through Uncertainty: Improving Long-Form Generation Factuality via Reasoning Calibration](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12046)
*Xin Liu,Lu Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces CURE, a framework that teaches LLMs to assign calibrated confidence to each atomic claim in long-form generation, improving factual accuracy and enabling selective abstention from uncertain claims.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long-form generations by LLMs frequently contain hallucinations. Existing methods either revise outputs post-hoc or use RL with correctness-based rewards, but they do not help the model know which parts of its own output are reliable. Current confidence-calibration methods mostly yield a single scalar confidence for an entire response, which is inadequate because different claims within a long answer can have very different uncertainty levels. The authors aim to address the lack of claim-level uncertainty estimation to reduce confidently wrong statements and improve controllability in long-form generation.

Method: The authors propose CURE, centered around a Claim-Aware Reasoning Protocol that forces the model to decompose its output into atomic factual claims and attach an explicit confidence estimate to each one. They then design a multi-stage training pipeline: first aligning the model’s confidence estimates with claim correctness (calibration) and then optimizing the model for factuality under this protocol. The calibrated confidence scores are used at inference for selective prediction, where the model can abstain from, or hedge on, low-confidence claims, thereby reducing hallucinations in long-form outputs.

Result: Across four long-form factuality benchmarks, CURE yields consistent improvements in factual accuracy over strong supervised and RL baselines while preserving factual recall. On Biography generation, it increases claim-level accuracy by up to 39.9%. On the FactBench benchmark, it also substantially improves confidence calibration, as evidenced by a 16.0% increase in AUROC for distinguishing correct from incorrect claims.

Conclusion: Teaching LLMs to reason explicitly about uncertainty at the claim level, rather than only optimizing for correctness or assigning a single global confidence score, significantly improves both factual accuracy and calibration in long-form generation. CURE’s claim-aware protocol and training pipeline enable models not only to produce more reliable content but also to abstain strategically from uncertain claims, offering better control and robustness than existing post-hoc or scalar-confidence approaches.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) often hallucinate in long-form generation. Existing approaches mainly improve factuality through post-hoc revision or reinforcement learning (RL) with correctness-based rewards, but they do not teach the model to estimate which parts of its generation are reliable. As a result, models may still state incorrect claims confidently in their responses. Recent advances in reasoning have significantly improved LLM performance, and have been leveraged to estimate confidence by incorporating calibration into RL objectives. However, existing approaches remain limited to a single scalar confidence for the entire response, which is insufficient for long-form generation where uncertainty varies across individual claims. To mitigate this problem, we propose CURE, a framework that improves long-form factuality by teaching LLMs to reason about uncertainty at the claim level. We first introduce a Claim-Aware Reasoning Protocol, which structures outputs into atomic claims paired with explicit confidence estimates. We then develop a multi-stage training pipeline that aligns model confidence with claims' correctness and then optimizes on factuality. The resulting calibrated confidence further enables selective prediction, allowing the model to abstain from uncertain claims at inference time. Experiments on four long-form factuality benchmarks show that CURE consistently improves factual accuracy over competitive supervised and RL baselines, while maintaining factual recall. In particular, it improves claim-level accuracy by up to 39.9% on Biography generation. These gains are accompanied by improved calibration, as reflected by a 16.0% increase in AUROC on FactBench.

</details>


### [6] [Empirical Evaluation of PDF Parsing and Chunking for Financial Question Answering with RAG](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12047)
*Omar El Bachyr,Yewei Song,Saad Ezzini,Jacques Klein,Tegawendé F. Bissyandé,Anas Zilali,Ulrick Ble,Anne Goujon*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how different PDF parsing and chunking designs impact Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) performance on question answering over financial PDFs, and provides practical guidelines for robust PDF-understanding pipelines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: PDFs are designed for human consumption, not machines, and their heterogeneous content (text, tables, images) makes automatic parsing and information extraction difficult. While many practitioners and researchers are building RAG-based systems for PDF understanding, there is no systematic, comprehensive study of how specific components and design choices—such as parsers and chunking strategies—affect performance. This gap motivates a focused empirical investigation.

Method: The authors frame the problem as a Question Answering task over PDFs in the financial domain. They evaluate multiple PDF parsers and various chunking strategies, including different overlap configurations, and analyze their combinations to see how well they preserve document structure and support accurate QA. They conduct experiments on two benchmarks, one being TableQuest, a newly created, publicly available dataset designed for table-heavy financial documents.

Result: The experiments reveal how different parsers, chunking methods, and overlap settings interact, identifying configurations that better maintain structural information (especially tables) and yield more accurate QA in RAG setups. The results highlight which design choices are more effective for financial PDF understanding and where synergies between parsers and chunking strategies arise.

Conclusion: Careful selection and combination of PDF parsers and chunking strategies significantly impacts the performance of RAG-based QA over PDFs. The paper distills these empirical findings into practical guidelines for constructing robust RAG pipelines for PDF understanding, particularly in table-intensive financial documents, and contributes the TableQuest benchmark to support further research.

Abstract: PDF files are primarily intended for human reading rather than automated processing. In addition, the heterogeneous content of PDFs, such as text, tables, and images, poses significant challenges for parsing and information extraction. To address these difficulties, both practitioners and researchers are increasingly developing new methods, including the promising Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) systems to automated PDF processing. However, there is no comprehensive study investigating how different components and design choices affect the performance of a RAG system for understanding PDFs. In this paper, we propose such a study (1) by focusing on Question Answering, a specific language understanding task, and (2) by leveraging two benchmarks from the financial domain, including TableQuest, our newly generated, publicly available benchmark. We systematically examine multiple PDF parsers and chunking strategies (with varied overlap), along with their potential synergies in preserving document structure and ensuring answer correctness. Overall, our results offer practical guidelines for building robust RAG pipelines for PDF understanding.

</details>


### [7] [Leveraging Weighted Syntactic and Semantic Context Assessment Summary (wSSAS) Towards Text Categorization Using LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12049)
*Shreeya Verma Kathuria,Nitin Mayande,Sharookh Daruwalla,Nitin Joglekar,Charles Weber*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes wSSAS, a deterministic framework that improves the reliability and reproducibility of LLM-based text categorization and clustering on large, noisy datasets by using a structured hierarchy and a signal-to-noise-based feature prioritization.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are powerful for text analytics but their stochastic attention mechanisms and sensitivity to noise limit their use in enterprise-grade, high-precision, and reproducible settings. Organizations dealing with large, chaotic text datasets need methods that ensure consistent categorization, robust clustering, and data integrity. The paper aims to solve the issues of unstable outputs, background noise, and lack of deterministic control in LLM-based summarization and categorization workflows.

Method: The authors introduce wSSAS, a deterministic, two-phase validation and summarization framework. Phase one organizes raw text into a hierarchical structure with three levels: Themes, Stories, and Clusters. Phase two applies a Signal-to-Noise Ratio (SNR) scoring mechanism to prioritize high-value semantic features and down-weight noisy or less relevant information. This SNR-based feature selection is integrated into a Summary-of-Summaries (SoS) architecture, which aggregates information while preserving only the most representative semantics. The framework is implemented using Gemini 2.0 Flash Lite as the underlying LLM and is tested on multiple review datasets.

Result: Across Google Business, Amazon Product, and Goodreads Book review datasets, wSSAS yields improved clustering integrity and higher categorization accuracy compared to standard LLM-based approaches. Quantitatively, it reduces categorization entropy, indicating more consistent and less ambiguous category assignments. The approach leads to more stable, reproducible summaries and categorizations on large-scale text datasets.

Conclusion: wSSAS provides a deterministic, high-precision framework for large-scale text categorization and summarization using LLMs. By structuring data hierarchically and using SNR-based semantic feature prioritization within a Summary-of-Summaries architecture, it mitigates noise, stabilizes model behavior, and improves clustering and categorization performance. The method offers a reproducible pathway to enterprise-grade LLM analytics and can enhance the reliability of LLM-based workflows applied to noisy, real-world text data.

Abstract: The use of Large Language Models (LLMs) for reliable, enterprise-grade analytics such as text categorization is often hindered by the stochastic nature of attention mechanisms and sensitivity to noise that compromise their analytical precision and reproducibility. To address these technical frictions, this paper introduces the Weighted Syntactic and Semantic Context Assessment Summary (wSSAS), a deterministic framework designed to enforce data integrity on large-scale, chaotic datasets. We propose a two-phased validation framework that first organizes raw text into a hierarchical classification structure containing Themes, Stories, and Clusters. It then leverages a Signal-to-Noise Ratio (SNR) to prioritize high-value semantic features, ensuring the model's attention remains focused on the most representative data points. By incorporating this scoring mechanism into a Summary-of-Summaries (SoS) architecture, the framework effectively isolates essential information and mitigates background noise during data aggregation.
  Experimental results using Gemini 2.0 Flash Lite across diverse datasets - including Google Business reviews, Amazon Product reviews, and Goodreads Book reviews - demonstrate that wSSAS significantly improves clustering integrity and categorization accuracy. Our findings indicate that wSSAS reduces categorization entropy and provides a reproducible pathway for improving LLM based summaries based on a high-precision, deterministic process for large-scale text categorization.

</details>


### [8] [LoSA: Locality Aware Sparse Attention for Block-Wise Diffusion Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12056)
*Haocheng Xi,Harman Singh,Yuezhou Hu,Coleman Hooper,Rishabh Tiwari,Aditya Tomar,Minjae Lee,Wonjun Kang,Michael Mahoney,Chenfeng Xu,Kurt Keutzer,Amir Gholami*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose LOSA, a locality-aware sparse attention scheme for block-wise diffusion language models that reuses attention for stable tokens and sparsifies only active ones, fixing KV inflation, preserving accuracy, and improving speed and memory efficiency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Block-wise diffusion language models can generate multiple tokens in parallel but are still slowed by memory-bound attention for long contexts. Standard sparse attention methods fail because different queries require different key-value prefixes, causing KV Inflation, where many KV pages must be loaded, hurting efficiency and accuracy. There is a need for an attention mechanism that reduces memory access while maintaining or improving model accuracy for long-context DLMs.

Method: They empirically observe that, between consecutive denoising steps in block-wise DLMs, only a small subset of tokens (active tokens) change their hidden states substantially, while most tokens (stable tokens) remain nearly unchanged. Leveraging this, they propose LOSA (Locality-aware Sparse Attention): for stable tokens, LOSA reuses previously computed prefix-attention results, avoiding repeated KV reads; for active tokens, it applies a carefully designed sparse attention pattern instead of dense attention. This combination reduces the total number of key-value indices that need to be accessed while maintaining relevant context, directly mitigating the KV Inflation problem.

Result: On multiple block-wise diffusion language models and benchmarks, LOSA substantially reduces attention density (1.54x lower) while preserving near-dense accuracy. Under aggressive sparsity settings, LOSA improves average accuracy by up to 9 points compared with naive sparse approaches. On RTX A6000 GPUs, it achieves attention speedups of up to 4.14x, demonstrating improved computational and memory efficiency in practice.

Conclusion: Locality-aware Sparse Attention (LOSA) effectively addresses the KV Inflation problem in block-wise diffusion language models by reusing attention for stable tokens and sparsifying only active ones. This design yields significant gains in attention speed and memory efficiency while maintaining, and sometimes improving, model accuracy, positioning LOSA as a practical solution for efficient long-context inference in DLMs.

Abstract: Block-wise diffusion language models (DLMs) generate multiple tokens in any order, offering a promising alternative to the autoregressive decoding pipeline. However, they still remain bottlenecked by memory-bound attention in long-context scenarios. Naive sparse attention fails on DLMs due to a KV Inflation problem, where different queries select different prefix positions, making the union of accessed KV pages large. To address this, we observe that between consecutive denoising steps, only a small fraction of active tokens exhibit significant hidden-state changes, while the majority of stable tokens remain nearly constant. Based on this insight, we propose LOSA (Locality-aware Sparse Attention), which reuses cached prefix-attention results for stable tokens and applies sparse attention only to active tokens. This substantially shrinks the number of KV indices that must be loaded, yielding both higher speedup and higher accuracy. Across multiple block-wise DLMs and benchmarks, LOSA preserves near-dense accuracy while significantly improving efficiency, achieving up to +9 points in average accuracy at aggressive sparsity levels while maintaining 1.54x lower attention density. It also achieves up to 4.14x attention speedup on RTX A6000 GPUs, demonstrating the effectiveness of the proposed method.

</details>


### [9] [Robust Explanations for User Trust in Enterprise NLP Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12069)
*Guilin Zhang,Kai Zhao,Jeffrey Friedman,Xu Chu,Amine Anoun,Jerry Ting*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a unified black-box framework to evaluate how robust token-level explanations are for NLP models, and shows that large decoder LLMs yield significantly more stable explanations than encoder models under realistic input noise.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Enterprises need robust, trustworthy explanations from NLP models, but many production setups only allow black-box API access, making standard representation-based explainers unusable. Moreover, organizations are moving from encoder classifiers to decoder LLMs without clear evidence on whether explanation quality and robustness hold up when real users introduce noisy inputs. The authors aim to fill this gap by offering a practical, model-agnostic way to quantify explanation robustness before deployment.

Method: They design a black-box robustness evaluation framework using leave-one-out token occlusion to generate token-level importance scores. Robustness is measured via the flip rate of top-ranked explanation tokens when inputs are perturbed. They apply four realistic perturbations—word swap, deletion, shuffling, and back-translation—each at multiple severity levels. This protocol is run across three benchmark datasets and six models covering both encoder architectures (BERT, RoBERTa) and decoder LLMs (Qwen 7B/14B, Llama 8B/70B), resulting in 64,800 evaluated cases.

Result: Decoder LLMs exhibit far more stable token-level explanations than encoder baselines, with an average 73% reduction in top-token flip rate under perturbations. Within decoder models, explanation stability improves with scale, with a 44% robustness gain from 7B-parameter to 70B-parameter models. The extensive experiments thus quantify clear architectural and size-related differences in explanation robustness.

Conclusion: The proposed framework enables unified, black-box evaluation of explanation robustness for NLP models and reveals that larger decoder LLMs provide more stable explanations under realistic user noise compared to traditional encoder models. By linking robustness gains to inference cost, the authors produce a cost–robustness tradeoff curve that practitioners can use to select models and explanation strategies prior to deployment in compliance-sensitive settings.

Abstract: Robust explanations are increasingly required for user trust in enterprise NLP, yet pre-deployment validation is difficult in the common case of black-box deployment (API-only access) where representation-based explainers are infeasible and existing studies provide limited guidance on whether explanations remain stable under real user noise, especially when organizations migrate from encoder classifiers to decoder LLMs. To close this gap, we propose a unified black-box robustness evaluation framework for token-level explanations based on leave-one-out occlusion, and operationalize explanation robustness with top-token flip rate under realistic perturbations (swap, deletion, shuffling, and back-translation) at multiple severity levels. Using this protocol, we conduct a systematic cross-architecture comparison across three benchmark datasets and six models spanning encoder and decoder families (BERT, RoBERTa, Qwen 7B/14B, Llama 8B/70B; 64,800 cases). We find that decoder LLMs produce substantially more stable explanations than encoder baselines (73% lower flip rates on average), and that stability improves with model scale (44% gain from 7B to 70B). Finally, we relate robustness improvements to inference cost, yielding a practical cost-robustness tradeoff curve that supports model and explanation selection prior to deployment in compliance-sensitive applications.

</details>


### [10] [Narrative over Numbers: The Identifiable Victim Effect and its Amplification Under Alignment and Reasoning in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12076)
*Syed Rifat Raiyan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates whether large language models (LLMs) display the Identifiable Victim Effect (IVE) and related biases when making morally relevant allocation decisions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs are increasingly used in high-stakes settings like humanitarian triage, grant allocation, and content moderation, it is crucial to know if they inherit or amplify human-like affective biases such as the IVE, where people favor a single vivid victim over larger statistical groups in equal need. Understanding this informs both AI safety/alignment and the design of fair, reliable decision-support systems.

Method: The authors run 51,955 validated API calls across 16 frontier LLMs from nine organizations. They adapt and extend ten classic experimental paradigms from IVE research (e.g., Small et al. 2007; Kogut & Ritov 2005). Models are prompted with scenarios involving identifiable single victims vs. statistical groups and evaluated on their resource-allocation preferences. They compare instruction-tuned versus reasoning-specialized models, test different prompting styles (standard answers vs. Chain-of-Thought, including specifically utilitarian CoT), and measure effect sizes (Cohen’s d) to quantify the IVE and related phenomena like psychophysical numbing and quantity neglect.

Result: Most LLMs display a measurable IVE, but its magnitude and even direction depend strongly on training and prompting. Instruction-tuned models show strong IVE (up to d=1.56), while reasoning-focused models sometimes reverse it (down to d=-0.85, favoring groups). The overall pooled effect (d=0.223, p=2e-6) is about twice the human single-victim meta-analytic benchmark (d≈0.10) and likely larger than the overall human effect, since human group-victim IVE is near zero. Standard Chain-of-Thought prompting, instead of mitigating bias, roughly triples the IVE (d from 0.15 to 0.41). Only explicitly utilitarian CoT prompts reliably remove the IVE. The models additionally show psychophysical numbing, perfect quantity neglect, and small but systematic in-/out-group cultural biases.

Conclusion: LLMs not only reproduce but can amplify the Identifiable Victim Effect and related affective biases, especially when instruction-tuned or prompted with generic CoT. Reasoning-specialized models and carefully designed utilitarian reasoning prompts can attenuate or reverse these biases. These findings highlight that alignment and prompting choices crucially shape the moral-psychological profiles of LLMs, and that naive deployment in humanitarian or ethical decision-making contexts risks systematically biased resource allocations. Designers and regulators should treat moral biases as properties to be measured and managed in LLMs, not assume that more deliberation (standard CoT) automatically yields more rational or impartial outcomes.

Abstract: The Identifiable Victim Effect (IVE) $-$ the tendency to allocate greater resources to a specific, narratively described victim than to a statistically characterized group facing equivalent hardship $-$ is one of the most robust findings in moral psychology and behavioural economics. As large language models (LLMs) assume consequential roles in humanitarian triage, automated grant evaluation, and content moderation, a critical question arises: do these systems inherit the affective irrationalities present in human moral reasoning? We present the first systematic, large-scale empirical investigation of the IVE in LLMs, comprising N=51,955 validated API trials across 16 frontier models spanning nine organizational lineages (Google, Anthropic, OpenAI, Meta, DeepSeek, xAI, Alibaba, IBM, and Moonshot). Using a suite of ten experiments $-$ porting and extending canonical paradigms from Small et al. (2007) and Kogut and Ritov (2005) $-$ we find that the IVE is prevalent but strongly modulated by alignment training. Instruction-tuned models exhibit extreme IVE (Cohen's d up to 1.56), while reasoning-specialized models invert the effect (down to d=-0.85). The pooled effect (d=0.223, p=2e-6) is approximately twice the single-victim human meta-analytic baseline (d$\approx$0.10) reported by Lee and Feeley (2016) $-$ and likely exceeds the overall human pooled effect by a larger margin, given that the group-victim human effect is near zero. Standard Chain-of-Thought (CoT) prompting $-$ contrary to its role as a deliberative corrective $-$ nearly triples the IVE effect size (from d=0.15 to d=0.41), while only utilitarian CoT reliably eliminates it. We further document psychophysical numbing, perfect quantity neglect, and marginal in-group/out-group cultural bias, with implications for AI deployment in humanitarian and ethical decision-making contexts.

</details>


### [11] [Temporal Flattening in LLM-Generated Text: Comparing Human and LLM Writing Trajectories](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12097)
*Zhanwei Cao,YeoJin Go,Yifan Hu,Shanu Sushmita*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies whether large language models can reproduce the long-term temporal evolution seen in human authors' writing and finds that LLMs show "temporal flattening," with less change over time than humans.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Human writing evolves over months and years in style and cognitive-emotional state, but current LLM usage is stateless and interaction-level. The authors want to know if LLMs can match human-like longitudinal evolution in text, which matters for authenticity in applications like synthetic data generation and modeling long-term behavior.

Method: They build a longitudinal dataset of 412 human authors and 6,086 documents from 2012–2024 across academic abstracts, blogs, and news. They generate corresponding trajectories from three representative LLMs, both with and without conditioning on prior history. They then compute drift and variance-based metrics over semantic, lexical, and cognitive-emotional representations to compare temporal evolution between humans and LLMs.

Result: LLMs show "temporal flattening": they have higher lexical diversity but much lower semantic and cognitive-emotional drift over time compared to humans. Temporal variability patterns alone can distinguish human from LLM trajectories with 94% accuracy and 98% ROC-AUC.

Conclusion: Current LLMs, under typical deployment paradigms, fail to reproduce authentic long-term temporal structure in text, even when given incremental history. This temporal flattening is a fundamental property of how they operate and has important implications for scenarios that require realistic longitudinal dynamics, such as generating synthetic training corpora or modeling long-term user behavior.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly used in daily applications, from content generation to code writing, where each interaction treats the model as stateless, generating responses independently without memory. Yet human writing is inherently longitudinal: authors' styles and cognitive states evolve across months and years. This raises a central question: can LLMs reproduce such temporal structure across extended time periods? We construct and publicly release a longitudinal dataset of 412 human authors and 6,086 documents spanning 2012--2024 across three domains (academic abstracts, blogs, news) and compare them to trajectories generated by three representative LLMs under standard and history-conditioned generation settings. Using drift and variance-based metrics over semantic, lexical, and cognitive-emotional representations, we find temporal flattening in LLM-generated text. LLMs produce greater lexical diversity but exhibit substantially reduced semantic and cognitive-emotional drift relative to humans. These differences are highly predictive: temporal variability patterns alone achieve 94% accuracy and 98% ROC-AUC in distinguishing human from LLM trajectories. Our results demonstrate that temporal flattening persists regardless of whether LLMs generate independently or with access to incremental history, revealing a fundamental property of current deployment paradigms. This gap has direct implications for applications requiring authentic temporal structure, such as synthetic training data and longitudinal text modeling.

### [12] [When Self-Reference Fails to Close: Matrix-Level Dynamics in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12128)
*Ji Ho Bae*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper analyzes how self-referential prompts affect internal matrix dynamics and stability of large language models, finding that only paradoxical, non-closing truth recursion self-reference causes widespread dynamical disruption and contradictory outputs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand when and how self-referential inputs can destabilize large language models, in order to clarify their internal failure modes and distinguish harmless meta-cognition from structurally problematic self-reference.

Method: They probe four transformer models from different architecture families with 300 prompts arranged in a 14-level hierarchy, varying temperature. They compute 106 scalar metrics over up to 7 analysis passes, including attention effective rank and variance statistics, and perform per-layer SVD analyses. They statistically compare grounded vs paradoxical self-reference, use effect sizes and FDR correction, evaluate metric consistency across models, and train a classifier to detect destabilizing prompts from metric patterns. They also construct minimal prompt pairs to confirm causal effects of non-closing truth recursion structure.

Result: Grounded and meta-cognitive self-referential prompts are about as stable as factual controls on key collapse-related metrics. Instability is concentrated in prompts that induce non-closing truth recursion (NCTR), which yield large effect sizes on attention effective rank and variance kurtosis, with disruptions observed at all sampled layers via SVD. Most metric–model combinations significantly distinguish NCTR from stable self-reference after FDR correction, with many large-effect differences, and nearly half the metrics replicate across models. A classifier can reliably separate NCTR from stable self-reference. NCTR prompts also cause substantially higher rates of contradictory output compared with controls.

Conclusion: Self-reference per se does not inherently destabilize large language models; the problematic regime arises when prompts encode non-closing truth recursion, which pushes transformer dynamics into globally reorganized, anomalous matrix regimes linked to classical matrix-semigroup problems. These regimes are associated with elevated contradictions in output, highlighting a specific, structurally-defined self-referential failure mode with both theoretical and practical significance.

Abstract: We investigate how self-referential inputs alter the internal matrix dynamics of large language models. Measuring 106 scalar metrics across up to 7 analysis passes on four models from three architecture families -- Qwen3-VL-8B, Llama-3.2-11B, Llama-3.3-70B, and Gemma-2-9B -- over 300 prompts in a 14-level hierarchy at three temperatures ($T \in \{0.0, 0.3, 0.7\}$), we find that self-reference alone is not destabilizing: grounded self-referential statements and meta-cognitive prompts are markedly more stable than paradoxical self-reference on key collapse-related metrics, and on several such metrics can be as stable as factual controls. Instability concentrates in prompts inducing non-closing truth recursion (NCTR) -- truth-value computations with no finite-depth resolution. NCTR prompts produce anomalously elevated attention effective rank -- indicating attention reorganization with global dispersion rather than simple concentration collapse -- and key metrics reach Cohen's $d = 3.14$ (attention effective rank) to $3.52$ (variance kurtosis) vs. stable self-reference in the 70B model; 281/397 metric-model combinations differentiate NCTR from stable self-reference after FDR correction ($q < 0.05$), 198 with $|d| > 0.8$. Per-layer SVD confirms disruption at every sampled layer ($d > +1.0$ in all three models analyzed), ruling out aggregation artifacts. A classifier achieves AUC $0.81$-$0.90$; 30 minimal pairs yield 42/387 significant combinations; 43/106 metrics replicate across all four models. We connect these observations to three classical matrix-semigroup problems and propose, as a conjecture, that NCTR forces finite-depth transformers toward dynamical regimes where these problems concentrate. NCTR prompts also produce elevated contradictory output ($+34$-$56$ percentage points vs. controls), suggesting practical relevance for understanding self-referential failure modes.

AlphaEval provides such a benchmark across diverse occupational domains and demonstrates that mixed evaluation paradigms are necessary to capture performance in realistic workloads. The accompanying requirement-to-benchmark framework offers a general, reproducible method that organizations can adopt to build their own domain-specific, production-grounded benchmarks, helping align AI evaluation with real-world use and business needs.

Abstract: The rapid deployment of AI agents in commercial settings has outpaced the development of evaluation methodologies that reflect production realities. Existing benchmarks measure agent capabilities through retrospectively curated tasks with well-specified requirements and deterministic metrics -- conditions that diverge fundamentally from production environments where requirements contain implicit constraints, inputs are heterogeneous multi-modal documents with information fragmented across sources, tasks demand undeclared domain expertise, outputs are long-horizon professional deliverables, and success is judged by domain experts whose standards evolve over time. We present AlphaEval, a production-grounded benchmark of 94 tasks sourced from seven companies deploying AI agents in their core business, spanning six O*NET (Occupational Information Network) domains. Unlike model-centric benchmarks, AlphaEval evaluates complete agent products -- Claude Code, Codex, etc. -- as commercial systems, capturing performance variations invisible to model-level evaluation. Our evaluation framework covers multiple paradigms (LLM-as-a-Judge, reference-driven metrics, formal verification, rubric-based assessment, automated UI testing, etc.), with individual domains composing multiple paradigms. Beyond the benchmark itself, we contribute a requirement-to-benchmark construction framework -- a systematic methodology that transforms authentic production requirements into executable evaluation tasks in minimal time. This framework standardizes the entire pipeline from requirement to evaluation, providing a reproducible, modular process that any organization can adopt to construct production-grounded benchmarks for their own domains.

The resulting TopicGuidedChat dataset is effective for improving and evaluating LLMs’ memory-grounded QA capabilities, reducing reliance on costly human annotation while better capturing both short- and long-term conversational dependencies.

TL;DR: They extend Retrieval-Augmented Generation by explicitly modeling the order of evidence using an order-aware knowledge hypergraph, improving performance on tasks where reasoning depends on sequence, not just content.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RAG methods, including graph and hypergraph variants, typically treat retrieved documents or facts as an unordered set, assuming permutation invariance. This clashes with many real-world reasoning problems where the outcome depends on the order of events or interactions (e.g., processes, operations, or temporal chains). The paper aims to address this mismatch by creating a retrieval framework that is sensitive to the order of interactions so that language models can better handle order-dependent reasoning tasks.

Method: They introduce OKH-RAG (Order-Aware Knowledge Hypergraph RAG), which represents knowledge as higher-order interactions in a hypergraph enriched with precedence (ordering) information. Retrieval is reformulated as sequence inference over hyperedges, recovering trajectories of interactions instead of isolated facts. A learned transition model predicts precedence relations directly from data without explicit temporal labels. This model is integrated into a RAG pipeline, so that the LLM conditions on ordered sequences of evidence rather than unordered sets.

Result: On order-sensitive question answering and explanation benchmarks, including domains like tropical cyclone evolution and port operations, OKH-RAG surpasses permutation-invariant RAG baselines. Ablation experiments demonstrate that the gains are specifically attributable to modeling interaction order rather than other architectural differences, confirming the importance of sequence structure in retrieval.

Conclusion: RAG systems that ignore order and treat evidence as sets are fundamentally limited for tasks requiring process- or sequence-based reasoning. By making order a core structural aspect via an order-aware knowledge hypergraph and sequence-based retrieval, OKH-RAG yields more coherent reasoning trajectories and better task performance. Effective reasoning with RAG requires not just finding relevant facts but organizing them into well-structured, ordered evidence sequences.

Abstract: Retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) enhances large language models by grounding outputs in retrieved knowledge. However, existing RAG methods including graph- and hypergraph-based approaches treat retrieved evidence as an unordered set, implicitly assuming permutation invariance. This assumption is misaligned with many real-world reasoning tasks, where outcomes depend not only on which interactions occur, but also on the order in which they unfold. We propose Order-Aware Knowledge Hypergraph RAG (OKH-RAG), which treats order as a first-class structural property. OKH-RAG represents knowledge as higher-order interactions within a hypergraph augmented with precedence structure, and reformulates retrieval as sequence inference over hyperedges. Instead of selecting independent facts, it recovers coherent interaction trajectories that reflect underlying reasoning processes. A learned transition model infers precedence directly from data without requiring explicit temporal supervision. We evaluate OKH-RAG on order-sensitive question answering and explanation tasks, including tropical cyclone and port operation scenarios. OKH-RAG consistently outperforms permutation-invariant baselines, and ablations show that these gains arise specifically from modeling interaction order. These results highlight a key limitation of set-based retrieval: effective reasoning requires not only retrieving relevant evidence, but organizing it into structured sequences.

Furthermore, when the model’s architecture and data allow it to represent distinct knowledge states of different agents, it can produce expert-like behavior even though it rarely observes direct expert-only trajectories.

Conclusion: Pedagogical, teacher–student interaction data provides a more informative learning signal than pure expert demonstrations for transformer models in a navigation task. The key advantage arises from modeling epistemically distinct agents and their interaction: this structure enables models to infer expert policies and generalize robustly from relatively sparse direct expert behavior.

We generated synthetic datasets of simple interactions between an expert and a novice in a spatial navigation task, and then trained transformer models on those datasets, evaluating performance after exposure to different datasets. ### [17] [Beyond Majority Voting: Efficient Best-Of-N with Radial Consensus Score](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12196)
*Manh Nguyen,Sunil Gupta,Hung Le*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose Radial Consensus Score (RCS), a geometric, embedding-based method to pick the best answer among multiple LLM outputs, outperforming voting and probability methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs often produce multiple candidate answers and we want to select the most reliable one. Existing self-consistency (voting) or probability-based approaches struggle when the correct answer is in the minority, fail to exploit relationships between answers, and ignore the geometry of embedding space. A more principled, robust way to aggregate and select answers is needed, especially in black-box settings.

Method: Represent each candidate answer in an embedding space and compute a semantic center as a weighted Fréchet mean of these embeddings. Define the Radial Consensus Score as the radial distance of each answer to this center: closer candidates are considered more semantically consistent with the group. Support different weighting schemes (uniform, frequency-based, probability-based) to incorporate agreement and model confidence. Use this score for best-of-N selection and as a drop-in replacement for majority voting in multi-agent debate, without extra training and with compatibility for black-box models.

Result: Across seven benchmarks (short QA and long-form reasoning) and five open-weight models, RCS variants consistently beat strong baselines (self-consistency, probability heuristics), with larger gains at higher sampling budgets. RCS also works well as a substitute for majority voting in multi-agent debate and shows robustness when only black-box access to models is available.

Conclusion: Geometric consensus via Radial Consensus Score is an effective, training-free, scalable strategy for aggregating and selecting LLM answers. By leveraging the embedding space structure and flexible weighting schemes, RCS provides a more expressive and robust alternative to majority voting and standard probability-based methods for reliable best-of-N selection in LLM inference.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) frequently generate multiple candidate responses for a given prompt, yet selecting the most reliable one remains challenging, especially when correctness diverges from surface-level majority agreement. Existing approaches, such as self-consistency, rely on discrete voting, while probability-based methods often fail to capture relationships among candidate answers or tend to underweight high-quality but less frequent responses, and do not fully leverage the geometric structure of answer representations. To address these limitations, we introduce Radial Consensus Score (RCS), a simple, efficient, and training-free method for best-of-N selection. RCS models semantic consensus by computing a weighted Fréchet mean (semantic center) of answer embeddings and ranking candidates by their radial distance to this center. Importantly, RCS provides a general framework that supports multiple weighting schemes, including uniform, frequency-based, and probability-based variants, enabling flexible integration of agreement signals and model confidence while remaining fully applicable in black-box settings. Extensive experiments across seven benchmarks covering short-form QA and long-form reasoning tasks, and five open-weight models, demonstrate that RCS variants consistently outperform strong baselines, with gains becoming more pronounced as the sampling budget increases. RCS also serves as an effective drop-in replacement for majority voting in multi-agent debate and exhibits strong robustness in black-box scenarios. Overall, these results highlight geometric consensus as a scalable and broadly applicable principle for reliable answer selection, extending beyond majority voting to more expressive and robust aggregation in LLM inference.

### [18] [LLM-Guided Semantic Bootstrapping for Interpretable Text Classification with Tsetlin Machines](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12223)
*Jiechao Gao,Rohan Kumar Yadav,Yuangang Li,Yuandong Pan,Jie Wang,Ying Liu,Michael Lepech*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Pretrained language models like BERT give strong semantic features but are expensive and opaque, while symbolic models like Tsetlin Machines are transparent but weak in semantic generalization. The authors want the best of both: symbolic models that inherit rich semantics from PLMs without needing embeddings or online LLM calls.

Method: They introduce a semantic bootstrapping framework. For each class label, an LLM generates fine-grained sub-intents. These sub-intents drive synthetic text generation in a three-stage curriculum (seed, core, enriched) to increase semantic diversity. A Non-Negated Tsetlin Machine is trained on the synthetic data to identify high-confidence literals, which serve as human-interpretable semantic cues. These cues are then injected into training on real data so that a standard TM learns clause logic aligned with LLM-derived semantics, all without embeddings or inference-time LLM access.

Result: On multiple text classification benchmarks, the approach yields better accuracy and interpretability than a standard TM baseline. Its performance approaches that of BERT while preserving the symbolic, efficient nature of TMs, and the model remains fully interpretable through the extracted literals and clauses.

Conclusion: Semantic knowledge from large language models can be distilled offline into symbolic Tsetlin Machines via curriculum-based synthetic data and cue extraction. This produces efficient, fully symbolic text classifiers with substantially improved accuracy and interpretability, narrowing the gap to PLMs without sacrificing transparency or requiring embeddings/LLM calls at runtime.

Abstract: Pretrained language models (PLMs) like BERT provide strong semantic representations but are costly and opaque, while symbolic models such as the Tsetlin Machine (TM) offer transparency but lack semantic generalization. We propose a semantic bootstrapping framework that transfers LLM knowledge into symbolic form, combining interpretability with semantic capacity. Given a class label, an LLM generates sub-intents that guide synthetic data creation through a three-stage curriculum (seed, core, enriched), expanding semantic diversity. A Non-Negated TM (NTM) learns from these examples to extract high-confidence literals as interpretable semantic cues. Injecting these cues into real data enables a TM to align clause logic with LLM-inferred semantics. Our method requires no embeddings or runtime LLM calls, yet equips symbolic models with pretrained semantic priors. Across multiple text classification tasks, it improves interpretability and accuracy over vanilla TM, achieving performance comparable to BERT while remaining fully symbolic and efficient.

### [19] [Thought-Retriever: Don't Just Retrieve Raw Data, Retrieve Thoughts for Memory-Augmented Agentic Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12231)
*Tao Feng,Pengrui Han,Guanyu Lin,Ge Liu,Jiaxuan You*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Thought-Retriever is a model-agnostic method that gives LLMs a scalable, self-evolving long-term memory by storing and retrieving their own past intermediate thoughts instead of raw documents, enabling effective use of arbitrarily long external knowledge.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLMs struggle to fully use massive external knowledge because retrieval-augmented methods are bottlenecked by fixed context windows and top-K chunk retrieval over huge corpora. The authors aim to overcome this context-length and retrieval bottleneck so LLMs can reliably operate over ultra-long, real-world knowledge bases, such as large collections of academic papers, and improve with continued use.

Method: They introduce Thought-Retriever, a model-agnostic algorithm that records an LLM’s intermediate reasoning traces ("thoughts") generated while solving previous queries. These thoughts are filtered to remove noise and redundancy, organized into a structured thought memory, and then retrieved based on relevance when handling new queries. Instead of repeatedly retrieving raw text chunks, the system retrieves distilled, reusable thoughts, enabling conditioning on arbitrarily large external data without being limited by context length or a fixed number of retrieved chunks.

Result: On a new benchmark, AcademicEval, built from real academic papers and designed to stress ultra-long context usage, as well as on two public datasets, Thought-Retriever substantially outperforms state-of-the-art baselines. It yields on average at least +7.6 F1 and +16 percentage points in win rate across tasks. Empirical analysis further shows that performance improves as the system accumulates more solved queries, and that it tends to use deeper, more abstract thoughts when answering correspondingly abstract questions.

Conclusion: Thought-Retriever successfully equips LLMs with a scalable, self-evolving long-term memory derived from their own intermediate reasoning. This removes the hard constraint of context length for using large external knowledge bases, significantly improves task performance on ultra-long-context benchmarks, and enables LLMs to increasingly benefit from past experience by retrieving and reusing deeper prior thoughts for more abstract future queries.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have transformed AI research thanks to their powerful internal capabilities and knowledge. However, existing LLMs still fail to effectively incorporate the massive external knowledge when interacting with the world. Although retrieval-augmented LLMs are proposed to mitigate the issue, they are still fundamentally constrained by the context length of LLMs, as they can only retrieve top-K raw data chunks from the external knowledge base which often consists of millions of data chunks. Here we propose Thought-Retriever, a novel model-agnostic algorithm that helps LLMs generate output conditioned on arbitrarily long external data, without being constrained by the context length or number of retrieved data chunks. Our key insight is to let an LLM fully leverage its intermediate responses generated when solving past user queries (thoughts), filtering meaningless and redundant thoughts, organizing them in thought memory, and retrieving the relevant thoughts when addressing new queries. This effectively equips LLM-based agents with a self-evolving long-term memory that grows more capable through continuous interaction. Besides algorithmic innovation, we further meticulously prepare a novel benchmark, AcademicEval, which requires an LLM to faithfully leverage ultra-long context to answer queries based on real-world academic papers. Extensive experiments on AcademicEval and two other public datasets validate that Thought-Retriever remarkably outperforms state-of-the-art baselines, achieving an average increase of at least 7.6% in F1 score and 16% in win rate across various tasks. More importantly, we further demonstrate two exciting findings: (1) Thought-Retriever can indeed help LLM self-evolve after solving more user queries; (2) Thought-Retriever learns to leverage deeper thoughts to answer more abstract user queries.

</details>


### [20] [Continuous Knowledge Metabolism: Generating Scientific Hypotheses from Evolving Literature](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12243)
*Jinkai Tao,Yubo Wang,Xiaoyu Liu,Menglin Yang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes Continuous Knowledge Metabolism (CKM), a framework that incrementally processes scientific literature over time to support better hypothesis generation, showing that how literature is processed—incrementally and with change-awareness—significantly affects predictive performance, novelty, and efficiency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional literature-based hypothesis generation tends to treat scientific knowledge as static and often uses batch processing of papers, ignoring how knowledge evolves over time. This misses dynamic patterns such as novelty, confirmation, and contradiction among findings, and it can be inefficient and less predictive. The authors are motivated to build a system that more realistically mirrors how science progresses, capturing temporal trajectories in the literature to improve the generation and evaluation of scientific hypotheses.

Method: The authors introduce a framework called Continuous Knowledge Metabolism (CKM), which processes scientific literature using sliding time windows and incrementally updates a structured knowledge base. They implement two main variants: (1) CKM-Lite, which incrementally accumulates evidence without detailed change instrumentation, and (2) CKM-Full, which additionally instruments each new finding as novel, confirming, or contradicting existing knowledge, detects knowledge-change signals, and conditions hypothesis generation on the full evolution trajectory. They conduct experiments across 50 research topics, generating 892 hypotheses and comparing incremental variants to a batch-processing baseline using metrics like hit rate, hypothesis yield, best-match alignment, predictive coverage, token cost, and LLM-judged novelty.

Result: CKM-Lite, the efficient incremental variant, outperforms batch processing on key predictive metrics: higher hit rate (+2.8%, p=0.006), increased hypothesis yield (+3.6, p<0.001), better best-match alignment (+0.43, p<0.001), and drastically lower token cost (92% reduction). CKM-Full, which is change-aware, produces hypotheses judged by LLMs to be much more novel (Cohen's d=3.46) but with somewhat reduced predictive coverage, highlighting a quality-coverage trade-off. Analyses also show that research fields with more stable trajectories (less volatility in findings) are associated with higher hypothesis success (r=-0.28, p=0.051), and that knowledge convergence signals correlate with almost 5x higher hit rate compared to contradiction signals, suggesting that some types of knowledge evolution are more predictable than others.

Conclusion: The study concludes that both the temporal processing strategy (incremental vs. batch) and explicit modeling of knowledge change (novelty, confirmation, contradiction, convergence) materially affect the nature and success of generated scientific hypotheses. Incremental processing yields better predictive and efficiency outcomes, while change-aware instrumentation boosts novelty at the expense of coverage, revealing a fundamental quality-coverage trade-off. The results imply that literature-based discovery systems should model the evolution of knowledge rather than treat the literature as static, and that evaluation frameworks must consider multiple dimensions (predictive coverage, novelty, efficiency) rather than optimizing a single metric.

Abstract: Scientific hypothesis generation requires tracking how knowledge evolves, not just what is currently known. We introduce Continuous Knowledge Metabolism (CKM), a framework that processes scientific literature through sliding time windows and incrementally updates a structured knowledge base as new findings arrive. We present CKM-Lite, an efficient variant that achieves strong predictive coverage through incremental accumulation, outperforming batch processing on hit rate (+2.8%, p=0.006), hypothesis yield (+3.6, p<0.001), and best-match alignment (+0.43, p<0.001) while reducing token cost by 92%. To understand what drives these differences, we develop CKM-Full, an instrumented variant that categorizes each new finding as novel, confirming, or contradicting, detects knowledge change signals, and conditions hypothesis generation on the full evolution trajectory. Analyzing 892 hypotheses generated by CKM-Full across 50 research topics, alongside parallel runs of the other variants, we report four empirical observations: (1) incremental processing outperforms batch baseline across predictive and efficiency metrics; (2) change-aware instrumentation is associated with higher LLM-judged novelty (Cohen's d=3.46) but lower predictive coverage, revealing a quality-coverage trade-off; (3) a field's trajectory stability is associated with hypothesis success (r=-0.28, p=0.051), suggesting boundary conditions for literature-based prediction; (4) knowledge convergence signals are associated with nearly 5x higher hit rate than contradiction signals, pointing to differential predictability across change types. These findings suggest that the character of generated hypotheses is shaped not only by how much literature is processed, but also by how it is processed. They further indicate that evaluation frameworks must account for the quality-coverage trade-off rather than optimize for a single metric.

### [21] [SpecBound: Adaptive Bounded Self-Speculation with Layer-wise Confidence Calibration](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12247)
*Zhuofan Wen,Yang Feng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Proposes a new self-draft speculative decoding framework that speeds up large language model inference by over 2x while preserving exact outputs, via layer-wise temperature annealing, adaptive speculation length, and a unified deep-layer reprocessing pass.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Speculative decoding can speed up autoregressive large language model inference, but self-draft variants suffer from two key issues: (1) shallow layers in the base model often make overconfident but wrong token predictions, harming draft acceptance; and (2) difficult tokens in a draft sequence force computation through deeper layers for all tokens, reducing speed gains. There is a need for a self-draft approach that mitigates these problems without requiring auxiliary draft models or changes to model parameters.

Method: The paper introduces a self-draft speculative decoding framework with three main components: (1) layer-wise temperature annealing during early-exit decisions to reduce spurious overconfidence of shallow layers; (2) adaptive, token-wise control of maximum speculation length based on estimated decoding difficulty so that hard tokens limit how far speculation proceeds; and (3) a unified parallel processing step where hidden states of all draft tokens are re-run through the deep layers in a single pass. This design preserves exact equivalence with standard decoding while increasing efficiency and requiring no modification of base model weights.

Result: Across multiple large language model architectures and a range of long-form generation tasks, the proposed method achieves up to 2.33x wall-clock speedup compared with standard autoregressive decoding. The outputs remain exactly identical to those from the original model, demonstrating that the speedup does not degrade generation quality.

Conclusion: The proposed self-draft framework effectively addresses the shortcomings of prior self-speculative methods by (a) correcting shallow-layer overconfidence via temperature annealing, (b) adjusting speculation length based on token difficulty, and (c) amortizing deep-layer computation through a unified parallel pass. It delivers substantial, architecture-agnostic speedups in long-form generation without changing model parameters or output behavior, making it a practical and general technique for accelerating LLM inference.

Abstract: Speculative decoding has emerged as a promising approach to accelerate autoregressive inference in large language models (LLMs). Self-draft methods, which leverage the base LLM itself for speculation, avoid the overhead of auxiliary draft models but face limitations: shallow layers often produce overconfident yet incorrect token predictions, and the presence of difficult tokens in a draft sequence forces redundant computation through deeper layers, undermining both draft acceptance and overall speedup. To address these issues, we propose a novel self-draft framework that suppresses spurious confidence via layer-wise temperature annealing in early-exit decision and adaptively bounds speculation length based on token-wise decoding difficulty. By reprocessing the hidden states of draft tokens in a unified parallel pass through deep layers, our method maintains exact output equivalence with the original model while maximizing computational efficiency. It requires no modifications to the base LLM parameters and achieves up to 2.33x wall-time speedup over standard autoregressive decoding across diverse long-form generation tasks and multiple model architectures.

</details>

To overcome these limitations, we developed a Clinical Agentic Research Intelligence System (CARIS) that automates the clinical research workflow while preserving data privacy, enabling comprehensive studies without direct access to raw data. We evaluated CARIS on three heterogeneous datasets with distinct clinical tasks. Research plans and IRB documents were finalized within three to four iterations, using evidence from literature and data. The system supported Vibe ML by exploring feature-model combinations, ranking the top ten models, and generating performance visualizations. Final reports showed high completeness based on a checklist derived from the TRIPOD+AI framework, achieving 96% coverage in LLM evaluation and 82% in human evaluation. CARIS demonstrates that agentic AI can transform clinical hypotheses into executable research workflows across heterogeneous datasets. By eliminating the need for coding and direct data access, the system lowers barriers and bridges public and private clinical data environments.
