### [23] [CascadeDebate: Multi-Agent Deliberation for Cost-Aware LLM Cascades](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12262)
*Raeyoung Chang,Dongwook Kwon,Jisoo Lee,Nikhil Verma*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: CascadeDebate improves cascaded LLM systems by adding selective multi-agent deliberation at escalation boundaries, boosting accuracy while controlling cost.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional cascaded LLM systems use a single model per tier and escalate to larger models or humans when uncertain, which leads to premature, expensive escalations and poor handling of ambiguous queries. There is a need for a way to better resolve uncertainty and ambiguity within each tier before escalating, to improve accuracy-cost trade-offs and dynamically scale compute based on query difficulty.

Method: CascadeDebate introduces multi-agent deliberation modules at the escalation boundaries of each cascade tier. Confidence-based routers detect uncertain queries and trigger lightweight ensembles of agents that debate and seek consensus only for those cases, while confident queries are handled by single-model inference. The architecture alternates between single-model prediction and selective multi-agent deliberation across increasing model scales, with humans as the final fallback. Additionally, an online threshold optimizer is used to adaptively tune the confidence thresholds and routing policies based on observed data distributions, instead of relying on fixed thresholds.

Result: On five benchmarks covering science, medical, and general knowledge tasks, CascadeDebate achieves up to 26.75% performance gains over strong baselines, including both single-model cascades and standalone multi-agent systems. The online threshold optimizer is shown to be crucial, providing 20.98% to 52.33% relative accuracy improvements over fixed routing policies and enabling the system to adapt to real-world data shifts.

Conclusion: Embedding selective multi-agent deliberation within each tier of a cascaded LLM architecture allows better handling of ambiguous queries, reduces unnecessary escalation to larger models or human experts, and yields a more efficient accuracy-cost-abstention trade-off. The dynamic, confidence-aware routing and online threshold optimization enable the system to scale test-time compute to match query difficulty and to adapt to changing data distributions, outperforming traditional cascades and multi-agent setups.

Abstract: Cascaded LLM systems coordinate models of varying sizes with human experts to balance accuracy, cost, and abstention under uncertainty. However, single-model tiers at each stage often struggle with ambiguous queries, triggering premature escalations to costlier models or experts due to under-confidence and inefficient compute scaling. CascadeDebate addresses this gap by inserting multi-agent deliberation directly at each tier's escalation boundary. Confidence-based routers activate lightweight agent ensembles only for uncertain cases, enabling consensus-driven resolution of ambiguities internally without invoking higher-cost upgrades. Our unified architecture alternates single-model inference with selective multi-agent deliberation across model scales, culminating in human experts as the final fallback. This design scales test-time compute dynamically according to query difficulty. Across five benchmarks spanning science, medicine, and general knowledge, CascadeDebate outperforms strong single-model cascades and standalone multi-agent systems by up to 26.75 percent. An online threshold optimizer proves essential, boosting accuracy by 20.98 to 52.33 percent relative improvement over fixed policies and enabling elastic adaptation to real-world distributions.

By incrementally reading spreadsheets with multi-modal cues and using a verified intermediate structural representation for downstream reasoning, it outperforms existing LLM-based agents and demonstrates the promise of step-by-step, structure-aware multi-agent approaches for real-world spreadsheet tasks.

The method first constructs a structural sketch and row/column summaries, and then performs task-driven reasoning over this intermediate representation in the Solving Stage. To further enhance reliability, we design a verification module that validates extracted structures via targeted inspections, reducing error propagation and ensuring trustworthy inputs for downstream reasoning. Extensive experiments on two spreadsheet datasets demonstrate the effectiveness of our approach. With GPT-OSS-120B, SpreadsheetAgent achieves 38.16% on Spreadsheet Bench, outperforming the ChatGPT Agent baseline (35.27%) by 2.89 absolute points. These results highlight the potential of SpreadsheetAgent to advance robust and scalable spreadsheet understanding in real-world applications. Code is available at https://github.com/renhouxing/SpreadsheetAgent.git.

Result: Empirical results show that ToxiTrace improves both sentence-level toxicity classification accuracy and token/span-level toxic evidence extraction. Conclusion: ToxiTrace offers a practical, explainability-focused enhancement to existing encoder-based Chinese toxicity detectors, simultaneously improving performance and interpretability by aligning model gradients and contrastive reasoning with fine-grained toxic evidence spans. Abstract: Existing Chinese toxic content detection methods mainly target sentence-level classification but often fail to provide readable and contiguous toxic evidence spans. We propose \textbf{ToxiTrace}, an explainability-oriented method for BERT-style encoders with three components: (1) \textbf{CuSA}, which refines encoder-derived saliency cues into fine-grained toxic spans with lightweight LLM guidance; (2) \textbf{GCLoss}, a gradient-constrained objective that concentrates token-level saliency on toxic evidence while suppressing irrelevant activations; and (3) \textbf{ARCL}, which constructs sample-specific contrastive reasoning pairs to sharpen the semantic boundary between toxic and non-toxic content. Experiments show that ToxiTrace improves classification accuracy and toxic span extraction while preserving efficient encoder-based inference and producing more coherent, human-readable explanations. ### [28] [Masked by Consensus: Disentangling Privileged Knowledge in LLM Correctness](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12373)
*Tomer Ashuach,Liat Ein-Dor,Shai Gretz,Yoav Katz,Yonatan Belinkov*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper tests whether language models have privileged internal knowledge about when their own answers are correct, beyond what other models can see.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In humans, introspection lets us judge our own understanding using internal mental states others cannot access. The authors want to know if large language models analogously encode internal signals about the correctness of their own outputs, and whether these signals are more informative than what can be inferred from other models’ states or outputs.

Method: They train binary correctness classifiers (“probes”) on hidden representations associated with question answering. Probes are trained in two settings: (1) self-probes that read the hidden states of the model that produced the answer; (2) peer-probes that read hidden states from other models answering the same questions. They evaluate probe accuracy both on full datasets and on disagreement subsets where models differ in their predicted answers, and they compare behavior across factual QA and math reasoning tasks as well as across layers to see where any privileged signals emerge.

Result: On the full evaluation datasets, probes using a model’s own internal representations perform similarly to probes using other models’ representations, suggesting no clear privileged self-knowledge when averaged broadly. However, on disagreement subsets where models’ predictions conflict, self-probes outperform peer-probes in factual question answering, revealing domain-specific privileged knowledge. This advantage is absent for math reasoning, where self- and peer-based probes perform similarly. Layer-wise analysis shows the factual privileged signal grows from early to mid layers onward, in contrast to math where no consistent depth pattern of advantage appears.

Conclusion: Language models do encode some form of domain-specific privileged knowledge about the correctness of their own factual answers, detectable in their internal activations and exceeding what can be extracted from peer models’ states when the models disagree. This privileged self-knowledge appears tied to model-specific memory retrieval mechanisms that strengthen through early-to-mid layers, while math reasoning correctness does not exhibit the same introspective advantage, suggesting different internal organization and accessibility of factual vs. mathematical knowledge.

Abstract: Humans use introspection to evaluate their understanding through private internal states inaccessible to external observers. We investigate whether large language models possess similar privileged knowledge about answer correctness, information unavailable through external observation. We train correctness classifiers on question representations from both a model's own hidden states and external models, testing whether self-representations provide a performance advantage. On standard evaluation, we find no advantage: self-probes perform comparably to peer-model probes. We hypothesize this is due to high inter-model agreement of answer correctness. To isolate genuine privileged knowledge, we evaluate on disagreement subsets, where models produce conflicting predictions. Here, we discover domain-specific privileged knowledge: self-representations consistently outperform peer representations in factual knowledge tasks, but show no advantage in math reasoning. We further localize this domain asymmetry across model layers, finding that the factual advantage emerges progressively from early-to-mid layers onward, consistent with model-specific memory retrieval, while math reasoning shows no consistent advantage at any depth.

### [29] [Cooperative Memory Paging with Keyword Bookmarks for Long-Horizon LLM Conversations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12376)
*Ziyang Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces cooperative paging, where long conversations exceeding an LLM’s context window are handled by replacing evicted segments with compact keyword bookmarks and a recall() tool that lets the model restore needed content, yielding state-of-the-art performance on a long-conversation benchmark and detailed insights into paging design choices.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs struggle with very long, multi-session conversations because their context windows are finite. Once the context is full, older parts must be removed, and it is unclear how the model should later recover important past information when it becomes relevant again. Existing strategies—simple truncation, classical IR, generic search tools, or keeping all context—either lose critical information, are inefficient, or do not align well with how LLMs decide what to retrieve. The paper aims to design and analyze a principled mechanism that lets LLMs offload and later recover past conversational content effectively.

Method: The authors propose cooperative paging: when conversation segments must be evicted from the context, they are replaced with short keyword-based bookmarks of about 8–24 tokens that summarize the evicted page and include an index (e.g., [pN:keywords]). The model is given a recall() tool that, when invoked with a page index, retrieves the full content of that page back into context. They evaluate this method on the LoCoMo benchmark (10 real multi-session conversations with 300+ turns) across four LLMs and compare against truncation, BM25 retrieval, word-overlap retrieval, a generic search-tool baseline, and even full-context access. They also perform a systematic ablation study with a 5x4 design over page boundary strategies (e.g., fixed-size vs. topic-based) and eviction policies (e.g., FIFO, LFU) using thousands of synthetic and LoCoMo probing tasks. Additionally, they compare different bookmark generation strategies and analyze failure modes in recall decisions.

Result: Cooperative paging achieves the best answer quality on LoCoMo among six methods across four different LLMs, outperforming not only truncation and traditional retrieval baselines but even a full-context condition, with statistical significance confirmed by four independent LLM judges (p=0.017 via paired bootstrap). In the ablation study, coarse, fixed-size paging (fixed_20) yields high performance (96.7%), while a seemingly more sophisticated content-aware topic_shift boundary strategy severely underperforms (56.7%). The optimal eviction policy depends on data: FIFO works best on synthetic data, whereas LFU is superior on LoCoMo. Two learned or refined bookmark generation strategies provide substantial gains over a heuristic baseline, improving end-to-end performance by +4.4 and +8.7 points. Despite strong overall gains, a key bottleneck remains: given non-distinctive bookmarks, models invoke recall() in 96% of relevant cases but choose the correct page only 57% of the time; increasing keyword specificity alone yields about a 25-point accuracy improvement.

Conclusion: Cooperative paging is an effective framework for managing long LLM conversations beyond the context window by having the model collaborate in evicting and later recovering information through compact keyword bookmarks and a recall() tool. It not only surpasses a variety of established baselines—including full-context access—on a realistic long-conversation benchmark but also reveals nuanced design trade-offs in page sizing, eviction policy, and bookmark generation. The study demonstrates that simple fixed-size paging with appropriate eviction and more specific, discriminative bookmarks enables reliable recall behavior, while insufficiently distinctive bookmarks are the main remaining failure point. This suggests that future work should focus on improving bookmark discrimination and retrieval decision-making as central levers for further gains in long-context conversational performance.

Abstract: When LLM conversations grow beyond the context window, old content must be evicted -- but how does the model recover it when needed? We propose cooperative paging: evicted segments are replaced with minimal keyword bookmarks ([pN:keywords], ~8-24 tokens each), and the model is given a recall() tool to retrieve full content on demand. On the LoCoMo benchmark (10 real multi-session conversations, 300+ turns), cooperative paging achieves the highest answer quality among six methods -- outperforming truncation, BM25, word-overlap retrieval, a search-tool baseline, and full context -- on four models (GPT-4o-mini, DeepSeek-v3.2, Claude Haiku, GLM-5), confirmed by four independent LLM judges ($p=0.017$, paired bootstrap). We then study the paging design space with a 5x4 ablation over boundary strategies and eviction policies (3,176 synthetic probes, 1,600 LoCoMo probes). Key findings: (1) coarse fixed-size pages (fixed_20) reach 96.7% while content-aware topic_shift collapses to 56.7%; (2) eviction policy choice is data-dependent (FIFO best on synthetic, LFU on LoCoMo); (3) two bookmark generation strategies improve over the heuristic baseline (+4.4 and +8.7 E2E points); (4) the remaining bottleneck is bookmark discrimination -- the model triggers recall() 96% of the time but selects the correct page only 57% when bookmarks are insufficiently distinctive. Keyword specificity alone accounts for a 25 percentage point accuracy difference.

### [31] [ReasonXL: Shifting LLM Reasoning Language Without Sacrificing Performance](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12378)
*Daniil Gurgurov,Tom Röhr,Sebastian von Rohrscheidt,Josef van Genabith,Alexander Löser,Simon Ostermann*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces ReasonXL, a large multilingual reasoning-trace corpus, and shows how to adapt LLMs to think and answer fully in specific non-English languages using SFT and RL with verifiable rewards, while analyzing how this adaptation changes internal representations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most LLMs, even when handling non-English tasks, internally reason in English, which is a mismatch for users and applications that require native-language reasoning traces. There is a lack of large-scale, parallel, language-specific reasoning-trace datasets and a limited understanding of how to systematically steer the reasoning language without sacrificing performance or cross-lingual abilities. The paper aims to close this gap by providing data, methods, and representational insights to support genuine multilingual reasoning.

Method: 1) Construct ReasonXL, a large parallel corpus of prompts, reasoning traces, and final answers across five European languages. 2) Fine-tune LLMs with a two-step pipeline: supervised fine-tuning (SFT) on language-specific reasoning traces, followed by reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards (RLVR) that explicitly rewards correct solutions expressed in the target language. 3) Perform representational analysis across model layers, using interventions and measurement of activation and weight changes, to identify where language identity and adaptation effects are localized in the network.

Result: LLMs fine-tuned with ReasonXL can produce full reasoning traces and answers entirely in a target non-English language while maintaining or improving task performance compared to baselines. General knowledge is largely preserved, and cross-lingual transfer capabilities remain broadly intact. Representational analysis shows that early layers contain an activation bottleneck that determines language identity, while higher layers capture most of the adaptation-induced changes. RLVR, compared to SFT, achieves larger behavioral shifts from the base model with smaller parameter updates, indicating a more efficient use of representational capacity.

Conclusion: It is possible to systematically adapt English-centric LLMs to reason natively in other languages by supervising language-specific reasoning traces and applying RL with verifiable rewards, without major performance trade-offs. The ReasonXL corpus enables such training at scale, and the internal analysis reveals a depth-wise functional split for language control and adaptation. RLVR appears particularly parameter-efficient in rerouting model behavior, providing a promising direction for controllable multilingual reasoning in future LLM development.

Abstract: Despite advances in multilingual capabilities, most large language models (LLMs) remain English-centric in their training and, crucially, in their production of reasoning traces. Even when tasked with non-English problems, these models predominantly reason in English, creating a fundamental mismatch for non-English usage scenarios.
  We address this disparity directly with three contributions. (i) We introduce ReasonXL, the first large-scale parallel corpus of cross-domain reasoning traces spanning five European languages (English, German, French, Italian, and Spanish), with over two million aligned samples per language, each comprising prompts, reasoning traces, and final outputs, enabling direct supervision of language-specific reasoning. (ii) Using ReasonXL, we demonstrate that LLMs can be adapted to reason entirely in a desired target language, using a simple two-stage pipeline of supervised fine-tuning (SFT) followed by reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards (RLVR). The resulting models match or exceed baseline performance, with minimal loss in general knowledge and broadly preserved cross-lingual transfer. (iii) We conduct an extensive representational analysis of the adaptation and find a clear functional division across model depth: early layers contain an activation bottleneck that causally determines language identity, while upper layers concentrate the weight and activation changes driven by adaptation. We further find that RLVR achieves greater behavioral divergence from the base model with smaller parameter updates than SFT, suggesting a more efficient representational rerouting despite much smaller weight updates.

### [32] [From Myopic Selection to Long-Horizon Awareness: Sequential LLM Routing for Multi-Turn Dialogue](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12385)
*Jiarui Zhang,Xiangyu Liu,Yong Hu,Chaoyue Niu,Hang Zeng,Shaojie Tang,Fan Wu,Guihai Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: DialRouter is a method for long-horizon routing among multiple LLMs in multi-turn dialogue, learning a routing policy from MCTS-generated trajectories to maximize cumulative task success under cost constraints.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM routing methods focus on single-turn, myopic selection of models, which is insufficient for multi-turn dialogues where early choices affect later turns and rewards are often delayed. There is a need for a routing strategy that explicitly handles interaction dynamics and long-term cumulative performance in multi-turn conversations while balancing performance and inference cost.

Method: DialRouter formulates multi-turn LLM routing as a long-horizon sequential decision problem. It first applies Monte Carlo Tree Search (MCTS) over possible dialogue branches created by choosing different LLMs at each turn, collecting high-reward trajectories. From these search-derived trajectories, it trains a lightweight routing policy that can select LLMs at each turn without online search. The method incorporates retrieval-based future state approximation to better estimate long-term returns and includes a cost-aware reward to manage the performance-cost trade-off.

Result: Across both open-domain and domain-specific dialogue benchmarks, and over diverse pools of candidate open-source and closed-source LLMs, DialRouter achieves higher task success rates than using any single LLM or existing routing baselines. When a cost-aware reward is used, the approach also yields a better balance between performance and computational cost than prior methods.

Conclusion: Treating multi-turn LLM routing as a long-horizon control problem and using MCTS to generate supervisory data for a learned routing policy leads to superior cumulative performance in dialogue tasks. DialRouter effectively exploits the complementary strengths of multiple LLMs over a conversation, improving task success while providing an advantageous performance-cost trade-off compared with single-model and myopic routing approaches.

Abstract: Multi-turn dialogue is the predominant form of interaction with large language models (LLMs). While LLM routing is effective in single-turn settings, existing methods fail to maximize cumulative performance in multi-turn dialogue due to interaction dynamics and delayed rewards. To address this challenge, we move from myopic, single-turn selection to long-horizon sequential routing for multi-turn dialogue. Accordingly, we propose DialRouter, which first performs MCTS to explore dialogue branches induced by different LLM selections and collect trajectories with high cumulative rewards. DialRouter then learns a lightweight routing policy from search-derived data, augmented with retrieval-based future state approximation, enabling multi-turn routing without online search. Experiments on both open-domain and domain-specific dialogue tasks across diverse candidate sets of both open-source and closed-source LLMs demonstrate that DialRouter significantly outperforms single LLMs and existing routing baselines in task success rate, while achieving a superior performance-cost trade-off when combined with a cost-aware reward.

*Yudong Li,Jiawei Cai,Linlin Shen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes Knowledge Coordinate Conditioning (KoCo), which assigns each document a 3D semantic coordinate and prepends it as a textual prefix during LLM pre-training to inject explicit global context, improving downstream performance and training efficiency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Conventional LLM pre-training linearizes text into flat token streams, ignoring higher-level, real-world knowledge structures (e.g., topic, domain, time, or other semantic axes) that humans implicitly use to contextualize information. This can slow learning, blur distinctions between stable knowledge and noise, and contribute to hallucinations. The paper aims to incorporate such global contextual structure into pre-training in a simple, scalable way.

Method: Each document in the pre-training corpus is embedded or otherwise mapped into a 3D semantic coordinate space. These three real-valued coordinates are then converted into textual tokens and prepended as a prefix to the document during LLM pre-training, so that every training example is conditioned on its position in semantic space. The approach does not alter the base architecture; it only augments inputs with these coordinate prefixes, allowing the model to implicitly learn the meaning and utility of the coordinates for organizing knowledge.

Result: On 10 downstream tasks, models trained with KoCo achieve consistently higher performance than baselines trained on the same data without coordinate prefixes. Additionally, KoCo speeds up pre-training convergence by about 30%, indicating more sample-efficient learning. Analyses further show that models with KoCo better separate core, stable facts from incidental or noisy text, leading to reduced hallucination rates in generation benchmarks.

Conclusion: Injecting explicit, low-dimensional semantic coordinates as textual prefixes during pre-training is an effective, architecture-agnostic way to give LLMs global contextual signals about document position in knowledge space. This improves downstream task performance, accelerates convergence, and helps the model distinguish stable knowledge from noise, thereby mitigating hallucinations. The results suggest that simple forms of structured, global conditioning can substantially improve the quality and reliability of LLMs.

Abstract: Standard Large Language Model (LLM) pre-training typically treats corpora as flattened token sequences, often overlooking the real-world context that humans naturally rely on to contextualize information. To bridge this gap, we introduce Knowledge Coordinate Conditioning (KoCo), a simple method that maps every document into a three-dimensional semantic coordinate. By prepending these coordinates as textual prefixes for pre-training, we aim to equip the model with explicit contextual awareness to learn the documents within the real-world knowledge structure. Experiment results demonstrate that KoCo significantly enhances performance across 10 downstream tasks and accelerates pre-training convergence by approximately 30\%. Furthermore, our analysis indicates that explicitly modeling knowledge coordinates helps the model distinguish stable facts from noise, effectively mitigating hallucination in generated outputs.

### [35] [Decoding by Perturbation: Mitigating MLLM Hallucinations via Dynamic Textual Perturbation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12424)
*Sihang Jia,Shuliang Liu,Songbo Yang,Yibo Yan,Xin Zou,Xuming Hu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes DeP, a training-free decoding framework that reduces hallucinations in multimodal LLMs by using controlled textual perturbations during inference to reveal and counteract language priors that override visual evidence.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multimodal LLMs tend to hallucinate, often because language priors dominate visual evidence. Existing training-free mitigation methods either distort visual features away from natural images or apply intrusive manipulations that hurt generation fluency. There is a need for a training-free, non-intrusive, and more principled way to reduce hallucinations without retraining or harming generative quality.

Method: The paper views multimodal hallucination as hypersensitivity of visual grounding to text phrasing at decoding time. It introduces Decoding by Perturbation (DeP), which uses a dynamic probe that applies multi-level textual perturbations to the input text during decoding. By observing attention variance induced by these perturbations, DeP identifies stable evidence regions vs. suspicious noisy regions in the feature space and reweights them. It also builds an interpretable prior drift direction in logit space based on statistics over perturbed decodings, then uses this direction to counteract probability biases caused by spurious textual co-occurrences, all without retraining the model.

Result: Experiments on multiple hallucination benchmarks show that DeP substantially reduces hallucinations of multimodal LLMs and yields better performance than prior training-free mitigation methods, while maintaining or improving generative fluency. Quantitative metrics and qualitative analyses confirm its effectiveness.

Conclusion: Decoding by Perturbation (DeP) provides an effective, training-free, and interpretable framework to mitigate prior-induced hallucinations in multimodal LLMs by leveraging controlled textual perturbations during decoding to expose and counteract harmful language priors. This approach improves hallucination robustness without modifying model parameters or heavily distorting visual features, suggesting a promising direction for inference-time control of multimodal generation.

Abstract: Multimodal Large Language Models frequently suffer from inference hallucinations, partially stemming from language priors dominating visual evidence. Existing training-free mitigation methods either perturb the visual representation and deviate from the natural image distribution, or enforce intrusive manipulations that compromise the model's inherent generative fluency. We introduce a novel perspective that multimodal hallucination manifests as the hypersensitivity of visual grounding to textual phrasing during the decoding phase. Building on this insight, we propose Decoding by Perturbation (DeP), a training-free framework mitigating prior-induced hallucinations via controlled textual interventions. DeP employs a dynamic probe applying multi-level textual perturbations to elicit latent language priors. Leveraging attention variance, it enhances stable evidence regions while suppressing suspicious noise in the feature space. Furthermore, it constructs an interpretable prior drift direction using logits statistics to counteract probability biases from textual co-occurrences. Extensive experiments confirm DeP effectively reduces hallucinations and achieves superior performance across multiple benchmarks.

To address this issue, we present GLeMM, a new derivational resource designed for experimentation and data-driven description in morphology. ### [37] [Latent-Condensed Transformer for Efficient Long Context Modeling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12452)
*Zeng You,Yaofo Chen,Qiuwu Chen,Ying Sun,Shuhai Zhang,Yingjian Li,Yaowei Wang,Mingkui Tan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Latent-Condensed Attention (LCA), a method that jointly reduces KV-cache memory and attention computation for long-context LLMs by condensing information in MLA’s latent space, achieving large speedups and memory savings with minimal performance loss.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs struggle with long contexts because KV cache grows linearly with sequence length and self-attention computation grows quadratically. Existing methods only partially solve this: MLA compresses KV cache but not compute, while sparse attention reduces compute but does not operate on MLA’s compressed latent representations. There is a need for a unified method that simultaneously reduces KV cache and computation, ideally in a way that is compatible with existing attention variants and does not require extra parameters.

Method: The authors propose Latent-Condensed Attention (LCA), which works on the latent space used in Multi-head Latent Attention (MLA). In this latent space, token representations are separated into semantic latent vectors and positional keys. LCA (1) aggregates semantic latent vectors via query-aware pooling so that similar or less relevant information is merged in a way dependent on the current query, and (2) preserves essential positional information by selecting a subset of positional keys as anchors. This condensation reduces the number of items that attention has to process, thus lowering both the KV cache size and computation. The design is architecture-agnostic, so it can also be applied to other attention mechanisms like GQA. The paper also provides a theoretical analysis establishing a length-independent error bound for the approximation introduced by condensation.

Result: Empirically, LCA yields up to 2.5× prefilling speedup and a 90% reduction in KV cache size at 128K context length, while maintaining performance competitive with baselines that do not use such condensation. This demonstrates that substantial efficiency gains are possible without major degradation in model quality, even at very long sequence lengths.

Conclusion: LCA offers an effective and general method to handle long contexts in LLMs by jointly optimizing memory and computation within the latent attention space. It integrates seamlessly with MLA and can be extended to other attention variants like GQA, achieving strong speed and memory benefits underpinned by theoretical guarantees, while preserving model performance on long-context tasks.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) face significant challenges in processing long contexts due to the linear growth of the key-value (KV) cache and quadratic complexity of self-attention. Existing approaches address these bottlenecks separately: Multi-head Latent Attention (MLA) reduces the KV cache by projecting tokens into a low-dimensional latent space, while sparse attention reduces computation. However, sparse methods cannot operate natively on MLA's compressed latent structure, missing opportunities for joint optimization. In this paper, we propose Latent-Condensed Attention (LCA), which directly condenses context within MLA's latent space, where the representation is disentangled into semantic latent vectors and positional keys. LCA separately aggregates semantic vectors via query-aware pooling and preserves positional keys via anchor selection. This approach jointly reduces both computational cost and KV cache without adding parameters. Beyond MLA, LCA's design is architecture-agnostic and readily extends to other attention mechanisms such as GQA. Theoretically, we prove a length-independent error bound. Experiments show LCA achieves up to 2.5$\times$ prefilling speedup and 90% KV cache reduction at 128K context while maintaining competitive performance.

</details>

GPT-4o Mini is substantially better than Gemini for Hausa and Fongbe, and prompt design must be tailored to each language’s characteristics. Openly releasing the generated corpora and code can help other researchers bootstrap resources for similar low-resource languages.

We systematically compare six elicitation task types across two commercial LLMs (GPT-4o Mini and Gemini 2.5 Flash). GPT-4o Mini extracts 6-41 times more usable target-language words per API call than Gemini. Optimal strategies differ by language: Hausa benefits from functional text and dialogue, while Fongbe requires constrained generation prompts. We release all generated corpora and code.

### [40] [KG-Reasoner: A Reinforced Model for End-to-End Multi-Hop Knowledge Graph Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12487)
*Shuai Wang,Yinan Yu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes KG-Reasoner, an end-to-end framework that trains a reasoning-focused LLM via reinforcement learning to perform unified multi-step reasoning over knowledge graphs, achieving competitive or better performance on multiple multi-hop and knowledge-intensive benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although LLMs are powerful in language understanding and generation, they perform poorly on knowledge-intensive and multi-hop reasoning tasks. Knowledge graphs have been effective external knowledge sources for KBQA, but precise multi-hop reasoning over KGs is still difficult. Existing methods typically decompose reasoning into fixed, isolated pipeline steps, which limits flexibility, fragments the reasoning process, and causes loss of crucial intermediate information. There is a need for a more integrated, flexible reasoning framework that can better leverage KGs for complex queries.

Method: The authors present KG-Reasoner, an end-to-end framework that embeds the entire multi-step reasoning process into a single "thinking" phase of a Reasoning LLM. Instead of using a rigid pipeline, they train the LLM via reinforcement learning to internalize the KG traversal process itself. The model learns to dynamically explore reasoning paths in the KG, including the ability to backtrack when needed, effectively treating KG navigation as a sequential decision-making problem optimized with RL signals.

Result: KG-Reasoner is evaluated on eight benchmarks focused on multi-hop and knowledge-intensive reasoning. Across these datasets, it achieves performance that is competitive with or superior to state-of-the-art approaches, indicating that the unified RL-based reasoning paradigm can match or exceed the effectiveness of traditional pipelined KG reasoning methods.

Conclusion: Integrating KG traversal into an LLM’s internal reasoning process and training it end-to-end with reinforcement learning enables more flexible and coherent multi-hop reasoning over knowledge graphs. KG-Reasoner overcomes key limitations of pipeline-based methods and demonstrates strong performance on diverse benchmarks, showing that unifying multi-step reasoning in a single thinking phase is a promising direction for knowledge-intensive LLM reasoning.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) exhibit strong abilities in natural language understanding and generation, yet they struggle with knowledge-intensive reasoning. Structured Knowledge Graphs (KGs) provide an effective form of external knowledge representation and have been widely used to enhance performance in classical Knowledge Base Question Answering (KBQA) tasks. However, performing precise multi-hop reasoning over KGs for complex queries remains highly challenging. Most existing approaches decompose the reasoning process into a sequence of isolated steps executed through a fixed pipeline. While effective to some extent, such designs constrain reasoning flexibility and fragment the overall decision process, often leading to incoherence and the loss of critical intermediate information from earlier steps. In this paper, we introduce KG-Reasoner, an end-to-end framework that integrates multi-step reasoning into a unified "thinking" phase of a Reasoning LLM. Through Reinforcement Learning (RL), the LLM is trained to internalize the KG traversal process, enabling it to dynamically explore reasoning paths, and perform backtracking when necessary. Experiments on eight multi-hop and knowledge-intensive reasoning benchmarks demonstrate that KG-Reasoner achieves competitive or superior performance compared to the state-of-the-art methods. Codes are available at the repository: https://github.com/Wangshuaiia/KG-Reasoner.

### [41] [Latent Planning Emerges with Scale](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12493)
*Michael Hanna,Emmanuel Ameisen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates whether and how large language models (LLMs) perform implicit, or “latent,” planning during generation, and how this capability scales with model size.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although LLMs appear to handle planning-heavy tasks (e.g., coherent long-form text, code) without explicit step-by-step plans, it is unclear whether they internally plan ahead or simply rely on local pattern matching. Understanding whether, how, and to what extent LLMs plan is important both scientifically (for mechanistic interpretability and cognitive analogies) and practically (for better control, reliability, and task performance).

Method: The authors formally define “latent planning” as internal representations that both (1) influence a particular future word or concept and (2) shape earlier tokens to make that future output appropriate. They probe models from the Qwen-3 family (0.6B–14B parameters) on controlled planning tasks. For simple tasks, they examine cases such as choosing “an” vs. “a” conditioned on a later, yet-to-appear word like “accountant” and inspect internal features that encode this planned word and causally affect earlier token choice. For more complex planning, they use rhyming couplet completion to test how far ahead models choose rhyme targets. They also run interventions that steer models toward particular planned words in prose to see whether planning can be elicited and how it scales.

Result: On simple planning tasks, latent planning ability increases with model scale across Qwen-3 models: larger models develop clearer internal features encoding planned-for words (e.g., “accountant”) that drive earlier token selection (e.g., “an”). Mid-sized models (4B–8B) already show emerging, but weaker, planning mechanisms. On rhyming couplets, models often preselect a rhyme word shortly before it appears, but even large models rarely plan multiple steps or long spans ahead. When explicitly steered towards specific future words in prose, models exhibit some planning behavior that also scales with size, though it remains limited in temporal extent.

Conclusion: The paper proposes an operational and mechanistic definition of latent planning in LLMs and demonstrates that such planning exists, is detectable in internal representations, and becomes stronger with model scale. However, the planning horizon is short, and even large models seldom plan far into the future. These findings provide a framework for measuring planning, evidence that planning mechanisms emerge with scale, and a basis for future work on extending and controlling LLM planning capabilities.

Abstract: LLMs can perform seemingly planning-intensive tasks, like writing coherent stories or functioning code, without explicitly verbalizing a plan; however, the extent to which they implicitly plan is unknown. In this paper, we define latent planning as occurring when LLMs possess internal planning representations that (1) cause the generation of a specific future token or concept, and (2) shape preceding context to license said future token or concept. We study the Qwen-3 family (0.6B-14B) on simple planning tasks, finding that latent planning ability increases with scale. Models that plan possess features that represent a planned-for word like "accountant", and cause them to output "an" rather than "a"; moreover, even the less-successful Qwen-3 4B-8B have nascent planning mechanisms. On the more complex task of completing rhyming couplets, we find that models often identify a rhyme ahead of time, but even large models seldom plan far ahead. However, we can elicit some planning that increases with scale when steering models towards planned words in prose. In sum, we offer a framework for measuring planning and mechanistic evidence of how models' planning abilities grow with scale.

</details>


### [42] [Topology-Aware Reasoning over Incomplete Knowledge Graph with Graph-Based Soft Prompting](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12503)
*Shuai Wang,Xixi Wang,Yinan Yu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose GraSP, a soft prompting framework using GNN-encoded subgraphs to guide LLMs for multi-hop KBQA, improving robustness to incomplete knowledge graphs and achieving SOTA on several benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs hallucinate in knowledge-intensive tasks, and KBQA methods that do multi-hop reasoning via explicit path traversal are brittle when knowledge graphs are incomplete. There is a need for a method that can leverage richer graph structure while being robust to missing edges and reducing computation costs with large LLMs.

Method: They extract structural subgraphs from a knowledge graph for each question and encode them with a Graph Neural Network into continuous soft prompts. These soft graph prompts are fed into LLMs to enable subgraph-level reasoning instead of explicit node/edge traversal. They also adopt a two-stage pipeline: (1) a smaller LLM uses the soft prompts to select question-relevant entities and relations, and (2) a larger LLM performs evidence-aware answer generation, conditioned on the selected evidence and prompts.

Result: On four multi-hop KBQA benchmarks, their framework achieves state-of-the-art performance on three datasets, indicating improved accuracy and robustness compared to prior multi-hop KBQA methods. They also report reduced sensitivity to missing KG edges and lower computational cost due to the two-stage design.

Conclusion: Encoding KG subgraphs as soft prompts for LLMs enables more robust and effective multi-hop KBQA than traditional path traversal methods, particularly under KG incompleteness. The proposed two-stage framework balances efficiency and performance and establishes new SOTA results on several benchmarks, suggesting soft graph prompting is a promising direction for knowledge-grounded LLM reasoning.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) have shown remarkable capabilities across various tasks but remain prone to hallucinations in knowledge-intensive scenarios. Knowledge Base Question Answering (KBQA) mitigates this by grounding generation in Knowledge Graphs (KGs). However, most multi-hop KBQA methods rely on explicit edge traversal, making them fragile to KG incompleteness. In this paper, we proposed a novel graph-based soft prompting framework that shifts the reasoning paradigm from node-level path traversal to subgraph-level reasoning. Specifically, we employ a Graph Neural Network (GNN) to encode extracted structural subgraphs into soft prompts, enabling LLM to reason over richer structural context and identify relevant entities beyond immediate graph neighbors, thereby reducing sensitivity to missing edges. Furthermore, we introduce a two-stage paradigm that reduces computational cost while preserving good performance: a lightweight LLM first leverages the soft prompts to identify question-relevant entities and relations, followed by a more powerful LLM for evidence-aware answer generation. Experiments on four multi-hop KBQA benchmarks show that our approach achieves state-of-the-art performance on three of them, demonstrating its effectiveness. Code is available at the repository: https://github.com/Wangshuaiia/GraSP.

### [43] [Enhance-then-Balance Modality Collaboration for Robust Multimodal Sentiment Analysis](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12518)
*Kang He,Yuzhe Ding,Xinrong Wang,Fei Li,Chong Teng,Donghong Ji*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes EBMC, a framework for multimodal sentiment analysis that strengthens weaker modalities and balances their contributions to improve robustness and performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multimodal sentiment analysis methods suffer from modality imbalance: strong modalities (often text) dominate, while weaker non-verbal modalities (audio, visual) are under-utilized, harming fusion performance, especially when some modalities are noisy or missing.

Method: The authors design EBMC (Enhance-then-Balance Modality Collaboration), which first improves representations via semantic disentanglement and cross-modal enhancement to boost weaker modalities. Then it employs an Energy-guided Modality Coordination mechanism that implicitly rebalances gradients using a differentiable equilibrium objective, preventing dominant modalities from overwhelming others. Finally, an Instance-aware Modality Trust Distillation module estimates reliability per sample and adaptively adjusts fusion weights for each modality.

Result: Experiments show that EBMC achieves state-of-the-art or competitive performance on standard multimodal sentiment benchmarks and maintains strong results even when some modalities are missing or corrupted, demonstrating improved robustness and better use of weaker modalities.

Conclusion: By enhancing weaker modalities and explicitly balancing cross-modal collaboration through energy-guided coordination and instance-aware trust distillation, EBMC effectively mitigates modality competition, leading to more robust and accurate multimodal sentiment analysis under both full-modality and missing-modality conditions.

Abstract: Multimodal sentiment analysis (MSA) integrates heterogeneous text, audio, and visual signals to infer human emotions. While recent approaches leverage cross-modal complementarity, they often struggle to fully utilize weaker modalities. In practice, dominant modalities tend to overshadow non-verbal ones, inducing modality competition and limiting overall contributions. This imbalance degrades fusion performance and robustness under noisy or missing modalities. To address this, we propose a novel model, Enhance-then-Balance Modality Collaboration framework (EBMC). EBMC improves representation quality via semantic disentanglement and cross-modal enhancement, strengthening weaker modalities. To prevent dominant modalities from overwhelming others, an Energy-guided Modality Coordination mechanism achieves implicit gradient rebalancing via a differentiable equilibrium objective. Furthermore, Instance-aware Modality Trust Distillation estimates sample-level reliability to adaptively modulate fusion weights, ensuring robustness. Extensive experiments demonstrate that EBMC achieves state-of-the-art or competitive results and maintains strong performance under missing-modality settings.

### [44] [When Does Data Augmentation Help? Evaluating LLM and Back-Translation Methods for Hausa and Fongbe NLP](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12540)
*Mahounan Pericles Adjovi,Roald Eiselen,Prasenjit Mitra*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper tests two data augmentation methods (LLM generation and back-translation) for Hausa and Fongbe on NER and POS tasks, finding that effectiveness depends more on task type than language or LLM quality, and can even hurt performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Low-resource African languages lack sufficient labeled data for NLP tasks, and researchers often assume that synthetic data from LLMs or back-translation will generally help. However, the relationship between LLM generation quality, language characteristics, and task performance gains is unclear, especially for languages like Hausa and Fongbe. The paper aims to systematically test whether common augmentation methods reliably improve models, and whether their success is predictable from LLM quality or is task-dependent.

Method: The authors use Hausa and Fongbe datasets from MasakhaNER 2.0 and MasakhaPOS benchmarks. They create synthetic training data via (1) LLM-based generation using Gemini 2.5 Flash and (2) back-translation using NLLB-200. They augment the original training sets with these synthetic samples and train NER and POS taggers. They then compare performance metrics (F1 for NER and accuracy for POS) against non-augmented baselines for each language-task-method combination to analyze where augmentation helps or hurts.

Result: For NER in both Hausa and Fongbe, neither LLM-based generation nor back-translation improves over the baseline. In fact, LLM augmentation slightly reduces F1: −0.24% for Hausa and −1.81% for Fongbe. For POS tagging, effects depend on both language and augmentation type: LLM-based augmentation improves Fongbe POS by +0.33% accuracy but not Hausa, while back-translation improves Hausa POS by +0.17% but slightly harms Fongbe POS (−0.35%) and is nearly neutral for Hausa. Notably, the same LLM-generated Fongbe data has opposite effects across tasks, degrading NER while improving POS.

Conclusion: The study concludes that data augmentation effectiveness is primarily task-specific rather than determined by language or LLM generation quality alone. High-quality LLM-generated data does not guarantee improvements and may even harm performance, particularly for structured prediction tasks like NER. The divergent effects across tasks for the same language and synthetic data indicate that task structure plays a major role. Therefore, practitioners should treat data augmentation as a targeted, task-dependent intervention and empirically validate it instead of assuming it is a uniformly beneficial preprocessing step.

Abstract: Data scarcity limits NLP development for low-resource African languages. We evaluate two data augmentation methods -- LLM-based generation (Gemini 2.5 Flash) and back-translation (NLLB-200) -- for Hausa and Fongbe, two West African languages that differ substantially in LLM generation quality. We assess augmentation on named entity recognition (NER) and part-of-speech (POS) tagging using MasakhaNER 2.0 and MasakhaPOS benchmarks. Our results reveal that augmentation effectiveness depends on task type rather than language or LLM quality alone. For NER, neither method improves over baseline for either language; LLM augmentation reduces Hausa NER by 0.24% F1 and Fongbe NER by 1.81% F1. For POS tagging, LLM augmentation improves Fongbe by 0.33% accuracy, while back-translation improves Hausa by 0.17%; back-translation reduces Fongbe POS by 0.35% and has negligible effect on Hausa POS. The same LLM-generated synthetic data produces opposite effects across tasks for Fongbe -- hurting NER while helping POS -- suggesting task structure governs augmentation outcomes more than synthetic data quality. These findings challenge the assumption that LLM generation quality predicts augmentation success, and provide actionable guidance: data augmentation should be treated as a task-specific intervention rather than a universally beneficial preprocessing step.

</details>


### [45] [FABLE: Fine-grained Fact Anchoring for Unstructured Model Editing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12559)
*Peng Wang,Biyu Zhou,Xuehai Tang,Jizhong Han,Songlin Hu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: FABLE is a hierarchical framework for unstructured model editing that injects fine-grained facts in shallow layers and minimally updates deeper layers, improving precise fact access while keeping strong holistic text editing performance; evaluated with a new fine-grained benchmark UnFine.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current unstructured model editing methods tend to memorize and reproduce whole text segments, which leads to poor access to specific, fine-grained facts embedded in that text. There is a mismatch between the need to edit and retrieve individual facts and the way models generate holistic surface forms. The authors want a method that can reliably update and query specific factual knowledge in large language models without sacrificing overall editing quality.

Method: They propose FABLE, a two-stage hierarchical editing framework aligned with the unidirectional Transformer architecture. In stage one, discrete factual information is injected and anchored into the model’s shallow layers. In stage two, they apply minimal updates to deeper layers so that generated text remains coherent with the edited facts while avoiding full-text memorization. This explicitly decouples fact-level editing from holistic surface-form generation. Additionally, they design UnFine, a diagnostic benchmark made of fine-grained question-answer pairs and fact-level metrics to evaluate how well model editing methods support precise fact access.

Result: On experiments using UnFine and existing holistic editing evaluations, FABLE substantially improves performance on fine-grained question answering (indicating better access to edited facts), while still matching or surpassing state-of-the-art results on traditional holistic model editing benchmarks.

Conclusion: Decoupling fine-grained fact injection from holistic text generation, in a way that respects the layer-wise flow of Transformers, yields more controllable and precise model editing. FABLE demonstrates that anchoring facts in shallow layers with minimal deep-layer updates enables both accurate fine-grained fact access and strong overall text editing performance. The UnFine benchmark provides a more systematic way to assess this capability.

Abstract: Unstructured model editing aims to update models with real-world text, yet existing methods often memorize text holistically without reliable fine-grained fact access. To address this, we propose FABLE, a hierarchical framework that decouples fine-grained fact injection from holistic text generation. FABLE follows a two-stage, fact-first strategy: discrete facts are anchored in shallow layers, followed by minimal updates to deeper layers to produce coherent text. This decoupling resolves the mismatch between holistic recall and fine-grained fact access, reflecting the unidirectional Transformer flow in which surface-form generation amplifies rather than corrects underlying fact representations. We also introduce UnFine, a diagnostic benchmark with fine-grained question-answer pairs and fact-level metrics for systematic evaluation. Experiments show that FABLE substantially improves fine-grained question answering while maintaining state-of-the-art holistic editing performance. Our code is publicly available at https://github.com/caskcsg/FABLE.

</details>


### [46] [Transforming External Knowledge into Triplets for Enhanced Retrieval in RAG of LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12610)
*Xudong Wang,Chaoning Zhang,Qigan Sun,Zhenzhen Huang,Chang Lu,Sheng Zheng,Zeyu Ma,Caiyan Qin,Yang Yang,Hengtao Shen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Tri-RAG is a triplet-based Retrieval-Augmented Generation framework that structures external knowledge into Condition–Proof–Conclusion units to improve retrieval accuracy, reasoning alignment, and token efficiency compared to standard text-concatenation RAG.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard RAG systems usually retrieve raw text chunks and concatenate them as context, which causes redundant or weakly relevant information, long contexts, lower semantic alignment with the query, and fragmented reasoning, ultimately harming generation quality and wasting tokens. The authors aim to design a retrieval and context-construction mechanism that better aligns evidence with reasoning needs, improves retrieval precision, and reduces context length without sacrificing performance.

Method: The paper introduces Tri-RAG, which first converts natural-language external knowledge into structured triplets of the form (Condition, Proof, Conclusion) via prompt-based adaptation of a frozen LLM. These triplets explicitly encode logical relations between knowledge fragments. During retrieval, the Condition component serves as a semantic anchor: queries are matched to Conditions instead of long raw texts, enabling more targeted retrieval of relevant triplets. The framework then uses these structured triplets rather than concatenated unstructured passages as context for the generator, thereby improving alignment and efficiency, all while keeping the base model parameters frozen and relying on lightweight prompting.

Result: Across several benchmark datasets, Tri-RAG yields higher retrieval quality, better reasoning performance, and more stable generations than conventional RAG methods that use unstructured text concatenation. It also achieves better token efficiency by reducing the amount of retrieved and processed text, thus improving resource utilization in complex reasoning tasks.

Conclusion: Structuring external knowledge into Condition–Proof–Conclusion triplets and using the Condition as a retrieval anchor allows RAG systems to more precisely align retrieved evidence with queries, reduce redundant context, and support more coherent reasoning. Tri-RAG demonstrates that reasoning-aligned, triplet-based retrieval can simultaneously enhance accuracy, stability, and token efficiency, offering a more effective way to couple retrieval with generation than standard unstructured-context approaches.

Abstract: Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) mitigates hallucination in large language models (LLMs) by incorporating external knowledge during generation. However, the effectiveness of RAG depends not only on the design of the retriever and the capacity of the underlying model, but also on how retrieved evidence is structured and aligned with the query. Existing RAG approaches typically retrieve and concatenate unstructured text fragments as context, which often introduces redundant or weakly relevant information. This practice leads to excessive context accumulation, reduced semantic alignment, and fragmented reasoning chains, thereby degrading generation quality while increasing token consumption. To address these challenges, we propose Tri-RAG, a structured triplet-based retrieval framework that improves retrieval efficiency through reasoning-aligned context construction. Tri-RAG automatically transforms external knowledge from natural language into standardized structured triplets consisting of Condition, Proof, and Conclusion, explicitly capturing logical relations among knowledge fragments using lightweight prompt-based adaptation with frozen model parameters. Building on this representation, the triplet head Condition is treated as an explicit semantic anchor for retrieval and matching, enabling precise identification of query-relevant knowledge units without directly concatenating lengthy raw texts. As a result, Tri-RAG achieves a favorable balance between retrieval accuracy and context token efficiency. Experimental results across multiple benchmark datasets demonstrate that Tri-RAG significantly improves retrieval quality and reasoning efficiency, while producing more stable generation behavior and more efficient resource utilization in complex reasoning scenarios.

</details>

We train and compare six multilingual transformer encoders, from DistilBERT (135M parameters) to XLM-R-Large (560M parameters), under identical conditions. On our in-domain test set, XLM-R-Large achieves 0.868 F1-micro and 0.987 AUC-micro. To validate against human-annotated data, we evaluate all models zero-shot on GoEmotions (English) and SemEval-2018 Task 1 E-c (English, Arabic, Spanish). On threshold-free ranking metrics, XLM-R-Large matches or exceeds English-only specialist models, tying on AP-micro (0.636) and LRAP (0.804) while surpassing on AUC-micro (0.810 vs. 0.787), while natively supporting all 23 languages. The best base-sized model is publicly available at https://huggingface.co/tabularisai/multilingual-emotion-classification

### [48] [Learning Chain Of Thoughts Prompts for Predicting Entities, Relations, and even Literals on Knowledge Graphs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12651)
*Alkid Baci,Luke Friedrichs,Caglar Demir,N'Dah Jean Kouagou,Axel-Cyrille Ngonga Ngomo*

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional knowledge graph embedding models perform well on standard link prediction but cannot naturally handle unseen entities, relations, or rich literals, which is problematic for dynamic and heterogeneous graphs. In contrast, large language models show strong generalization via prompting. The authors are motivated to leverage LLMs’ prompt-based reasoning to overcome KGE limitations and to handle more complex reasoning tasks such as OWL instance retrieval with complex class expressions.

Method: The authors reformulate link prediction as a prompt learning problem. They propose RALP, which learns string-based chain-of-thought prompts that act as scoring functions for knowledge graph triples. Using the MIPRO algorithm for Bayesian Optimization, RALP searches over candidate prompts without gradient access, using fewer than 30 training examples. At inference time, the learned prompts are used with an LLM to predict missing entities, relations, or full triples, and to assign confidence scores. The approach is evaluated on multiple benchmarks including transductive KGE datasets, numerical KGs, and OWL instance retrieval tasks.

Result: RALP improves mean reciprocal rank (MRR) by more than 5% over state-of-the-art KGE models across various datasets. It also yields high-quality inferred triples that enhance generalization, and on OWL reasoning tasks with complex class expressions (e.g., existential and cardinality restrictions), it achieves over 88% Jaccard similarity between predicted and true instance sets.

Conclusion: Prompt-based reasoning with LLMs, instantiated in RALP, is a flexible and effective alternative to traditional embedding-based knowledge graph models. By learning chain-of-thought prompts via Bayesian optimization with minimal training data, RALP addresses generalization to unseen entities, relations, and literals and performs strongly on both link prediction and OWL instance retrieval tasks. The authors position this as evidence that LLM prompting can complement or surpass embedding-based methods for knowledge graph reasoning and release their code and pipeline as open source.

Abstract: Knowledge graph embedding (KGE) models perform well on link prediction but struggle with unseen entities, relations, and especially literals, limiting their use in dynamic, heterogeneous graphs. In contrast, pretrained large language models (LLMs) generalize effectively through prompting. We reformulate link prediction as a prompt learning problem and introduce RALP, which learns string-based chain-of-thought (CoT) prompts as scoring functions for triples. Using Bayesian Optimization through MIPRO algorithm, RALP identifies effective prompts from fewer than 30 training examples without gradient access. At inference, RALP predicts missing entities, relations or whole triples and assigns confidence scores based on the learned prompt. We evaluate on transductive, numerical, and OWL instance retrieval benchmarks. RALP improves state-of-the-art KGE models by over 5% MRR across datasets and enhances generalization via high-quality inferred triples. On OWL reasoning tasks with complex class expressions (e.g., $\exists hasChild.Female$, $\geq 5 \; hasChild.Female$), it achieves over 88% Jaccard similarity. These results highlight prompt-based LLM reasoning as a flexible alternative to embedding-based methods. We release our implementation, training, and evaluation pipeline as open source: https://github.com/dice-group/RALP .

### [49] [InsightFlow: LLM-Driven Synthesis of Patient Narratives for Mental Health into Causal Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12721)
*Shreya Gupta,Prottay Kumar Adhikary,Bhavyaa Dave,Salam Michael Singh,Aniket Deroy,Tanmoy Chakraborty*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces InsightFlow, an LLM-based system that automatically builds 5P-style clinical case formulation causal graphs from psychotherapy transcripts and shows these are broadly comparable to expert-created graphs in structure, content, and perceived usefulness.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Manual construction of clinical case formulations (often structured with the 5P model: presenting, predisposing, precipitating, perpetuating, and protective factors) from therapy transcripts is slow, labor intensive, and subject to substantial variability between clinicians. There is a need for scalable, consistent tools that can extract and organize clinically relevant information into causal models to support and augment clinicians rather than fully replace them.

Method: The authors develop InsightFlow, which uses a large language model to convert patient-therapist intake session transcripts into 5P-aligned causal graphs. They evaluate InsightFlow on 46 psychotherapy intake transcripts annotated by clinical experts. LLM-generated graphs are compared with human expert graphs using three complementary approaches: (1) structural similarity using NetSimile to quantify graph topology resemblance, (2) semantic similarity via embedding-based measures of node and edge content, and (3) blinded expert ratings of clinical qualities such as completeness, internal consistency, and clinical usefulness.

Result: LLM-generated causal graphs show structural similarity to human expert graphs at a level comparable to inter-annotator agreement between different human clinicians. Semantic similarity between LLM and human graphs is high, indicating that the LLM captures much of the same conceptual content. Expert raters judge the LLM outputs as moderately complete, internally consistent, and clinically useful. A key qualitative difference is that LLM graphs are more densely interconnected, whereas human graphs often have more linear, chain-like structures; nevertheless, the overall complexity and coverage are similar.

Conclusion: InsightFlow demonstrates that LLMs can automatically produce 5P-aligned clinical case formulation graphs that fall within the normal range of variability seen across expert clinicians, suggesting real potential to augment clinical workflows. The system can generate clinically meaningful causal models from raw dialogues, though improvements in temporal reasoning and reduction of redundant graph elements are needed. The work points toward automated causal modeling as a promising assistive tool for mental health practitioners rather than a replacement for expert judgment.

Abstract: Clinical case formulation organizes patient symptoms and psychosocial factors into causal models, often using the 5P framework. However, constructing such graphs from therapy transcripts is time consuming and varies across clinicians. We present InsightFlow, an LLM based approach that automatically generates 5P aligned causal graphs from patient-therapist dialogues. Using 46 psychotherapy intake transcripts annotated by clinical experts, we evaluate LLM generated graphs against human formulations using structural (NetSimile), semantic (embedding similarity), and expert rated clinical criteria. The generated graphs show structural similarity comparable to inter annotator agreement and high semantic alignment with human graphs. Expert evaluations rate the outputs as moderately complete, consistent, and clinically useful. While LLM graphs tend to form more interconnected structures compared to the chain like patterns of human graphs, overall complexity and content coverage are similar. These results suggest that LLMs can produce clinically meaningful case formulation graphs within the natural variability of expert practice. InsightFlow highlights the potential of automated causal modeling to augment clinical workflows, with future work needed to improve temporal reasoning and reduce redundancy.

### [50] [Token-Level Policy Optimization: Linking Group-Level Rewards to Token-Level Aggregation via Sequence-Level Likelihood](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12736)
*Xingyu Lin,Yilin Wen,Du Su,Jinchang Hou,En Wang,Wenbin Liu,Chenfu Bao,Zhonghou Lv*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes TEPO, a token-level policy optimization framework that improves large language models’ chain-of-thought mathematical reasoning by better handling sparse, token-level rewards, achieving higher accuracy and faster, more stable training than GRPO/DAPO.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing methods like GRPO improve LLM reasoning but still struggle with sparse rewards at the token level, which is typical in chain-of-thought reasoning where only some tokens meaningfully affect the final reward. Standard entropy regularization is applied uniformly across tokens, often causing entropy collapse or degraded performance when rewards are sparse. There is a need for a method that can more precisely connect sequence-level rewards to token-level updates and stabilize learning under sparse rewards.

Method: The paper introduces TEPO, a token-level policy optimization framework with two core ideas: (1) it uses sequence-level likelihoods to distribute group-level (sequence) rewards down to individual tokens via a token-level aggregation scheme, thereby linking sparse, sequence-level feedback to each token’s update; and (2) it applies a masked token-level KL-divergence constraint that only targets tokens with positive advantage and decreasing entropy, preventing overly aggressive updates that could collapse entropy and destabilize training. This effectively yields a more selective, token-aware regularization compared to undifferentiated entropy penalties in GRPO-like methods.

Result: Experimental evaluations show that TEPO achieves state-of-the-art performance on mathematical reasoning benchmarks compared with prior methods such as GRPO and DAPO. It notably improves training stability and reduces convergence time by about 50% relative to GRPO/DAPO, while sustaining or improving reasoning accuracy.

Conclusion: By redistributing sequence-level rewards to tokens and constraining only the most critical token updates via a KL-divergence mask, TEPO provides a more stable and efficient way to optimize LLM policies under token-level sparse rewards. This leads to better mathematical reasoning performance and faster, more stable training than existing GRPO-style approaches, indicating that fine-grained token-level control is a promising direction for improving chain-of-thought reasoning in LLMs.

Abstract: Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO) has significantly advanced the reasoning ability of large language models (LLMs), particularly in their mathemat ical reasoning performance. However, GRPO and related entropy regularization methods still struggle with token-level sparse-rewards, which is an inherent chal lenge in chain-of-thought (CoT) reasoning. These approaches often rely on undifferen tiated token-level entropy regularization, which easily leads to entropy collapse or model degradation under sparse token rewards. In this work, we propose TEPO, a novel token-level framework that (1) leverages sequence-level likelihood to link group-level rewards with individual tokens via token-level aggregation, and (2) introduces a token-level KL-Divergence mask constraint that targets tokens with positive advantages and decreasing entropy to mitigate abrupt policy updates. Experiments demonstrate that TEPO not only achieves state-of-the-art performance on mathematical reasoning benchmarks but also markedly enhances training stability, reducing convergence time by 50% compared with GRPO/DAPO.

</details>

The authors aim to overcome the limitations of static, one-shot retrieval and make RAG better at handling long documents and multi-hop reasoning.

Method: They first reorganize source documents into a hierarchical knowledge structure that encodes semantic relationships from coarse topics to fine details. On top of this, they deploy an LLM agent that actively navigates the hierarchy: iteratively identifying information gaps in its current understanding, then selectively retrieving from the appropriate granularity level. The process is dynamic and multi-step, enabling multi-granular evidence localization and retrieval planning. They evaluate on long-document QA benchmarks and conduct ablations.

Result: Across long-document QA benchmarks, NaviRAG achieves consistently higher retrieval recall and better end-to-end answer accuracy than standard flat RAG baselines. Ablation studies show that the gains are specifically due to multi-granular evidence localization and dynamic retrieval planning, rather than incidental factors.

Conclusion: Transforming RAG from passive, flat segment retrieval into active navigation over a hierarchical knowledge structure makes systems more capable on complex, long-context QA. NaviRAG demonstrates that LLM agents can plan retrieval across granularities, improving both retrieval and answer quality. The authors also analyze efficiency and usage scenarios, and suggest this direction can lead to more intelligent, autonomous RAG systems.

Abstract: Retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) typically relies on a flat retrieval paradigm that maps queries directly to static, isolated text segments. This approach struggles with more complex tasks that require the conditional retrieval and dynamic synthesis of information across different levels of granularity (e.g., from broad concepts to specific evidence). To bridge this gap, we introduce NaviRAG, a novel framework that shifts from passive segment retrieval to active knowledge navigation. NaviRAG first structures the knowledge documents into a hierarchical form, preserving semantic relationships from coarse-grained topics to fine-grained details. Leveraging this reorganized knowledge records, a large language model (LLM) agent actively navigates the records, iteratively identifying information gaps and retrieving relevant content from the most appropriate granularity level. Extensive experiments on long-document QA benchmarks show that NaviRAG consistently improves both retrieval recall and end-to-end answer performance over conventional RAG baselines. Ablation studies confirm performance gains stem from our method's capacity for multi-granular evidence localization and dynamic retrieval planning. We further discuss efficiency, applicable scenario, and future directions of our method, hoping to make RAG systems more intelligent and autonomous.
