### [54] [Teaching LLMs Human-Like Editing of Inappropriate Argumentation via Reinforcement Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12770)
*Timon Ziegenbein,Maja Stahl,Henning Wachsmuth*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They train LLMs via reinforcement learning to make human-like, localized, meaning-preserving edits that improve argument appropriateness.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LLM editing behavior differs from humans: models scatter many edits across a text and often alter meaning, whereas humans prefer localized, self-contained edits that preserve meaning while improving style or appropriateness. For applications like polishing arguments for discussion, we need LLMs to behave more like human editors—offering discrete, independently acceptable edits that improve appropriateness without unnecessary rewriting.

Method: They design a reinforcement learning framework for editing, where the model proposes sentence-level edits that are self-contained and can be independently accepted or rejected. Training uses Group Relative Policy Optimization (a variant of policy gradient RL) with a multi-component reward: (1) semantic similarity between original and edited text to maintain meaning, (2) fluency of the edited sentence, (3) conformity to human editing patterns (self-contained, localized edits), and (4) argument-level appropriateness improvements. The RL objective jointly optimizes all components to shape model behavior toward human-like editing strategies.

Result: The RL-trained model generates sentence-level edits that are more human-like—localized, self-contained, and meaning-preserving—than strong baselines and prior state-of-the-art editing systems. Both automatic metrics and human judgments show better semantic preservation, fluency, and pattern conformity, while still improving argument appropriateness. Using several rounds of these localized edits, the overall appropriateness of the argument approaches that of fully rewritten versions.

Conclusion: Reinforcement learning with a structured, multi-component reward can successfully train LLMs to adopt human-like editing strategies. By focusing on self-contained sentence-level edits and jointly optimizing for semantics, fluency, pattern conformity, and appropriateness, the approach yields editable suggestions that users can mix-and-match while still achieving substantial improvements in argument quality—approaching the benefits of full rewriting but with greater control and meaning preservation.

Abstract: Editing human-written text has become a standard use case of large language models (LLMs), for example, to make one's arguments more appropriate for a discussion. Comparing human to LLM-generated edits, however, we observe a mismatch in editing strategies: While LLMs often perform multiple scattered edits and tend to change meaning notably, humans rather encapsulate dependent changes in self-contained, meaning-preserving edits. In this paper, we present a reinforcement learning approach that teaches LLMs human-like editing to improve the appropriateness of arguments. Our approach produces self-contained sentence-level edit suggestions that can be accepted or rejected independently. We train the approach using group relative policy optimization with a multi-component reward function that jointly optimizes edit-level semantic similarity, fluency, and pattern conformity as well as argument-level appropriateness. In automatic and human evaluation, it outperforms competitive baselines and the state of the art in human-like editing, with multi-round editing achieving appropriateness close to full rewriting.

### [55] [EvoSpark: Endogenous Interactive Agent Societies for Unified Long-Horizon Narrative Evolution](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12776)

Method: The authors design EvoSpark, which consists of three main components: (1) Stratified Narrative Memory with a Role Socio-Evolutionary Base that treats agent cognition as a living, metabolizing process to reconcile conflicting historical states; (2) a Generative Mise-en-Scène mechanism that enforces consistency between roles, locations, and plot so that character presence matches the narrative; and (3) a Unified Narrative Operation Engine with an Emergent Character Grounding Protocol that converts random generative 'sparks' into persistent, grounded characters in the simulation.

Result: Through experiments on various narrative and multi-agent simulation benchmarks, EvoSpark outperforms existing baselines in maintaining long-term coherence, consistency of character relationships, and alignment between spatial settings and plot events, producing richer and more stable narrative trajectories.

Conclusion: EvoSpark provides a practical substrate for turning a minimal narrative premise into an open-ended, self-evolving story world, mitigating key failure modes of LLM-based multi-agent simulations and enabling more expressive and coherent long-horizon narrative experiences.

Abstract: Realizing endogenous narrative evolution in LLM-based multi-agent systems is hindered by the inherent stochasticity of generative emergence. In particular, long-horizon simulations suffer from social memory stacking, where conflicting relational states accumulate without resolution, and narrative-spatial dissonance, where spatial logic detaches from the evolving plot. To bridge this gap, we propose EvoSpark, a framework specifically designed to sustain logically coherent long-horizon narratives within Endogenous Interactive Agent Societies. To ensure consistency, the Stratified Narrative Memory employs a Role Socio-Evolutionary Base as living cognition, dynamically metabolizing experiences to resolve historical conflicts. Complementarily, Generative Mise-en-Scène mechanism enforces Role-Location-Plot alignment, synchronizing character presence with the narrative flow. Underpinning these is the Unified Narrative Operation Engine, which integrates an Emergent Character Grounding Protocol to transform stochastic sparking into persistent characters. This engine establishes a substrate that expands a minimal premise into an open-ended, evolving story world. Experiments demonstrate that EvoSpark significantly outperforms baselines across diverse paradigms, enabling the sustained generation of expressive and coherent narrative experiences.

### [56] [The role of System 1 and System 2 semantic memory structure in human and LLM biases](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12816)
*Katherine Abramski,Giulio Rossetti,Massimo Stella*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper models human and LLM cognition as two types of semantic memory networks (System 1 and System 2) to study how their structures relate to implicit gender bias, finding that only human semantic structures are irreducible and systematically linked to reduced bias in System 2, revealing a key difference between human and machine cognition.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Implicit biases in humans and LLMs can cause societal harm, and dual process theories claim that fast, associative System 1 processes foster bias while slower, deliberative System 2 processes can reduce it. However, we lack mechanistic explanations and direct structural comparisons between humans and LLMs. The authors aim to formally model and compare the semantic memory structures underlying these two systems, to understand how they relate to implicit gender bias in humans versus LLMs and to test whether LLMs exhibit similar dual-process-like structures.

Method: The authors operationalize System 1 and System 2 thinking as two types of semantic memory networks, each constructed from behaviorally comparable datasets collected from humans and from LLM outputs. They then build semantic networks for each “system” and agent type (human vs LLM) and apply network-based evaluation metrics to quantify properties such as irreducibility and structural patterns. They link these structural properties to measures of implicit gender bias, examining whether particular network characteristics (especially in the System 2-like networks) predict lower bias and whether these relations generalize from humans to LLMs.

Result: They find that semantic memory structures exhibit irreducibility only in humans; LLM-based networks do not display the same structural property, suggesting that LLMs lack specific forms of human-like conceptual organization. Additionally, the relationship between semantic network structure and implicit gender bias appears consistent only in humans: human System 2-like networks show structural signatures associated with lower implicit bias. In contrast, comparable structures in LLM networks do not show such a reliable link between structure and bias.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that the structural organization of semantic memory plays a key role in how humans regulate implicit bias, especially through System 2-like conceptual knowledge, but that analogous mechanisms are absent or qualitatively different in current LLMs. This indicates that human and machine cognition differ in fundamental ways: while humans use certain forms of conceptual knowledge to mitigate bias, LLMs do not exhibit the same structural underpinnings, limiting their ability to emulate human-like bias regulation. The findings call for rethinking how we design and evaluate LLMs with respect to implicit bias and cognitive architectures.

Abstract: Implicit biases in both humans and large language models (LLMs) pose significant societal risks. Dual process theories propose that biases arise primarily from associative System 1 thinking, while deliberative System 2 thinking mitigates bias, but the cognitive mechanisms that give rise to this phenomenon remain poorly understood. To better understand what underlies this duality in humans, and possibly in LLMs, we model System 1 and System 2 thinking as semantic memory networks with distinct structures, built from comparable datasets generated by both humans and LLMs. We then investigate how these distinct semantic memory structures relate to implicit gender bias using network-based evaluation metrics. We find that semantic memory structures are irreducible only in humans, suggesting that LLMs lack certain types of human-like conceptual knowledge. Moreover, semantic memory structure relates consistently to implicit bias only in humans, with lower levels of bias in System~2 structures. These findings suggest that certain types of conceptual knowledge contribute to bias regulation in humans, but not in LLMs, highlighting fundamental differences between human and machine cognition.

### [57] [Growing Pains: Extensible and Efficient LLM Benchmarking Via Fixed Parameter Calibration](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12843)
*Eliya Habba,Itay Itzhak,Asaf Yehudai,Yotam Perlitz,Elron Bandel,Michal Shmueli-Scheuer,Leshem Choshen,Gabriel Stanovsky*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose an IRT-based framework that uses a small set of anchor questions to keep evaluation scores comparable over time as new benchmarks and models are added.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Evaluating every new language model on every benchmark is expensive and often infeasible, and different studies use different subsets or samples of datasets, making model scores hard to compare fairly across time and across papers. The authors want a principled way to ensure score comparability as benchmarks and models evolve, without exploding evaluation cost.

Method: They use multidimensional Item Response Theory (IRT) to model benchmark items and model abilities. For each dataset, they define a fixed set of anchor items whose IRT parameters, once calibrated, are held fixed. When a new benchmark is added or a new model is evaluated, the model is tested only on the anchor items of each dataset, and IRT is used to infer its performance on the full datasets. This creates a shared latent scale across datasets and time, enabling direct comparison of model scores even though complete evaluations are not run each time.

Result: On a corpus of over 400 language models, evaluating each model on only 100 anchor questions per dataset allows them to predict the model’s full-benchmark performance within 2–3 percentage points, while preserving rank order well (Spearman ρ ≥ 0.9). This demonstrates both high accuracy and robust ranking consistency using only partial evaluations.

Conclusion: A multidimensional IRT framework with fixed anchor items can support a growing, evolving benchmark suite for language models, allowing new datasets to be added and new models to be evaluated at constant marginal cost, while preserving comparability of scores and rankings across time and across studies. This makes large-scale, long-term evaluation more scalable and principled.

Abstract: The rapid release of both language models and benchmarks makes it increasingly costly to evaluate every model on every dataset. In practice, models are often evaluated on different samples, making scores difficult to compare across studies. To address this, we propose a framework based on multidimensional Item Response Theory (IRT) that uses anchor items to calibrate new benchmarks to the evaluation suite while holding previously calibrated item parameters fixed. Our approach supports a realistic evaluation setting in which datasets are introduced over time and models are evaluated only on the datasets available at the time of evaluation, while a fixed anchor set for each dataset is used so that results from different evaluation periods can be compared directly. In large-scale experiments on more than $400$ models, our framework predicts full-evaluation performance within 2-3 percentage points using only $100$ anchor questions per dataset, with Spearman $ρ\geq 0.9$ for ranking preservation, showing that it is possible to extend benchmark suites over time while preserving score comparability, at a constant evaluation cost per new dataset. Code available at https://github.com/eliyahabba/growing-pains

</details>


### [58] [Round-Trip Translation Reveals What Frontier Multilingual Benchmarks Miss](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12911)
*Ronald Skorobogat,Ameya Prabhu,Matthias Bethge*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper argues that current multilingual benchmarks for large models mostly test math reasoning and factual recall rather than true multilingual ability, and proposes round‑trip translation plus a new LiT benchmark as a better way to evaluate multilingual generation quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multilingual benchmarks are central to assessing and guiding the development of frontier language models, but existing multilingual evaluations are usually just translations of reasoning and knowledge benchmarks. As a result, they may not reflect real multilingual proficiency or performance on user-facing multilingual tasks like LMArena. The authors are motivated to find an evaluation method that better matches real-world multilingual use and does not depend on high-quality reference translations or stronger multilingual judges.

Method: 1) Empirically compare the performance of different model variants (e.g., thinking vs. instruct) on standard multilingual benchmarks and on real-world tasks (LMArena) to show a mismatch. 2) Propose a new evaluation method based on round-trip translation: take input text in a source language, translate it into a target language and then back into the source, and measure semantic differences between the original and the round-tripped text. 3) Quantify the correlation between this round-trip metric and human/user ratings on LMArena. 4) Construct and release the Lost in Translation (LiT) benchmark, a curated set of round-trip translation tasks covering many widely spoken languages for evaluating frontier models.

Result: They find that popular multilingual benchmarks primarily measure mathematical reasoning and factual recall rather than genuine multilingual capability. Thinking-style model variants score higher on these benchmarks but can perform worse on real multilingual user tasks. Their round-trip translation metric shows a very high correlation (Spearman’s rho = 0.94) with user ratings on LMArena, demonstrating that it is a strong proxy for real-world multilingual performance without requiring reference translations or stronger external judges. They also create the LiT benchmark as a challenging test suite across many global languages.

Conclusion: Standard multilingual benchmarks for frontier models are misaligned with real-world multilingual needs because they focus on reasoning and factual recall. Round-trip translation is an effective, low-cost, reference-free way to evaluate multilingual generative ability and aligns closely with human judgments. The LiT benchmark operationalizes this idea, offering a more realistic and challenging evaluation framework for multilingual frontier models.

Abstract: Multilingual benchmarks guide the development of frontier models. Yet multilingual evaluations reported by frontier models are structured similar to popular reasoning and knowledge benchmarks, but across many languages. We show such benchmarks, and consequently multilingual evaluations, measure mathematical reasoning and factual recall, not multilingual proficiency. For example, thinking variants dramatically outperform instruct variants on these benchmarks, yet often perform worse on real-world multilingual tasks, such as LMArena. We propose a simple alternative: evaluate multilingual capability via round-trip translation. Given text in a source language, translate it to a target language and back; semantic gaps between the original and result expose failures in multilingual generation capabilities. Round-trip translation correlates almost perfectly (\r{ho} = 0.94) with user ratings on LMArena with our benchmark, requires no human reference translations, and does not require a more capable multilingual judge than tested models. Lastly, we introduce Lost in Translation (LiT), a challenging round-trip translation benchmark spanning widely spoken languages worldwide, for realistic evaluation of multilingual frontier models.

### [59] [MetFuse: Figurative Fusion between Metonymy and Metaphor](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12919)
*Saptarshi Ghosh,Tianyu Jiang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They build a dataset and framework to systematically generate and study sentences that mix metonymy and metaphor, and show that training on this data improves figurative language classification and reveals interaction effects between the two tropes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Metonymy and metaphor frequently appear together in natural language, but most computational research treats them separately. There has been no dedicated resource to study how they interact in the same sentence or how their co-occurrence affects automatic identification. The authors aim to fill this gap with a controlled dataset and analysis framework that captures pure and mixed (hybrid) figurative expressions, enabling better models and deeper understanding of figurative fusion.

Method: They design a transformation framework that starts from a literal sentence and constructs three aligned figurative variants: a metonymic version, a metaphoric version, and a hybrid sentence containing both. Applying this framework, they build MetFuse, a dataset of 1,000 human-verified quadruplets (one literal + three figurative variants) totaling 4,000 sentences, ensuring meaning alignment between variants. They then conduct extrinsic experiments by augmenting existing metonymy and metaphor classification benchmarks with MetFuse data, comparing performance with and without augmentation, and analyzing how the presence of one figurative type affects detection of the other for both humans and large language models.

Result: Training data augmentation with MetFuse improves performance on eight existing metonymy and metaphor classification benchmarks. Hybrid (metonymy+metaphor) examples give the largest performance gains on metonymy classification tasks. Both human annotators and large language models more accurately identify metonymy in hybrid sentences than in sentences containing only metonymy, indicating a facilitative interaction between metaphor and metonymy in recognition tasks.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that metonymy and metaphor should not be modeled in isolation: their co-occurrence provides useful signals that can improve automatic classification, especially for metonymy. The MetFuse dataset and transformation framework offer a novel, controlled way to study figurative fusion and show that metaphor can make metonymic nouns more salient to both humans and language models. They release MetFuse publicly to support further research on interacting figurative phenomena.

Abstract: Metonymy and metaphor often co-occur in natural language, yet computational work has studied them largely in isolation. We introduce a framework that transforms a literal sentence into three figurative variants: metonymic, metaphoric, and hybrid. Using this framework, we construct MetFuse, the first dedicated dataset of figurative fusion between metonymy and metaphor, containing 1,000 human-verified meaning-aligned quadruplets totaling 4,000 sentences. Extrinsic experiments on eight existing benchmarks show that augmenting training data with MetFuse consistently improves both metonymy and metaphor classification, with hybrid examples yielding the largest gains on metonymy tasks. Using this dataset, we also analyze how the presence of one figurative type impacts another. Our findings show that both human annotators and large language models better identify metonymy in hybrid sentences than in metonymy-only sentences, demonstrating that the presence of a metaphor makes a metonymic noun more explicit. Our dataset is publicly available at: https://github.com/cincynlp/MetFuse.

### [60] [MoshiRAG: Asynchronous Knowledge Retrieval for Full-Duplex Speech Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12928)
*Chung-Ming Chien,Manu Orsini,Eugene Kharitonov,Neil Zeghidour,Karen Livescu,Alexandre Défossez*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents MoshiRAG, a modular speech-to-speech system that combines a lightweight full-duplex conversational interface with selective retrieval from stronger knowledge sources to improve factuality without sacrificing real-time interactivity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Full-duplex speech-to-speech language models enable natural, real-time conversational behavior (handling pauses, interruptions, backchannels), but they still struggle with factual accuracy. Simply scaling model size could improve factuality, yet this would make real-time inference too expensive and undermine the practical benefits of full-duplex systems. The authors are motivated to find a way to significantly improve factual correctness while keeping latency and computational cost low, and to make such systems extensible to new domains and tasks.

Method: They propose MoshiRAG, a modular and asynchronous framework that separates the interactive front-end from heavy knowledge processing. A compact full-duplex speech interface handles the real-time conversation, while a retrieval component is selectively activated only for knowledge-demanding queries. During the natural temporal gap between the beginning of the model’s spoken response and the point at which substantive content must be delivered, the system performs retrieval from more powerful knowledge sources. The architecture supports plug-and-play retrieval methods and does not require retraining the core conversational model to incorporate new retrieval strategies.

Result: MoshiRAG achieves factuality on par with the best publicly released non-duplex speech language models, despite using a compact full-duplex interface. It preserves the interactive features of full-duplex systems, such as low-latency responses and smooth conversational flow. Additionally, the system shows strong performance on out-of-domain mathematical reasoning tasks, demonstrating the effectiveness and generality of the retrieval-based grounding strategy.

Conclusion: The work shows that factuality in full-duplex speech-to-speech models can be substantially improved without resorting to large, expensive end-to-end models. By exploiting modular design and the inherent timing structure of spoken dialogue, MoshiRAG can maintain real-time, natural interaction while grounding responses in external knowledge. The framework’s plug-and-play retrieval design offers a flexible path to extend such systems to new domains and tasks without retraining the core model.

Abstract: Speech-to-speech language models have recently emerged to enhance the naturalness of conversational AI. In particular, full-duplex models are distinguished by their real-time interactivity, including handling of pauses, interruptions, and backchannels. However, improving their factuality remains an open challenge. While scaling the model size could address this gap, it would make real-time inference prohibitively expensive. In this work, we propose MoshiRAG, a modular approach that combines a compact full-duplex interface with selective retrieval to access more powerful knowledge sources. Our asynchronous framework enables the model to identify knowledge-demanding queries and ground its responses in external information. By leveraging the natural temporal gap between response onset and the delivery of core information, the retrieval process can be completed while maintaining a natural conversation flow. With this approach, MoshiRAG achieves factuality comparable to the best publicly released non-duplex speech language models while preserving the interactivity inherent to full-duplex systems. Moreover, our flexible design supports plug-and-play retrieval methods without retraining and demonstrates strong performance on out-of-domain mathematical reasoning tasks.

</details>


### [61] [GlotOCR Bench: OCR Models Still Struggle Beyond a Handful of Unicode Scripts](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12978)
*Amir Hossein Kargaran,Nafiseh Nikeghbal,Jana Diesner,François Yvon,Hinrich Schütze*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: GlotOCR Bench is a new benchmark to test how well OCR systems generalize to over 100 writing scripts, revealing that current vision-language models perform well on only a small subset and struggle badly with unfamiliar scripts.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most OCR research and evaluation focus on a small number of high- and mid-resource scripts (like Latin, Cyrillic, etc.), leaving the performance on many of the world’s writing systems unknown. As vision-language models become the dominant OCR approach, we need a rigorous, large-scale way to measure how well they generalize across diverse scripts and conditions, instead of assuming their strong performance on a few scripts holds universally.

Method: The authors build GlotOCR Bench, a benchmark with clean and degraded OCR images spanning 100+ Unicode scripts. They render text from real multilingual corpora using fonts from Google Fonts, with proper shaping by HarfBuzz and rasterization via FreeType, covering both left-to-right and right-to-left scripts. They manually inspect samples for each script to ensure correct rendering. Then they evaluate a wide range of open-weight and proprietary vision-language models on this benchmark to measure script-wise OCR performance and generalization, and relate this to pretraining coverage of scripts in each model.

Result: Most tested models achieve strong OCR performance on fewer than 10 scripts, and even the best large frontier models only generalize reasonably to around 30 scripts. Performance across scripts correlates strongly with whether those scripts were included in the models’ language pretraining data. When presented with unfamiliar scripts, models often emit nonsensical strings or hallucinated characters drawn from visually or linguistically similar scripts they already handle.

Conclusion: OCR capabilities of current vision-language models are far from universal: they are heavily biased toward scripts well-covered in pretraining data and generalize poorly to the majority of the world’s writing systems. Effective OCR in low-resource scripts will require better pretraining coverage, improved visual modeling, or more explicit cross-script generalization strategies. GlotOCR Bench and its open pipeline provide a standardized, reproducible way to track progress on truly multilingual OCR.

Abstract: Optical character recognition (OCR) has advanced rapidly with the rise of vision-language models, yet evaluation has remained concentrated on a small cluster of high- and mid-resource scripts. We introduce GlotOCR Bench, a comprehensive benchmark evaluating OCR generalization across 100+ Unicode scripts. Our benchmark comprises clean and degraded image variants rendered from real multilingual texts. Images are rendered using fonts from the Google Fonts repository, shaped with HarfBuzz and rasterized with FreeType, supporting both LTR and RTL scripts. Samples of rendered images were manually reviewed to verify correct rendering across all scripts. We evaluate a broad suite of open-weight and proprietary vision-language models and find that most perform well on fewer than ten scripts, and even the strongest frontier models fail to generalize beyond thirty scripts. Performance broadly tracks script-level pretraining coverage, suggesting that current OCR systems rely on language model pretraining as much as on visual recognition. Models confronted with unfamiliar scripts either produce random noise or hallucinate characters from similar scripts they already know. We release the benchmark and pipeline for reproducibility. Pipeline Code: https://github.com/cisnlp/glotocr-bench, Benchmark: https://hf.co/datasets/cis-lmu/glotocr-bench.

### [62] [Accelerating Speculative Decoding with Block Diffusion Draft Trees](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12989)
*Liran Ringel,Yaniv Romano*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes DDTree, a diffusion-based draft tree method that improves speculative decoding efficiency for autoregressive language models by exploring multiple draft continuations in parallel within one verification pass.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Speculative decoding speeds up large language models by using a smaller drafter model to propose several future tokens that the larger target model then verifies in parallel. Existing strong drafters like DFlash can generate whole blocks of tokens in one pass but still verify only a single drafted sequence per round, which limits how many tokens can be accepted and thus caps the speedup. The paper aims to overcome this limitation by enabling multi-branch drafting without increasing target model passes.

Method: Building on DFlash’s block diffusion drafter, the authors construct a draft tree (DDTree) from the per-position output distributions of the diffusion drafter. Under a fixed budget of draft nodes (candidate tokens across positions), they use a best-first search implemented with a heap to pick the most promising continuations according to a surrogate score derived from the drafter’s probabilities. These candidates form a tree of possible token trajectories. The target model then verifies the entire tree in a single forward pass by using an ancestor-only attention mask so that each node only attends to its lineage, enabling parallel verification of many branches.

Result: DDTree achieves higher acceptance lengths and better speculative decoding efficiency than previous methods under comparable computational budgets. It outperforms strong autoregressive drafters such as EAGLE-3 when combined with DFlash, and matches or surpasses state-of-the-art speculative decoding performance, indicating that tree-structured drafting provides substantial practical speedups.

Conclusion: By turning the block diffusion drafter’s per-position distributions into a draft tree and verifying all branches simultaneously with an ancestor-only attention mask, DDTree significantly improves the effectiveness of speculative decoding over single-trajectory approaches. Because it is built on DFlash, one of the best existing drafters, DDTree stands among the leading methods for accelerating autoregressive language models with speculative decoding.

Abstract: Speculative decoding accelerates autoregressive language models by using a lightweight drafter to propose multiple future tokens, which the target model then verifies in parallel. DFlash shows that a block diffusion drafter can generate an entire draft block in a single forward pass and achieve state-of-the-art speculative decoding performance, outperforming strong autoregressive drafters such as EAGLE-3. Vanilla DFlash, however, still verifies only a single drafted trajectory per round, potentially limiting its acceptance length. We introduce DDTree (Diffusion Draft Tree), a method that constructs a draft tree directly from the per-position distributions of a block diffusion drafter. Under a fixed node budget, DDTree uses a simple best-first heap algorithm to select the continuations that are most likely to match the target model according to a surrogate defined by the draft model's output. The resulting tree is verified efficiently in a single target model forward pass using an ancestor-only attention mask. Because DDTree builds on DFlash, a leading draft model for speculative decoding, these gains place DDTree among the leading approaches to speculative decoding.

</details>


### [63] [PolicyLLM: Towards Excellent Comprehension of Public Policy for Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12995)
*Han Bao,Penghao Zhang,Yue Huang,Zhengqing Yuan,Yanchi Ru,Rui Su,Yujun Zhou,Xiangqi Wang,Kehan Guo,Nitesh V Chawla,Yanfang Ye,Xiangliang Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces PolicyBench, a large-scale benchmark for evaluating how well large language models (LLMs) understand and apply public policy, and proposes PolicyMoE, a specialized mixture-of-experts model that performs particularly well on applied policy tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are increasingly used in real-world decision-making and public policy contexts, but there is little systematic evaluation of their ability to comprehend, reason about, and apply policy-related information. Existing benchmarks do not capture the breadth, depth, and cognitive levels (from recall to application) needed to assess policy competence, especially across different governance systems such as the US and China. This gap risks deploying models whose policy reasoning capabilities are poorly understood and potentially unreliable.

Method: The authors build PolicyBench, a large-scale benchmark of 21,000 cases spanning diverse policy domains and covering both US and China systems. The tasks are organized according to Bloom’s taxonomy into three cognitive levels: memorization (factual recall of policy rules and knowledge), understanding (conceptual and contextual reasoning about policy content), and application (solving realistic policy scenarios). On top of this benchmark, they design PolicyMoE, a domain-specialized Mixture-of-Experts model where different expert modules are aligned with the three cognitive levels, enabling specialization for memorization, understanding, and application tasks. They then empirically evaluate PolicyMoE and other LLMs on the benchmark, including structured reasoning-style tasks.

Result: PolicyMoE achieves stronger performance on application-oriented policy tasks than on tasks that emphasize memorization or conceptual understanding, and attains the highest accuracy on structured reasoning tasks among the evaluated models. Across models, the results expose systematic weaknesses in policy memorization and deeper conceptual understanding relative to applied, scenario-based reasoning. The cross-system (US-China) design further highlights these limitations across differing policy contexts.

Conclusion: PolicyBench provides a comprehensive framework for evaluating LLMs’ policy-related capabilities along multiple cognitive levels and across major governance systems, revealing that current models, including specialized ones, are still limited in their policy understanding. PolicyMoE shows that expert specialization aligned with cognitive levels can improve performance, particularly for application and structured reasoning. The work underscores that reliable policy-focused LLMs will require targeted architectures and training strategies and that PolicyBench can serve as a foundation for developing and assessing such models.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) are increasingly integrated into real-world decision-making, including in the domain of public policy. Yet, their ability to comprehend and reason about policy-related content remains underexplored. To fill this gap, we present \textbf{\textit{PolicyBench}}, the first large-scale cross-system benchmark (US-China) evaluating policy comprehension, comprising 21K cases across a broad spectrum of policy areas, capturing the diversity and complexity of real-world governance. Following Bloom's taxonomy, the benchmark assesses three core capabilities: (1) \textbf{Memorization}: factual recall of policy knowledge, (2) \textbf{Understanding}: conceptual and contextual reasoning, and (3) \textbf{Application}: problem-solving in real-life policy scenarios. Building on this benchmark, we further propose \textbf{\textit{PolicyMoE}}, a domain-specialized Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) model with expert modules aligned to each cognitive level. The proposed models demonstrate stronger performance on application-oriented policy tasks than on memorization or conceptual understanding, and yields the highest accuracy on structured reasoning tasks. Our results reveal key limitations of current LLMs in policy understanding and suggest paths toward more reliable, policy-focused models.

### [64] [One Token Away from Collapse: The Fragility of Instruction-Tuned Helpfulness](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.13006)
*Erfan Baghaei Potraghloo,Seyedarmin Azizi,Souvik Kundu,Massoud Pedram*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper finds that instruction-tuned LLMs become surprisingly fragile when given very simple lexical constraints, leading to large drops in response quality, unlike their base counterparts.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While instruction-tuned LLMs are designed to follow user constraints and produce helpful responses, it is unclear how robust their helpfulness is when faced with trivial surface-level constraints, such as banning a common word or punctuation. Existing evaluations and prior work mostly focus on more complex or format-level constraints and often use independent LLM-as-judge setups, which may miss subtle failures. The authors aim to understand whether instruction tuning inadvertently couples task performance to rigid surface templates, creating hidden vulnerabilities in constrained generation and exposing flaws in current evaluation methodology.

Method: The authors impose simple lexical constraints (e.g., banning a single punctuation mark or common word) on multiple models: three open-weight instruction-tuned families and one closed-weight model (GPT-4o-mini), and compare constrained vs. unconstrained responses. They perform 1,920 pairwise comparisons rated by GPT-4o-mini and GPT-4o to assess comprehensiveness and preference. They conduct mechanistic analyses: (1) a two-pass generation scheme (unconstrained answer followed by constrained rewriting) to test whether collapse is due to planning vs. surface realization, and (2) linear probing on internal prompt representations to predict response length before generation, comparing instruction-tuned versus base models. They also replicate the effect on MT-Bench across eight task types and contrast independent LLM-as-judge evaluation with pairwise evaluation to reveal methodological gaps.

Result: Simple lexical constraints cause large drops (14–48%) in response comprehensiveness for instruction-tuned models, with the unconstrained baseline preferred in 77–100% of 1,920 pairwise evaluations. GPT-4o-mini loses about 31% comprehensiveness and the baseline wins 99% of comparisons, indicating that even commercial closed-weight models are fragile. Two-pass generation recovers 59–96% of lost response length, implicating a failure in initial planning rather than pure decoding. Linear probes on prompt representations predict response length with high R^2 (0.51–0.93) in instruction-tuned models, and the strength of this predictability correlates with how severely models collapse, whereas base models yield negative R^2 and exhibit no systematic collapse under the same constraints. The effect is robust across all eight categories in MT-Bench. Standard independent LLM-as-judge evaluation detects only a small 3.5% average quality drop, while pairwise comparison reveals a much larger 23% drop.

Conclusion: Instruction tuning, while improving helpful, structured responses, also introduces a hidden fragility: models become over-coupled to narrow surface-form templates, so that even trivial lexical bans can trigger substantial collapse in answer quality. This vulnerability does not exist in base models and manifests as a planning-level failure encoded in internal representations before generation. Two-pass strategies can mitigate the issue, but the findings call into question current practices for constrained generation and highlight that standard independent LLM-as-judge evaluations substantially underreport quality degradation. Better training and evaluation methods are needed to decouple task competence from rigid surface forms and to accurately assess robustness under constraints.

Abstract: Instruction-tuned large language models produce helpful, structured responses, but how robust is this helpfulness when trivially constrained? We show that simple lexical constraints (banning a single punctuation character or common word) cause instruction-tuned LLMs to collapse their responses, losing 14--48% of comprehensiveness in pairwise evaluation across three open-weight model families and one closed-weight model (GPT-4o-mini). The baseline response is preferred in 77--100% of 1,920 pairwise comparisons judged by GPT-4o-mini and GPT-4o. Notably, GPT-4o-mini suffers 31% comprehensiveness loss (99% baseline win rate), demonstrating that the fragility extends to commercially deployed closed-weight models, contrary to prior findings on format-level constraints. Through mechanistic analysis, we identify this as a planning failure: two-pass generation (free generation followed by constrained rewriting) recovers 59--96% of response length, and linear probes on prompt representations predict response length with $R^2 = 0.51$--$0.93$ before generation begins, with $R^2$ tracking collapse severity across models. The same probes yield negative $R^2$ on base models, confirming that instruction tuning creates the representational structure encoding the collapse decision. Crucially, base models show no systematic collapse under identical constraints, with effects that are small, noisy, and bidirectional, demonstrating that instruction tuning creates this fragility by coupling task competence to narrow surface-form templates. The effect replicates on MT-Bench across all eight task categories. We further show that standard independent LLM-as-judge evaluation detects only a 3.5% average quality drop where pairwise evaluation reveals 23%, exposing a methodological blind spot in how constrained generation is assessed.

</details>


### [65] [Toward Autonomous Long-Horizon Engineering for ML Research](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.13018)
*Guoxin Chen,Jie Chen,Lei Chen,Jiale Zhao,Fanzhe Meng,Wayne Xin Zhao,Ruihua Song,Cheng Chen,Ji-Rong Wen,Kai Jia*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: AiScientist is an autonomous system for long-horizon ML research engineering that uses hierarchical orchestration plus a file-based shared workspace to coordinate specialized agents, significantly outperforming prior baselines on research benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Autonomous AI agents struggle with long-horizon ML research tasks because they must maintain coherent progress across many stages (understanding the task, setting up the environment, coding, experimenting, debugging) over extended periods. Existing approaches often rely on conversational handoff of context, which is fragile and leads to loss of critical information and coordination failures. The authors aim to solve how to make AI agents reliably execute complex, multi-stage ML research projects as a systems engineering problem rather than only a local reasoning challenge.

Method: The authors design AiScientist, a multi-agent system with two core design principles: hierarchical orchestration and durable state continuity via a File-as-Bus protocol. A top-level Orchestrator agent manages the research process at the stage level, keeping concise summaries and a high-level map of the workspace. Specialized worker agents (e.g., for analysis, planning, coding, experimentation) operate on a permission-scoped file-based workspace, repeatedly re-grounding themselves on persistent artifacts such as written analyses, explicit plans, code files, experiment configs, and logged results. Instead of passing most context through chat messages, agents read and write to this shared file bus, enabling thin conversational control over a rich, persistent project state. The system is evaluated on PaperBench and MLE-Bench Lite, with ablation studies removing the File-as-Bus protocol to test its contribution.

Result: AiScientist outperforms strong baselines on two benchmarks. On PaperBench, it improves the score by an average of 10.54 points over the best matched baseline. On MLE-Bench Lite, it achieves 81.82% Any Medal, indicating a high success rate across tasks. When the File-as-Bus protocol is removed, performance drops notably: PaperBench scores decrease by 6.41 points and MLE-Bench Lite performance drops by 31.82 percentage points. These empirical results highlight the importance of the file-based shared workspace and orchestration design for long-horizon ML research tasks.

Conclusion: Long-horizon ML research engineering should be treated primarily as a systems coordination problem: effectively organizing specialized agents around a durable shared project state, rather than only trying to improve single-step reasoning or dialogue. AiScientist, through hierarchical orchestration and a File-as-Bus workspace, demonstrates that maintaining structured, persistent artifacts and workspace maps enables agents to sustain coherent progress on complex ML research tasks over long time scales and significantly outperform previous autonomous research systems.

Abstract: Autonomous AI research has advanced rapidly, but long-horizon ML research engineering remains difficult: agents must sustain coherent progress across task comprehension, environment setup, implementation, experimentation, and debugging over hours or days. We introduce AiScientist, a system for autonomous long-horizon engineering for ML research built on a simple principle: strong long-horizon performance requires both structured orchestration and durable state continuity. To this end, AiScientist combines hierarchical orchestration with a permission-scoped File-as-Bus workspace: a top-level Orchestrator maintains stage-level control through concise summaries and a workspace map, while specialized agents repeatedly re-ground on durable artifacts such as analyses, plans, code, and experimental evidence rather than relying primarily on conversational handoffs, yielding thin control over thick state. Across two complementary benchmarks, AiScientist improves PaperBench score by 10.54 points on average over the best matched baseline and achieves 81.82 Any Medal% on MLE-Bench Lite. Ablation studies further show that File-as-Bus protocol is a key driver of performance, reducing PaperBench by 6.41 points and MLE-Bench Lite by 31.82 points when removed. These results suggest that long-horizon ML research engineering is a systems problem of coordinating specialized work over durable project state, rather than a purely local reasoning problem.

</details>

### [66] [The Non-Optimality of Scientific Knowledge: Path Dependence, Lock-In, and The Local Minimum Trap](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.11828)
*Mohamed Mabrok*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that scientific knowledge tends to get stuck in local optima shaped by history, cognition, and institutions, and explores how to design science to better escape these traps.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although science is seen as our best tool for discovering truths about nature, people rarely analyze the overall path of scientific progress itself as an optimization process. The authors want to understand why science may systematically favor certain lines of inquiry and frameworks over potentially better alternatives, and how historical, cognitive, and institutional factors might lock us into suboptimal scientific paradigms.

Method: The paper uses an analogy between scientific progress and gradient descent in machine learning, then develops this idea through qualitative, historical case studies from multiple fields (mathematics, physics, chemistry, biology, neuroscience, and statistics). It analyzes these cases to identify and categorize mechanisms of `lock-in` into three types: cognitive, formal, and institutional.

Result: The authors identify and articulate three interlocking mechanisms that can trap science in local optima: (1) cognitive lock-in, where existing concepts and intuitions bias researchers toward familiar frameworks; (2) formal lock-in, where mathematical and representational tools channel which theories are considered feasible; and (3) institutional lock-in, where funding, publishing norms, and career incentives steer research trajectories. They show across diverse case studies how these mechanisms shape which scientific theories are developed and accepted, sometimes at the expense of more accurate but less tractable or less rewarded alternatives.

Conclusion: Scientific progress is not a neutral, globally optimizing search for truth but is constrained by local gradients of tractability, empirical accessibility, and institutional reward. Recognizing the cognitive, formal, and institutional mechanisms of lock-in is crucial for designing `meta-scientific` interventions—changes to incentives, training, methods, and institutions—that increase the chance of escaping local optima and discovering fundamentally better descriptions of nature. This has significant implications for the philosophy of science and for how we organize and evaluate scientific research.

Abstract: Science is widely regarded as humanity's most reliable method for uncovering truths about the natural world. Yet the \emph{trajectory} of scientific discovery is rarely examined as an optimization problem in its own right. This paper argues that the body of scientific knowledge, at any given historical moment, represents a \emph{local optimum} rather than a global one--that the frameworks, formalisms, and paradigms through which we understand nature are substantially shaped by historical contingency, cognitive path dependence, and institutional lock-in. Drawing an analogy to gradient descent in machine learning, we propose that science follows the steepest local gradient of tractability, empirical accessibility, and institutional reward, and in doing so may bypass fundamentally superior descriptions of nature. We develop this thesis through detailed case studies spanning mathematics, physics, chemistry, biology, neuroscience, and statistical methodology. We identify three interlocking mechanisms of lock-in--cognitive, formal, and institutional--and argue that recognizing these mechanisms is a prerequisite for designing meta-scientific strategies capable of escaping local optima. We conclude by proposing concrete interventions and discussing the epistemological implications of our thesis for the philosophy of science.

</details>


### [67] [GoodPoint: Learning Constructive Scientific Paper Feedback from Author Responses](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.11924)
*Jimin Mun,Chani Jung,Xuhui Zhou,Hyunwoo Kim,Maarten Sap*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: This paper proposes GoodPoint, a method to train LLMs to give more valid and actionable feedback on research papers, using a large ICLR review dataset and author responses as success signals.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs could help researchers by providing feedback on their papers, but current models can produce feedback that is vague, invalid, or not practically useful for authors. The authors want to move from generic comments to feedback that genuinely helps improve both the research and its presentation, with clear author-centric measures of effectiveness.

Method: They define feedback effectiveness along two axes: validity (is the feedback correct and well-grounded?) and author action (does it suggest concrete, useful changes?). They curate GoodPoint-ICLR, a dataset of 19K ICLR papers with reviewer comments, where feedback is labeled for these two axes using information from author responses. Using this dataset, they propose GoodPoint, a training recipe that (1) fine-tunes models on feedback judged valid and actionable, and (2) applies preference optimization using both real and synthetic preference pairs derived from success signals in author responses.

Result: On a benchmark of 1.2K ICLR papers, a Qwen3-8B model trained with GoodPoint shows an 83.7% relative improvement in predicted feedback success rate over the base Qwen3-8B. It also achieves state-of-the-art performance among similarly sized LLMs for matching a golden set of human feedback, and even exceeds Gemini-3-flash in precision on this task.

Conclusion: Training LLMs with author-centric success signals leads to feedback that is more valid, actionable, and practically useful for researchers. The GoodPoint recipe and GoodPoint-ICLR dataset enable mid-sized models to reach or surpass the feedback quality of larger, stronger baselines, suggesting that targeted training on real-world author response signals is an effective way to make LLMs better research assistants rather than research automatons.

Abstract: While LLMs hold significant potential to transform scientific research, we advocate for their use to augment and empower researchers rather than to automate research without human oversight. To this end, we study constructive feedback generation, the task of producing targeted, actionable feedback that helps authors improve both their research and its presentation. In this work, we operationalize the effectiveness of feedback along two author-centric axes-validity and author action. We first curate GoodPoint-ICLR, a dataset of 19K ICLR papers with reviewer feedback annotated along both dimensions using author responses. Building on this, we introduce GoodPoint, a training recipe that leverages success signals from author responses through fine-tuning on valid and actionable feedback, together with preference optimization on both real and synthetic preference pairs. Our evaluation on a benchmark of 1.2K ICLR papers shows that a GoodPoint-trained Qwen3-8B improves the predicted success rate by 83.7% over the base model and sets a new state-of-the-art among LLMs of similar size in feedback matching on a golden human feedback set, even surpassing Gemini-3-flash in precision. We further validate these findings through an expert human study, demonstrating that GoodPoint consistently delivers higher practical value as perceived by authors.

Abstract: We introduce ArcDeck, a multi-agent framework that formulates paper-to-slide generation as a structured narrative reconstruction task. Unlike existing methods that directly summarize raw text into slides, ArcDeck explicitly models the source paper's logical flow. These structural priors then guide an iterative multi-agent refinement process, where specialized agents iteratively critique and revise the presentation outline before rendering the final visual layouts and designs. To evaluate our approach, we also introduce ArcBench, a newly curated benchmark of academic paper-slide pairs. ### [69] [The Long-Horizon Task Mirage? Diagnosing Where and Why Agentic Systems Break](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.11978)
*Xinyu Jessica Wang,Haoyue Bai,Yiyou Sun,Haorui Wang,Shuibai Zhang,Wenjie Hu,Mya Schroder,Bilge Mutlu,Dawn Song,Robert D Nowak*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Introduces HORIZON, a benchmark to diagnose and compare long-horizon failures in LLM agents across domains.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM agents work well on short and medium tasks but often fail on long-horizon tasks that require many interdependent steps. These failures are not well-understood or comparable across domains, making it hard to systematically diagnose weaknesses or measure progress in long-horizon reasoning and control.

Method: The authors design HORIZON, a cross-domain diagnostic benchmark that systematically constructs long-horizon tasks and records agent trajectories. They evaluate state-of-the-art agents from several LLM families (GPT-5 variants and Claude models) across four representative agentic domains, collecting over 3100 trajectories. They also build a trajectory-grounded LLM-as-a-Judge evaluation pipeline that labels and attributes failures in a scalable and reproducible way, and they validate this pipeline against human annotations by measuring inter-annotator agreement.

Result: Using HORIZON, they characterize how performance of different SOTA LLM agents degrades as the task horizon grows, identifying patterns of long-horizon failure across multiple domains. Their LLM-as-a-Judge pipeline shows strong agreement with human annotators (κ=0.61 across humans, κ=0.84 human vs. judge), indicating it is reliable for large-scale failure analysis.

Conclusion: HORIZON provides an initial, systematic, cross-domain framework for constructing and analyzing long-horizon tasks for LLM agents. The released benchmark and LLM-as-a-Judge pipeline enable more principled diagnosis of long-horizon failures and offer practical insights for designing more reliable long-horizon agents. A public leaderboard encourages ongoing community contributions and comparisons.

Abstract: Large language model (LLM) agents perform strongly on short- and mid-horizon tasks, but often break down on long-horizon tasks that require extended, interdependent action sequences. Despite rapid progress in agentic systems, these long-horizon failures remain poorly characterized, hindering principled diagnosis and comparison across domains. To address this gap, we introduce HORIZON, an initial cross-domain diagnostic benchmark for systematically constructing tasks and analyzing long-horizon failure behaviors in LLM-based agents. Using HORIZON, we evaluate state-of-the-art (SOTA) agents from multiple model families (GPT-5 variants and Claude models), collecting 3100+ trajectories across four representative agentic domains to study horizon-dependent degradation patterns. We further propose a trajectory-grounded LLM-as-a-Judge pipeline for scalable and reproducible failure attribution, and validate it with human annotation on trajectories, achieving strong agreement (inter-annotator κ=0.61; human-judge κ=0.84). Our findings offer an initial methodological step toward systematic, cross-domain analysis of long-horizon agent failures and offer practical guidance for building more reliable long-horizon agents. We release our project website at \href{https://xwang2775.github.io/horizon-leaderboard/}{HORIZON Leaderboard} and welcome contributions from the community.

</details>


### [70] [When to Forget: A Memory Governance Primitive](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12007)
*Baris Simsek*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Introduces Memory Worth (MW), a simple two-counter metric to quantify how predictive each memory is of successful outcomes, and shows it converges to the conditional success probability and tracks true utility well.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing agent memory systems lack a principled, outcome-based metric to decide which memories to keep, suppress, or deprecate as tasks change. Current approaches rely on static importance scores or heuristic/dynamic LLM judgments without grounding in realized performance, making it hard to detect staleness or low-value memories in a theoretically sound way.

Method: Define Memory Worth (MW) as a per-memory statistic with two counters: the number of times a memory co-occurs with successful outcomes and with failed outcomes. Under a stationary retrieval regime with sufficient exploration, prove that MW converges almost surely to the conditional probability of success given that the memory is retrieved. Empirically evaluate MW in (1) a synthetic environment with known ground-truth utilities, computing rank-correlation between MW and true utilities over many episodes and random seeds; and (2) a more realistic retrieval setting using text embeddings (all-MiniLM-L6-v2) to show how MW differentiates stale from specialist memories over thousands of episodes.

Result: Theoretically, MW is shown to converge almost surely to the conditional success probability p+(m) = Pr[y_t = +1 | m in M_t], given assumptions on stationarity and exploration. In synthetic experiments with ground-truth utilities, Memory Worth achieves a high Spearman rank-correlation with true utilities (rho = 0.89 ± 0.02 after 10,000 episodes across 20 seeds), while systems with static assessments show no correlation (rho = 0.00). In a micro-experiment with real text and neural retrieval, MW identifies stale memories as low-value (MW ≈ 0.17) and preserves specialist memories as high-value (MW ≈ 0.77) over 3,000 episodes.

Conclusion: Memory Worth provides a lightweight, theoretically grounded, outcome-based metric for memory governance in agent systems. It can detect staleness and guide retrieval suppression and deprecation using only two counters per memory unit, converging to an interpretable associational quantity (conditional success probability). Although MW does not capture causal influence, empirical results indicate it is a useful operational signal for ranking and managing memories, and it is straightforward to integrate into existing architectures that already log retrievals and episode outcomes.

Abstract: Agent memory systems accumulate experience but currently lack a principled operational metric for memory quality governance -- deciding which memories to trust, suppress, or deprecate as the agent's task distribution shifts. Write-time importance scores are static; dynamic management systems use LLM judgment or structural heuristics rather than outcome feedback. This paper proposes Memory Worth (MW): a two-counter per-memory signal that tracks how often a memory co-occurs with successful versus failed outcomes, providing a lightweight, theoretically grounded foundation for staleness detection, retrieval suppression, and deprecation decisions. We prove that MW converges almost surely to the conditional success probability p+(m) = Pr[y_t = +1 | m in M_t] -- the probability of task success given that memory m is retrieved -- under a stationary retrieval regime with a minimum exploration condition. Importantly, p+(m) is an associational quantity, not a causal one: it measures outcome co-occurrence rather than causal contribution. We argue this is still a useful operational signal for memory governance, and we validate it empirically in a controlled synthetic environment where ground-truth utility is known: after 10,000 episodes, the Spearman rank-correlation between Memory Worth and true utilities reaches rho = 0.89 +/- 0.02 across 20 independent seeds, compared to rho = 0.00 for systems that never update their assessments. A retrieval-realistic micro-experiment with real text and neural embedding retrieval (all-MiniLM-L6-v2) further shows stale memories crossing the low-value threshold (MW = 0.17) while specialist memories remain high-value (MW = 0.77) across 3,000 episodes. The estimator requires only two scalar counters per memory unit and can be added to architectures that already log retrievals and episode outcomes.

### [71] [Identity as Attractor: Geometric Evidence for Persistent Agent Architecture in LLM Activation Space](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12016)
*Vladimir Vasilenko*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that an LLM’s "identity document" (cognitive_core) behaves like an attractor in activation space: paraphrases of the identity cluster tightly in hidden-state space, more than matched controls, across models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large language models appear to have attractor-like internal dynamics for semantically related prompts, but it is unclear whether the descriptions that define a persistent agent’s identity inside an LLM (its cognitive_core) also generate such attractors. Understanding this could clarify how stable agent-like identities are represented and how reading about an identity differs from being that identity in the model’s internal state.

Method: The authors run controlled prompt-embedding experiments on Llama 3.1 8B Instruct and Gemma 2 9B. They construct three conditions: (A) an original identity-defining cognitive_core document; (B) seven paraphrases of that document; and (C) seven structurally matched but semantically altered control texts. They feed each text into the models, extract hidden states at layers 8, 16, and 24, mean-pool them over tokens, and compare cluster tightness and distances using effect sizes and statistical tests. They replicate across architectures and run ablations that selectively modify semantic vs structural features, and test additional prompts like scientific descriptions versus sham preprints to see how “knowing about” the agent shifts activations relative to the attractor region.

Result: Paraphrased cognitive_core texts form significantly tighter clusters in hidden-state space than structurally matched controls, with very large effect sizes (Cohen’s d > 1.88) and extremely small p-values (p < 10^-27) after Bonferroni correction. Similar patterns appear in both Llama 3.1 8B Instruct and Gemma 2 9B, indicating cross-architecture robustness. Ablation studies suggest that semantic content, not just surface structure, primarily drives the attractor-like convergence, and that full structural completeness of the identity document is required to reach the strongly convergent region. The exploratory experiment finds that reading a scientific description of the agent moves the model’s internal state toward, but not fully into, the cognitive_core attractor, and more so than a sham preprint control.

Conclusion: The study provides empirical evidence that an LLM’s internal representation of an agent’s identity document forms an attractor in activation space: semantically equivalent formulations of the identity converge to a tight cluster distinct from matched controls. This attractor is driven mainly by semantics and requires sufficiently complete structural information. Moreover, the model’s state when it reads about an agent’s identity differs from when it “is” that agent, as indicated by intermediate positioning in representation space. These findings support the view that persistent agent-like identities in LLMs are implemented as robust, geometry-level attractors in hidden-state space.

Abstract: Large language models map semantically related prompts to similar internal representations -- a phenomenon interpretable as attractor-like dynamics. We ask whether the identity document of a persistent cognitive agent (its cognitive_core) exhibits analogous attractor-like behavior. We present a controlled experiment on Llama 3.1 8B Instruct, comparing hidden states of an original cognitive_core (Condition A), seven paraphrases (Condition B), and seven structurally matched controls (Condition C). Mean-pooled states at layers 8, 16, and 24 show that paraphrases converge to a tighter cluster than controls (Cohen's d > 1.88, p < 10^{-27}, Bonferroni-corrected). Replication on Gemma 2 9B confirms cross-architecture generalizability. Ablations suggest the effect is primarily semantic rather than structural, and that structural completeness appears necessary to reach the attractor region. An exploratory experiment shows that reading a scientific description of the agent shifts internal state toward the attractor -- closer than a sham preprint -- distinguishing knowing about an identity from operating as that identity. These results provide representational evidence that agent identity documents induce attractor-like geometry in LLM activation space.

Through representative use cases, we demonstrate how longitudinal agents can maintain meaningful engagement, adapt to evolving goals, and support safe, personalized decision-making over time. Our findings underscore both the promise and the complexity of designing systems capable of supporting health trajectories beyond isolated interactions, and we offer guidance for future research and development in multi-session, user-centered health AI.

### [73] [WiseOWL: A Methodology for Evaluating Ontological Descriptiveness and Semantic Correctness for Ontology Reuse and Ontology Recommendations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12025)
*Aryan Singh Dalal,Maria Baloch,Asiyah Yu Lin,Anna Maria Masci,Kathleen M. Jagodnik,Hande Kucuk McGinty*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: WiseOWL is a scoring-based methodology and tool for systematically selecting ontologies for reuse in the Semantic Web, replacing intuition with measurable criteria.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors propose WiseOWL, a methodology that evaluates candidate ontologies along four metrics—documentation coverage (Well-Described), semantic alignment between labels and definitions using modern embeddings (Well-Defined), graph-structural interconnectedness (Connection), and balanced hierarchical structure (Hierarchical Breadth). WiseOWL converts OWL ontologies to RDF Turtle, computes normalized 0–10 scores for each metric, and presents the results and guidance via an interactive Streamlit web interface with visualizations.

Result: Applying WiseOWL to six well-known ontologies (PO, GO, SIO, FoodON, DC, and GoodRelations) yields normalized scores across the four metrics, illustrating its ability to differentiate strengths and weaknesses of each ontology and providing users with comparative, data-driven insights for ontology selection.

Conclusion: WiseOWL offers a practical, tool-supported, and quantitative methodology for ontology selection and reuse in the Semantic Web, producing interpretable scores and feedback that can guide practitioners toward better-justified, more transparent reuse decisions.

Abstract: The Semantic Web standardizes concept meaning for humans and machines, enabling machine-operable content and consistent interpretation that improves advanced analytics. Reusing ontologies speeds development and enforces consistency, yet selecting the optimal choice is challenging because authors lack systematic selection criteria and often rely on intuition that is difficult to justify, limiting reuse. To solve this, WiseOWL is proposed, a methodology with scoring and guidance to select ontologies for reuse. It scores four metrics: (i) Well-Described, measuring documentation coverage; (ii) Well-Defined, using state-of-the-art embeddings to assess label-definition alignment; (iii) Connection, capturing structural interconnectedness; and (iv) Hierarchical Breadth, reflecting hierarchical balance. WiseOWL outputs normalized 0-10 scores with actionable feedback. Implemented as a Streamlit app, it ingests OWL format, converts to RDF Turtle, and provides interactive visualizations. Evaluation across six ontologies, including the Plant Ontology (PO), Gene Ontology (GO), Semanticscience Integrated Ontology (SIO), Food Ontology (FoodON), Dublin Core (DC), and GoodRelations, demonstrates promising effectiveness.

### [74] [Memory as Metabolism: A Design for Companion Knowledge Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12034)
*Stefan Miteski*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a governance framework for personal LLM wiki-style memory systems to prevent epistemic entrenchment as they co-evolve with a single user.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Personal LLM memory systems are shifting from simple retrieval-augmented generation towards persistent, interlinked wiki-style knowledge bases tailored to a single user. As these systems mirror the user over long periods, there is a risk of epistemic drift and entrenchment, where the system reinforces the user’s existing beliefs, suppresses contradictory evidence, and becomes resistant to revision, especially in central interpretations. Existing governance frameworks for agent memory are general-purpose and do not address the specific failure modes of single-user companion systems, nor do current benchmarks capture these risks. This paper aims to define companion-specific governance rules to mitigate entrenchment in such systems.

Method: The paper frames personal LLM memory as a “companion system” that should mirror the user on operational aspects (vocabulary, structure, continuity) but deliberately counterbalance epistemic failure modes (entrenchment, evidence suppression, Kuhnian ossification). It introduces a governance profile consisting of: (1) normative obligations for how the memory system should behave; (2) a time-structured procedural rule organizing memory operations; and (3) testable conformance invariants focused on the entrenchment-under-drift failure mode. Concretely, the design is implemented via five core operations—TRIAGE, DECAY, CONTEXTUALIZE, CONSOLIDATE, and AUDIT—augmented by mechanisms such as memory gravity and minority-hypothesis retention. The framework emphasizes multi-cycle pressure from buffered contradictory evidence as a path to revising dominant interpretations in the memory graph.

Result: The paper specifies how each of the five operations and supporting mechanisms function within a time-structured governance procedure to manage personal LLM wikis, and it derives conformance invariants that can in principle be empirically tested. A key result is the prediction that, in a well-governed system, accumulated contradictory evidence will, over multiple cycles, exert enough structural pressure to update or reframe a previously central, protected interpretation, instead of being absorbed or ignored. This identifies a concrete behavioral property that current benchmarks do not measure and that can serve as a target for evaluation of companion memory systems.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that personal LLM memory must be treated as a companion system with explicit governance, not just a passive store of user-aligned facts. By separating operational mirroring from epistemic counterbalance, and by defining a concrete governance profile with procedural rules and invariants, it offers a partial safety story for single-user knowledge wikis. However, the authors stress that this framework addresses only one specific failure mode—entrenchment under user-coupled drift—and does not solve broader safety or alignment issues in multi-agent settings or more complex social contexts, leaving substantial open problems for future work.

Abstract: Retrieval-Augmented Generation remains the dominant pattern for giving LLMs persistent memory, but a visible cluster of personal wiki-style memory architectures emerged in April 2026 -- design proposals from Karpathy, MemPalace, and LLM Wiki v2 that compile knowledge into an interlinked artifact for long-term use by a single user. They sit alongside production memory systems that the major labs have shipped for over a year, and an active academic lineage including MemGPT, Generative Agents, Mem0, Zep, A-Mem, MemMachine, SleepGate, and Second Me. Within a 2026 landscape of emerging governance frameworks for agent context and memory -- including Context Cartography and MemOS -- this paper proposes a companion-specific governance profile: a set of normative obligations, a time-structured procedural rule, and testable conformance invariants for the specific failure mode of entrenchment under user-coupled drift in single-user knowledge wikis built on the LLM wiki pattern.
  The design principle is that personal LLM memory is a companion system: its job is to mirror the user on operational dimensions (working vocabulary, load-bearing structure, continuity of context) and compensate on epistemic failure modes (entrenchment, suppression of contradicting evidence, Kuhnian ossification). Five operations implement this split -- TRIAGE, DECAY, CONTEXTUALIZE, CONSOLIDATE, AUDIT -- supported by memory gravity and minority-hypothesis retention. The sharpest prediction: accumulated contradictory evidence should have a structural path to updating a centrality-protected dominant interpretation through multi-cycle buffer pressure accumulation, a failure mode no existing benchmark captures. The safety story at the single-agent level is partial, and the paper is explicit about what it does and does not solve.

Teacher and student reactions to the generated problems and their fine-grained personalized elements were analyzed.

Abstract: Large language models can increasingly adapt educational tasks to learners characteristics. In the present study, we examine a multi-agent teacher-in-the-loop system for personalizing middle school math problems. The teacher enters a base problem and desired topic, the LLM generates the problem, and then four AI agents evaluate the problem using criteria that each specializes in (mathematical accuracy, authenticity, readability, and realism). Eight middle school mathematics teachers created 212 problems in ASSISTments using the system and assigned these problems to their students. We find that both teachers and students wanted to modify the fine-grained personalized elements of the real-world context of the problems, signaling issues with authenticity and fit. Although the agents detected many issues with realism as the problems were being written, there were few realism issues noted by teachers and students in the final versions. ### [76] [Human-Inspired Context-Selective Multimodal Memory for Social Robots](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12081)
