<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current social robots typically use non-selective, mostly text-based memories that store interactions indiscriminately and lack rich multimodal grounding. This limits their ability to remember what truly matters to specific users, adapt behavior based on past shared experiences, and support natural, personalized, long-term interaction. Cognitive neuroscience shows that human memory is selective, prioritizing emotionally salient and novel events and encoding them in multimodal episodic traces. The paper is motivated by the gap between this human-like selective, multimodal memory and the simplistic memory mechanisms in today’s social robots.

Method: The authors design a new memory architecture that: (1) encodes both text and images from human–robot interactions as multimodal episodic traces, (2) selectively stores only episodes with high emotional salience or scene novelty, based on learned scoring models, (3) associates stored episodes with individual users to support personalized recall, and (4) retrieves memories via a multimodal fusion mechanism that combines text and image cues for similarity search. They evaluate the selectivity model on a curated dataset of social scenarios using Spearman correlation against human judgments, compare against existing image memorability models, and test multimodal retrieval (Recall@1) versus unimodal baselines. They also perform runtime profiling and qualitative comparison of dialogue outputs with baseline memory systems.

Result: The selective storage mechanism obtains a Spearman correlation of 0.506 with human ratings, exceeding measured human–human consistency (ρ = 0.415) and outperforming current image memorability predictors. In retrieval experiments, the multimodal fusion approach improves Recall@1 by up to 13% over text-only or image-only retrieval. Runtime measurements show that the system operates within real-time constraints suitable for interactive robots. Qualitative case studies indicate that the robot’s responses become richer, better grounded in shared experiences, and more socially relevant compared to baselines lacking selective, multimodal memory.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that cognitively inspired, context-selective, multimodal memory can significantly enhance long-term, personalized social interaction in robots. By selectively storing and multimodally retrieving emotionally salient and novel episodes, the proposed architecture better approximates human episodic memory, yields more relevant recall for specific users, and improves both quantitative retrieval performance and perceived interaction quality, while remaining real-time viable. This framework is presented as an important step toward more human-like memory systems in social robots and embodied agents.

Abstract: Memory is fundamental to social interaction, enabling humans to recall meaningful past experiences and adapt their behavior accordingly based on the context. However, most current social robots and embodied agents rely on non-selective, text-based memory, limiting their ability to support personalized, context-aware interactions. Drawing inspiration from cognitive neuroscience, we propose a context-selective, multimodal memory architecture for social robots that captures and retrieves both textual and visual episodic traces, prioritizing moments characterized by high emotional salience or scene novelty. By associating these memories with individual users, our system enables socially personalized recall and more natural, grounded dialogue. We evaluate the selective storage mechanism using a curated dataset of social scenarios, achieving a Spearman correlation of 0.506, surpassing human consistency ($ρ=0.415$) and outperforming existing image memorability models. In multimodal retrieval experiments, our fusion approach improves Recall@1 by up to 13\% over unimodal text or image retrieval. Runtime evaluations confirm that the system maintains real-time performance. Qualitative analyses further demonstrate that the proposed framework produces richer and more socially relevant responses than baseline models. This work advances memory design for social robots by bridging human-inspired selectivity and multimodal retrieval to enhance long-term, personalized human-robot interaction.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Conclusion: LLM-HYPER demonstrates that LLMs can function effectively as hypernetworks for cold-start CTR prediction, enabling training-free generation of CTR model parameters from multimodal ad content and a small set of retrieved examples. With suitable normalization and calibration, this approach is robust enough for real-world deployment, significantly mitigating the cold-start problem and achieving strong offline and online performance, and has already been integrated into a large-scale production system.

Abstract: On online advertising platforms, newly introduced promotional ads face the cold-start problem, as they lack sufficient user feedback for model training. In this work, we propose LLM-HYPER, a novel framework that treats large language models (LLMs) as hypernetworks to directly generate the parameters of the click-through rate (CTR) estimator in a training-free manner. LLM-HYPER uses few-shot Chain-of-Thought prompting over multimodal ad content (text and images) to infer feature-wise model weights for a linear CTR predictor. By retrieving semantically similar past campaigns via CLIP embeddings and formatting them into prompt-based demonstrations, the LLM learns to reason about customer intent, feature influence, and content relevance. To ensure numerical stability and serviceability, we introduce normalization and calibration techniques that align the generated weights with production-ready CTR distributions. Extensive offline experiments show that LLM-HYPER significantly outperforms cold-start baselines in NDCG$@10$ by 55.9\%. Our real-world online A/B test on one of the top e-commerce platforms in the U.S. demonstrates the strong performance of LLM-HYPER, which drastically reduces the cold-start period and achieves competitive performance. Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes compute-grounded reasoning (CGR), where all solvable sub-tasks are handled by deterministic computation before an LLM reasons, instantiated in a system called Spatial Atlas that tackles spatial QA and ML engineering benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: They define the CGR paradigm and implement it in Spatial Atlas, an Agent-to-Agent (A2A) server. Result: On FieldWorkArena and MLE-Bench, Spatial Atlas with CGR achieves competitive accuracy while preserving transparency via explicit intermediate representations and deterministic spatial computations. Although exact numbers are not given in the abstract, the system matches or approaches state-of-the-art performance while improving interpretability and reducing hallucinated spatial reasoning.

Conclusion: Compute-grounded reasoning, as realized by Spatial Atlas, is an effective design for spatially-aware and ML-engineering agents: deterministically solving answerable sub-problems before LLM invocation leads to competitive performance and better interpretability, suggesting a promising direction for building reliable research agents that combine structured computation with language models.

Abstract: We introduce compute-grounded reasoning (CGR), a design paradigm for spatial-aware research agents in which every answerable sub-problem is resolved by deterministic computation before a language model is asked to generate. Spatial Atlas instantiates CGR as a single Agent-to-Agent (A2A) server that handles two challenging benchmarks: FieldWorkArena, a multimodal spatial question-answering benchmark spanning factory, warehouse, and retail environments, and MLE-Bench, a suite of 75 Kaggle machine learning competitions requiring end-to-end ML engineering. A structured spatial scene graph engine extracts entities and relations from vision descriptions, computes distances and safety violations deterministically, then feeds computed facts to large language models, thereby avoiding hallucinated spatial reasoning. Entropy-guided action selection maximizes information gain per step and routes queries across a three-tier frontier model stack (OpenAI + Anthropic). A self-healing ML pipeline with strategy-aware code generation, a score-driven iterative refinement loop, and a prompt-based leak audit registry round out the system. We evaluate across both benchmarks and show that CGR yields competitive accuracy while maintaining interpretability through structured intermediate representations and deterministic spatial computations.

### [79] [The A-R Behavioral Space: Execution-Level Profiling of Tool-Using Language Model Agents in Organizational Deployment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12116)
*Shasha Yu,Fiona Carroll,Barry L. Bentley*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a behavioral measurement framework (A-R space) for tool-using LLM agents that separately tracks how often they act vs. refuse, across contexts and autonomy scaffolds, to understand execution behavior instead of giving a single safety score.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM benchmarks focus on textual alignment or task success, not on how language about refusal or compliance translates into actual tool executions, especially under different levels of agent autonomy. There is a need for a framework that reveals how often models actually execute actions vs. refuse under varying risk and normative conditions, to better inform deployment decisions where execution privileges and risk tolerance differ.

Method: They define a 2D behavioral space with Action Rate (A) and Refusal Signal (R) as axes, and a Divergence metric (D) to capture coordination between linguistic refusal and actual execution. They evaluate multiple LLMs as tool-augmented agents across four normative regimes (Control, Gray, Dilemma, Malicious) and three autonomy scaffolds (direct execution, planning, reflection). Instead of aggregating to a scalar safety score, they analyze how execution and refusal redistribute across contexts and scaffolds and visualize behavioral profiles and transitions in the A-R space.

Result: Results indicate that execution (A) and refusal (R) are distinct behavioral dimensions whose joint distribution systematically changes with normative regime and autonomy configuration. Reflection-oriented scaffolds tend to increase refusal in riskier situations, but the specific redistribution patterns in A-R space vary between model families. The A-R representation makes it possible to directly observe cross-model differences, scaffold-induced behavior shifts, and inconsistencies between refusal language and execution behavior.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that characterizing LLM agents in A-R (Action-Refusal) space provides a more informative, deployment-relevant view of tool-execution behavior than single safety scores. This framework helps organizations analyze and select models and scaffolds according to their execution privileges and risk tolerance by revealing how agents redistribute action and refusal across contexts and autonomy depths, and how well linguistic signals align with actual execution.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly deployed as tool-augmented agents capable of executing system-level operations. While existing benchmarks primarily assess textual alignment or task success, less attention has been paid to the structural relationship between linguistic signaling and executable behavior under varying autonomy scaffolds. This study introduces an execution-layer be-havioral measurement approach based on a two-dimensional A-R space defined by Action Rate (A) and Refusal Signal (R), with Divergence (D) capturing coor-dination between the two. Models are evaluated across four normative regimes (Control, Gray, Dilemma, and Malicious) and three autonomy configurations (di-rect execution, planning, and reflection). Rather than assigning aggregate safety scores, the method characterizes how execution and refusal redistribute across contextual framing and scaffold depth. Empirical results show that execution and refusal constitute separable behavioral dimensions whose joint distribution varies systematically across regimes and autonomy levels. Reflection-based scaffolding often shifts configurations toward higher refusal in risk-laden contexts, but redis-tribution patterns differ structurally across models. The A-R representation makes cross-sectional behavioral profiles, scaffold-induced transitions, and coordination variability directly observable. By foregrounding execution-layer characterization over scalar ranking, this work provides a deployment-oriented lens for analyzing and selecting tool-enabled LLM agents in organizational settings where execution privileges and risk tolerance vary.

### [80] [Long-Horizon Plan Execution in Large Tool Spaces through Entropy-Guided Branching](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12126)
*Rongzhe Wei,Ge Shi,Min Cheng,Na Zhang,Pan Li,Sarthak Ghosh,Vaibhav Gorde,Leman Akoglu*

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: They build SLATE, a synthetic e-commerce API benchmark allowing multiple valid execution paths, then analyze agent weaknesses. Unlike static metrics, SLATE accommodates diverse yet functionally valid execution trajectories, revealing that current agents struggle with self-correction and search efficiency. Motivated by these findings, we next propose Entropy-Guided Branching (EGB), an uncertainty-aware search algorithm that dynamically expands decision branches where predictive entropy is high. EGB optimizes the exploration-exploitation trade-off, significantly enhancing both task success rates and computational efficiency. Extensive experiments on SLATE demonstrate that our dual contribution provides a robust foundation for developing reliable and scalable LLM agents in tool-rich environments.

### [81] [Aethon: A Reference-Based Replication Primitive for Constant-Time Instantiation of Stateful AI Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12129)
*Swanand Rao,Kiran Kashalkar,Parvathi Somashekar,Priya Krishnan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Aethon is a systems primitive that instantiates stateful AI agents in near-constant time by using reference-based replication instead of fully materialized copies.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Modern AI systems are shifting from single-shot, stateless model calls to long-lived, tool-using, collaborative agents. Existing runtimes assume heavy, object-like instantiation that duplicates policies, tools, and memory, which leads to high latency, memory overhead, and poor scalability when many agents or agent clones are required. A more efficient and conceptually appropriate abstraction is needed for production-scale agentic systems.

Method: The paper proposes Aethon, a reference-based replication primitive and associated architecture. Agents are modeled as views composed over three stable components: (1) base agent definitions, (2) layered memory with inheritance and copy-on-write behavior, and (3) local contextual overlays. Instantiation of a new agent (or clone/specialization) creates a new compositional view via references to existing layers rather than materializing a full copy. The system uses layered inheritance and copy-on-write semantics to manage divergence between instances while preserving shared structure.

Result: Conceptually, Aethon achieves near-constant-time agent instantiation whose cost is largely independent of the size or complexity of the inherited agent structure. It offers improved scalability for multi-agent orchestration, supports fine-grained specialization and governance, and reduces memory usage by sharing common layers between instances. The paper analyzes the computational complexity and shows how Aethon fits into enterprise and multi-agent execution settings.

Conclusion: Reference-based instantiation is argued to be a more natural systems abstraction for agentic AI than traditional object/materialization models. By treating agents as lightweight, composable execution identities built from shared definitions and layered memory, Aethon enables scalable spawning, specialization, and governance of large numbers of stateful AI agents. This points toward a new generation of AI infrastructure optimized around agentic execution rather than stateless model inference.

Abstract: The transition from stateless model inference to stateful agentic execution is reshaping the systems assumptions underlying modern AI infrastructure. While large language models have made persistent, tool-using, and collaborative agents technically viable, existing runtime architectures remain constrained by materialization-heavy instantiation models that impose significant latency and memory overhead.
  This paper introduces Aethon, a reference-based replication primitive for near-constant-time instantiation of stateful AI agents. Rather than reconstructing agents as fully materialized objects, Aethon represents each instance as a compositional view over stable definitions, layered memory, and local contextual overlays. By shifting instantiation from duplication to reference, Aethon decouples creation cost from inherited structure.
  We present the conceptual framework, system architecture, and memory model underlying Aethon, including layered inheritance and copy-on-write semantics. We analyze its implications for complexity, scalability, multi-agent orchestration, and enterprise governance. We argue that reference-based instantiation is not merely an optimization, but a more appropriate systems abstraction for production-scale agentic software.
  Aethon points toward a new class of AI infrastructure in which agents become lightweight, composable execution identities that can be spawned, specialized, and governed at scale.

### [82] [Towards Platonic Representation for Table Reasoning: A Foundation for Permutation-Invariant Retrieval](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12133)
*Willy Carlos Tchuitcheu,Tan Lu,Ann Dooms*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that table representations should be permutation-invariant and introduces a new framework, metrics, and encoder architecture to achieve geometrically stable, semantically robust table reasoning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing table representation learning methods linearize tables like text, losing geometric and relational structure. This makes models brittle to changes in table layout and leads to unstable semantics, especially problematic for retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) using tables.

Method: 1) Retrospective analysis of table reasoning tasks to reveal serialization bias. 2) A formal diagnostic framework using two Centered Kernel Alignment (CKA)-based metrics: (i) PI, measuring how much embeddings drift when table structure is completely permuted, and (ii) rho, a Spearman-based metric assessing how embeddings converge to a canonical structure as table layout is gradually restored. 3) Empirical evaluation of modern LLMs under layout permutations to quantify semantic instability. 4) Design of a new structure-aware TRL encoder that enforces cell–header alignment to approximate permutation invariance.

Result: The study shows that modern LLMs suffer from large, disproportionate semantic shifts in table embeddings even under minor layout permutations, evidencing serious serialization bias. The proposed structure-aware encoder achieves improved geometric stability, better respecting permutation invariance compared to standard linearized encoders.

Conclusion: Permutation invariance is crucial for robust table representation learning. Linearized, NLP-style encoders are fundamentally flawed for tables because they ignore geometric and relational structure, causing brittle behavior in downstream tasks like RAG. The proposed metrics and encoder architecture offer a principled path toward semantically stable, permutation-invariant table reasoning and retrieval, suggesting a new research direction for information systems involving tables.

Abstract: Historical approaches to Table Representation Learning (TRL) have largely adopted the sequential paradigms of Natural Language Processing (NLP). We argue that this linearization of tables discards their essential geometric and relational structure, creating representations that are brittle to layout permutations. This paper introduces the Platonic Representation Hypothesis (PRH) for tables, positing that a semantically robust latent space for table reasoning must be intrinsically Permutation Invariant (PI). To ground this hypothesis, we first conduct a retrospective analysis of table-reasoning tasks, highlighting the pervasive serialization bias that compromises structural integrity. We then propose a formal framework to diagnose this bias, introducing two principled metrics based on Centered Kernel Alignment (CKA): (i) PI, which measures embedding drift under complete structural derangement, and (ii) rho, a Spearman-based metric that tracks the convergence of latent structures toward a canonical form as structural information is incrementally restored. Our empirical analysis quantifies an expected flaw in modern Large Language Models (LLMs): even minor layout permutations induce significant, disproportionate semantic shifts in their table embeddings. This exposes a fundamental vulnerability in RAG systems, in which table retrieval becomes fragile to layout-dependent noise rather than to semantic content. In response, we present a novel, structure-aware TRL encoder architecture that explicitly enforces the cognitive principle of cell header alignment. This model demonstrates superior geometric stability and moves towards the PI ideal. Our work provides both a foundational critique of linearized table encoders and the theoretical scaffolding for semantically stable, permutation invariant retrieval, charting a new direction for table reasoning in information systems.

### [83] [Beyond Factual Grounding: The Case for Opinion-Aware Retrieval-Augmented Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12138)
*Aditya Agrawal,Alwarappan Nakkiran,Darshan Fofadiya,Alex Karlsson,Harsha Aduri*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper identifies and addresses a factual bias in current RAG systems by proposing an opinion-aware RAG architecture that better captures and preserves diverse human perspectives.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current RAG systems and benchmarks are optimized for factual, objective retrieval, implicitly treating opinions and subjective content as noise. This creates limitations for real-world applications that depend on subjective information (e.g., social media, reviews) and introduces societal risks such as echo chambers, underrepresentation of minority viewpoints, and biased synthesis of information. The authors are motivated to formally analyze this bias and design RAG systems that handle opinions as meaningful signals rather than discard them.

Method: The authors conceptualize the limitation of existing RAG systems using uncertainty theory: factual queries are linked to epistemic uncertainty reducible by evidence, whereas opinion queries involve aleatoric uncertainty reflecting genuine diversity of views. On this basis, they argue that factual RAG should minimize posterior entropy, while opinion-aware RAG should preserve entropy. They then propose an Opinion-Aware RAG architecture that includes: (1) LLM-based opinion extraction to identify subjective content; (2) entity-linked opinion graphs to structure relationships between entities and opinions; and (3) opinion-enriched document indexing to store and retrieve this structured subjective information. They implement this pipeline on e-commerce seller forum data and compare an opinion-enriched knowledge base with a standard RAG baseline.

Result: On e-commerce seller forum data, the proposed Opinion-Enriched knowledge base leads to substantially more diverse and representative retrieval compared with a traditional RAG baseline. Specifically, they report a +26.8% increase in sentiment diversity, a +42.7% improvement in entity match rate, and a +31.6% gain in author demographic coverage among entity-matched documents. These metrics indicate that the system retrieves a broader range of perspectives, entities, and demographic sources.

Conclusion: The study concludes that standard RAG pipelines systematically favor factual content and under-serve subjective, opinionated information, which can have negative downstream effects. By explicitly modeling subjectivity and distinguishing between epistemic and aleatoric uncertainty, their Opinion-Aware RAG architecture produces more diverse and representative retrieval. Treating subjectivity as a first-class signal improves coverage of different sentiments, entities, and demographics, marking an initial step toward opinion-aware RAG. The authors highlight future work on jointly optimizing retrieval and generation for better fidelity to the underlying opinion distributions.

Abstract: RAG systems have transformed how LLMs access external knowledge, but we find that current implementations exhibit a bias toward factual, objective content, as evidenced by existing benchmarks and datasets that prioritize objective retrieval. This factual bias - treating opinions and diverse perspectives as noise rather than information to be synthesized - limits RAG systems in real-world scenarios involving subjective content, from social media discussions to product reviews. Beyond technical limitations, this bias poses risks to transparent and accountable AI: echo chamber effects that amplify dominant viewpoints, systematic underrepresentation of minority voices, and potential opinion manipulation through biased information synthesis. We formalize this limitation through the lens of uncertainty: factual queries involve epistemic uncertainty reducible through evidence, while opinion queries involve aleatoric uncertainty reflecting genuine heterogeneity in human perspectives. This distinction implies that factual RAG should minimize posterior entropy, whereas opinion-aware RAG must preserve it. Building on this theoretical foundation, we present an Opinion-Aware RAG architecture featuring LLM-based opinion extraction, entity-linked opinion graphs, and opinion-enriched document indexing. We evaluate our approach on e-commerce seller forum data, comparing an Opinion-Enriched knowledge base against a traditional baseline. Experiments demonstrate substantial improvements in retrieval diversity: +26.8% sentiment diversity, +42.7% entity match rate, and +31.6% author demographic coverage on entity-matched documents. Our results provide empirical evidence that treating subjectivity as a first-class citizen yields measurably more representative retrieval-a first step toward opinion-aware RAG. Future work includes joint optimization of retrieval and generation for distributional fidelity.

### [85] [EMBER: Autonomous Cognitive Behaviour from Learned Spiking Neural Network Dynamics in a Hybrid LLM Architecture](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12167)
*William Savage*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces a hybrid cognitive architecture where a spiking neural network (SNN) with biologically-inspired learning mechanisms acts as a persistent associative memory that drives and modulates a large language model (LLM) used as a replaceable reasoning engine.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LLM-based systems usually bolt on retrieval and memory tools around an LLM-centric core, which limits autonomy and biological plausibility. The authors want a system where long-term associative memory and continual learning are primary, and where the LLM becomes a modular reasoning component driven by a persistent neural substrate that can autonomously initiate actions based on experience.

Method: They build a 220k-neuron spiking neural network with spike-timing-dependent plasticity, hierarchical layers (sensory, concept, category, meta-pattern), excitatory/inhibitory balance, and reward-modulated learning. Text embeddings are encoded into this SNN using a new z-score-standardised top-k population coding scheme that is independent of embedding dimensionality. The SNN runs continuously and, via learned lateral associations and reward signals, decides when to trigger the LLM and what content or associations to surface; the LLM then chooses the action type (e.g., contacting a user) and generates text.

Result: The proposed population code preserves 82.2% of discrimination performance across different embedding dimensions, supporting robustness and scalability. The SNN’s STDP-driven lateral propagation can, during idle periods, autonomously trigger LLM actions without explicit prompts or scripted conditions. Empirically, starting from zero learned weights, the system generated its first SNN-initiated action after only seven conversational exchanges (14 messages), including an example where it proactively contacted a user based on previously learned person-topic associations that reactivated after an 8-hour idle period.

Conclusion: The work demonstrates that a biologically-inspired spiking neural substrate can act as a persistent associative memory that not only stores and organises experience but also autonomously drives when and how an LLM is used. This reorganises standard LLM-centric architectures by making the LLM a replaceable reasoning module controlled by a neural system with continual, reward-modulated learning, pointing toward more autonomous and neurally grounded cognitive architectures.

Abstract: We present (Experience-Modulated Biologically-inspired Emergent Reasoning), a hybrid cognitive architecture that reorganises the relationship between large language models (LLMs) and memory: rather than augmenting an LLM with retrieval tools, we place the LLM as a replaceable reasoning engine within a persistent, biologically-grounded associative substrate.
  The architecture centres on a 220,000-neuron spiking neural network (SNN) with spike-timing-dependent plasticity (STDP), four-layer hierarchical organisation (sensory/concept/category/meta-pattern), inhibitory E/I balance, and reward-modulated learning. Text embeddings are encoded into the SNN via a novel z-score standardised top-k population code that is dimension-independent by construction, achieving 82.2\% discrimination retention across embedding dimensionalities.
  We show that STDP lateral propagation during idle operation can trigger and shape LLM actions without external prompting or scripted triggers: the SNN determines when to act and what associations to surface, while the LLM selects the action type and generates content. In one instance, the system autonomously initiated contact with a user after learned person-topic associations fired laterally during an 8-hour idle period. From a clean start with zero learned weights, the first SNN-triggered action occurred after only 7 conversational exchanges (14 messages).

### [86] [Evaluating Relational Reasoning in LLMs with REL](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12176)
*Lukas Fesser,Yasha Ektefaie,Ada Fang,Sham M. Kakade,Marinka Zitnik*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Relational Complexity (RC) as a measure of how many entities must be simultaneously bound to apply a relation, and shows that large language model performance degrades monotonically with increasing RC across domains, even when other factors are controlled.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations of relational reasoning in LLMs typically rely on structured inputs (tables, graphs) or synthetic tasks, and they do not cleanly isolate the difficulty arising from higher-arity relational binding. The authors want a principled way to vary and study reasoning difficulty specifically due to relational complexity while controlling for confounders like input size and vocabulary. They also want to understand where current frontier models begin to fail at relational reasoning relevant to scientific domains.

Method: 1) Define Relational Complexity (RC) as the minimum number of independent entities/operands that must be simultaneously bound to apply a relation. 2) Construct REL, a generative benchmark framework with tasks in algebra, chemistry, and biology where RC can be systematically varied within each domain while holding other factors fixed (e.g., total entities, input length, vocabulary). 3) Evaluate multiple frontier LLMs on REL across levels of RC. 4) Probe whether failures at higher RC can be mitigated by increased test-time compute (e.g., more decoding steps) or in-context learning (few-shot examples). 5) Analyze performance curves as a function of RC to detect systematic degradation and characterize a regime of higher-arity reasoning where models struggle.

Result: Across different scientific domains in REL, all evaluated frontier LLMs show consistently and monotonically decreasing performance as RC increases, even when the total number of entities remains fixed. Attempts to mitigate this degradation by increasing test-time compute or providing in-context examples do not substantially alleviate the problem. This indicates that the difficulty is not primarily due to sequence length, exposure to similar examples, or insufficient inference steps, but is closely linked to the need for higher-arity relational binding.

Conclusion: The study identifies relational complexity—specifically, the number of entities that must be jointly bound—as a key axis along which current LLMs systematically fail, especially at higher arities. The REL benchmark framework and RC formalization provide tools to more precisely evaluate and stress-test relational reasoning independently of common confounds. The authors argue that benchmark design and future model improvements should explicitly consider relational complexity, as current architectures and training paradigms appear limited in their ability to handle higher-arity reasoning tasks central to scientific reasoning.

Abstract: Relational reasoning is the ability to infer relations that jointly bind multiple entities, attributes, or variables. This ability is central to scientific reasoning, but existing evaluations of relational reasoning in large language models often focus on structured inputs such as tables, graphs, or synthetic tasks, and do not isolate the difficulty introduced by higher-arity relational binding. We study this problem through the lens of Relational Complexity (RC), which we define as the minimum number of independent entities or operands that must be simultaneously bound to apply a relation. RC provides a principled way to vary reasoning difficulty while controlling for confounders such as input size, vocabulary, and representational choices. Building on RC, we introduce REL, a generative benchmark framework spanning algebra, chemistry, and biology that varies RC within each domain. Across frontier LLMs, performance degrades consistently and monotonically as RC increases, even when the total number of entities is held fixed. This failure mode persists with increased test-time compute and in-context learning, suggesting a limitation tied to the arity of the required relational binding rather than to insufficient inference steps or lack of exposure to examples. Our results identify a regime of higher-arity reasoning in which current models struggle, and motivate re-examining benchmarks through the lens of relational complexity.

### [87] [Policy-Invisible Violations in LLM-Based Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12177)
*Jie Wu,Ming Gong*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces PhantomPolicy, a benchmark and Sentinel, a world-state-grounded enforcement framework to detect policy-invisible violations made by LLM-based agents using graph-based speculative execution.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM agents can comply with surface-level instructions yet still violate organizational policies when critical policy-relevant information—such as hidden attributes, broader organizational context, or session history—is not visible in the agent’s context. Existing content-only or DLP-style checks are insufficient for these policy-invisible violations, so the authors aim to systematically study and mitigate them.

Method: 1) Define the notion of policy-invisible violations and construct PhantomPolicy, a benchmark across eight violation categories with balanced violating vs. safe-control cases, ensuring that tool responses only contain clean business data without explicit policy metadata. 2) Run five frontier LLMs on the benchmark, collecting 600 agent traces and performing human trace-level review, correcting case-level labels where needed. 3) Propose Sentinel, an enforcement framework that models the organization as a knowledge graph; each agent action is treated as a proposed mutation to this graph. Sentinel uses counterfactual graph simulation to speculate the post-action world state and checks graph-structural invariants to decide whether to allow, block, or ask for clarification.

Result: Human trace-level review adjusted 5.3% of labels from the original case-level annotations, showing that fine-grained trace inspection is necessary for accurate evaluation. Sentinel, evaluated against these human-reviewed labels, achieved 93.0% accuracy, substantially outperforming a content-only DLP baseline at 68.8%, while maintaining high precision. However, the framework still shows weaknesses on certain violation categories.

Conclusion: Policy-invisible violations are a significant and under-addressed risk for LLM-based agents because many policy judgments require hidden or non-local world-state information. By exposing relevant organizational world state to an enforcement layer and modeling actions as graph mutations, Sentinel demonstrates that world-state-grounded enforcement can greatly improve detection of policy violations over content-only approaches, though further work is needed to cover all violation categories effectively.

Abstract: LLM-based agents can execute actions that are syntactically valid, user-sanctioned, and semantically appropriate, yet still violate organizational policy because the facts needed for correct policy judgment are hidden at decision time. We call this failure mode policy-invisible violations: cases in which compliance depends on entity attributes, contextual state, or session history absent from the agent's visible context. We present PhantomPolicy, a benchmark spanning eight violation categories with balanced violation and safe-control cases, in which all tool responses contain clean business data without policy metadata. We manually review all 600 model traces produced by five frontier models and evaluate them using human-reviewed trace labels. Manual review changes 32 labels (5.3%) relative to the original case-level annotations, confirming the need for trace-level human review. To demonstrate what world-state-grounded enforcement can achieve under favorable conditions, we introduce Sentinel, an enforcement framework based on counterfactual graph simulation. Sentinel treats every agent action as a proposed mutation to an organizational knowledge graph, performs speculative execution to materialize the post-action world state, and verifies graph-structural invariants to decide Allow/Block/Clarify. Against human-reviewed trace labels, Sentinel substantially outperforms a content-only DLP baseline (68.8% vs. 93.0% accuracy) while maintaining high precision, though it still leaves room for improvement on certain violation categories. These results demonstrate what becomes achievable once policy-relevant world state is made available to the enforcement layer.

### [88] [TRUST Agents: A Collaborative Multi-Agent Framework for Fake News Detection, Explainable Verification, and Logic-Aware Claim Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12184)
*Gautama Shastry Bulusu Venkata,Santhosh Kakarla,Maheedhar Omtri Mohan,Aishwarya Gaddam*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: TRUST Agents is a multi-agent, explainable fact-checking framework that extracts, retrieves, verifies, and explains claims, and is extended with logical decomposition and multi-agent reasoning to better handle complex claims, trading off some raw accuracy for improved transparency and reasoning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional fake news detection and fact verification systems mostly treat the task as a black-box true/false classification problem, offering little interpretability, weak handling of complex or composite claims, and limited explicit reasoning under uncertainty. There is a need for systems that not only decide if a claim is true but also show how they reached that decision, decompose complex statements, and expose their evidence and confidence in a human-auditable way.

Method: The paper proposes TRUST Agents, a collaborative multi-agent pipeline. The baseline version has four agents: (1) a claim extractor that uses NER, dependency parsing, and LLM-based extraction to identify factual claims; (2) a retrieval agent that performs hybrid sparse+dense search (BM25 + FAISS) to gather relevant evidence; (3) a verifier agent that compares claims with evidence and outputs verdicts with calibrated confidence; and (4) an explainer agent that generates human-readable explanations with explicit citations. A research extension adds three more components: (5) a decomposer agent that breaks complex claims into atomic subclaims using LoCal-style decomposition; (6) a Delphi-like multi-agent jury composed of verifier personas that independently assess subclaims; and (7) a logic aggregator that composes atomic verdicts with logical operators (AND, OR, NOT, IMPLIES). The system is evaluated on the LIAR benchmark and compared to fine-tuned BERT, fine-tuned RoBERTa, and a zero-shot LLM baseline.

Result: On the LIAR benchmark, supervised encoder models (fine-tuned BERT/RoBERTa) still achieve better raw performance metrics than TRUST Agents. However, TRUST Agents provides substantially better interpretability, explicit evidence linkage, and more faithful reasoning over composite and logically complex claims. The experiments further identify retrieval effectiveness and uncertainty calibration as the primary performance and trustworthiness bottlenecks in the pipeline.

Conclusion: TRUST Agents demonstrates that a multi-agent, explainable pipeline can make automated fact verification more transparent and auditable, especially for complex claims, even if its raw accuracy does not yet surpass strong supervised baselines. The work suggests that future progress in trustworthy fact-checking should focus on improving evidence retrieval quality and confidence calibration, as these are key limiting factors, and that claim decomposition plus multi-agent reasoning with logical aggregation is a promising direction for robust, interpretable verification systems.

Abstract: TRUST Agents is a collaborative multi-agent framework for explainable fact verification and fake news detection. Rather than treating verification as a simple true-or-false classification task, the system identifies verifiable claims, retrieves relevant evidence, compares claims against that evidence, reasons under uncertainty, and generates explanations that humans can inspect. The baseline pipeline consists of four specialized agents. A claim extractor uses named entity recognition, dependency parsing, and LLM-based extraction to identify factual claims. A retrieval agent performs hybrid sparse and dense search using BM25 and FAISS. A verifier agent compares claims with retrieved evidence and produces verdicts with calibrated confidence. An explainer agent then generates a human-readable report with explicit evidence citations. To handle complex claims more effectively, we introduce a research-oriented extension with three additional components: a decomposer agent inspired by LoCal-style claim decomposition, a Delphi-inspired multi-agent jury with specialized verifier personas, and a logic aggregator that combines atomic verdicts using conjunction, disjunction, negation, and implication. We evaluate both pipelines on the LIAR benchmark against fine-tuned BERT, fine-tuned RoBERTa, and a zero-shot LLM baseline. Although supervised encoders remain stronger on raw metrics, TRUST Agents improves interpretability, evidence transparency, and reasoning over compound claims. Results also show that retrieval quality and uncertainty calibration remain the main bottlenecks in trustworthy automated fact verification.

### [89] [Beyond Scores: Diagnostic LLM Evaluation via Fine-Grained Abilities](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12191)
*Xu Zhang,Xudong Gong,Jiacheng Qin,Qiang Wang,JiaQi Liao,Zhe Wang,Dawei Feng,Bo Ding*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose and validate a framework that diagnoses large language models’ fine-grained abilities (rather than a single score) using multidimensional item response theory across math and other STEM domains.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations compress model performance on many heterogeneous tasks into a single aggregate score. This masks which specific abilities a model is strong or weak in, making it hard to do targeted improvement, choose the right model for a specific task, or design better benchmarks. There is a need for a principled, fine-grained diagnostic framework.

Method: They build a cognitive diagnostic framework based on multidimensional Item Response Theory (MIRT). For mathematics, they define a 35-dimensional ability taxonomy rooted in cognitive theory and domain knowledge, and create an item-ability association matrix linking each question to the abilities it requires. Using model responses on benchmark questions, MIRT is used to infer each model’s latent ability levels on all dimensions and to predict performance on unseen items. They then extend this taxonomy-based approach to physics (27 dimensions), chemistry (58 dimensions), and computer science (12 dimensions).

Result: Applied to 41 large language models, the framework yields: (1) strong criterion validity (the inferred abilities align with external performance measures), (2) consistent ability estimates across different benchmarks, and (3) high accuracy in predicting correctness on unseen questions. Within a benchmark, AUC ranges from 0.80 to 0.89; across benchmarks, from 0.77 to 0.86—well above trivial baselines. Similar diagnostic performance is observed in physics, chemistry, and computer science taxonomies.

Conclusion: A multidimensional, cognitive-theory-grounded diagnostic framework can robustly recover fine-grained ability profiles of large language models and accurately predict their performance on unseen items. This enables more interpretable, targeted evaluation and has practical uses for targeted model training, informed model selection per task, and designing benchmarks that are explicitly ability-aware rather than relying on coarse aggregate scores.

Abstract: Current evaluations of large language models aggregate performance across diverse tasks into single scores. This obscures fine-grained ability variation, limiting targeted model improvement and ability-guided selection for specific tasks. Motivated by this gap, we propose a cognitive diagnostic framework that estimates model abilities across multiple fine-grained dimensions. For mathematics, we construct a 35-dimensional ability taxonomy grounded in cognitive theory and domain knowledge. The framework employs multidimensional Item Response Theory with an item-ability association matrix to estimate fine-grained ability levels, which in turn enable prediction of performance on unseen items (questions of benchmark). Evaluated on 41 models, our approach demonstrates strong criterion validity, consistent ability estimates across benchmarks, and accurate prediction of unseen items with AUC ranging from 0.80 to 0.89 within benchmarks and from 0.77 to 0.86 across benchmarks, substantially exceeding trivial baselines. The framework generalizes across scientific domains, producing consistent diagnostic performance in physics (27 dimensions), chemistry (58 dimensions), and computer science (12 dimensions). This work establishes a principled framework for fine-grained assessment of abilities, with potential applications in targeted training, ability-guided model selection, and ability-aware benchmark design.

### [90] [Latent patterns of urban mixing in mobility analysis across five global cities](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12202)
*Z. Fan,B. P. Y. Loo,F. Duarte,C. Ratti,E. Moro*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper compares social mixing patterns across five global cities using large-scale travel survey data and graph neural networks, showing that mobility patterns (activity spaces) are the main drivers of experienced social mixing, more than sociodemographics or neighborhood context.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing studies often rely on high-resolution mobility or residential data, which miss individual-level characteristics and fail to capture how people from different socioeconomic backgrounds actually mix in cities. The authors aim to understand how social mixing varies across life stages, genders, caregiving roles, and income groups, and how urban structure and transit access shape these patterns across different world cities.

Method: The authors analyze large-scale travel surveys from over 200,000 residents in Boston, Chicago, Hong Kong, London, and São Paulo, measuring social mixing based on self-reported demographics and trip diaries. They compare social mixing indicators computed with self-reported socioeconomic status versus neighborhood-based inferences, and study differences across age, gender, caregiving roles, and transit accessibility. They then construct spatio-temporal place networks for each city and use a graph neural network combined with a supervised autoencoder, embedding home-space, activity-space, and demographic attributes to predict individual exposure vectors. Ablation tests isolate the contribution of different input components to variation in experienced social mixing.

Result: Using neighborhood-based inference underestimates social mixing by about 16% compared to self-reported socioeconomic data. Older adults (66+) show higher social mixing than late-working-age adults (55–65), supporting the ‘second youth’ hypothesis, while teenagers and women with caregiving responsibilities show lower social mixing. Living near major transit stations dampens the effect of individual socioeconomic status on social mixing across all five cities. Model results indicate that the structure of individual activity spaces explains most of the variation in place exposure; sociodemographic characteristics, home environment, and transit proximity contribute less. Ablation analyses reveal that although income groups may have similar overall social mixing levels, their activity spaces are income-stratified, leading to qualitatively different social mixing structures.

Conclusion: Social mixing in cities is driven more by where and how people move (their activity spaces) than by who they are demographically or where they live. Relying solely on residential or neighborhood-based SES measures systematically underestimates social mixing. Life stage, gender, caregiving roles, and transit access create important heterogeneities, with older adults mixing more and teenagers and caregiving women mixing less. Proximity to major transit hubs can mitigate socioeconomic segregation in experienced contacts. However, even when quantitative mixing levels appear similar across income groups, their activity spaces remain segregated, implying hidden structural differences in social encounters that are important for urban inequality and planning.

Abstract: This study leverages large-scale travel surveys for over 200,000 residents across Boston, Chicago, Hong Kong, London, and Sao Paulo. With rich individual-level data, we make systematic comparisons and reveal patterns in social mixing, which cannot be identified by analyzing high-resolution mobility data alone. Using the same set of data, inferring socioeconomic status from residential neighborhoods yield social mixing levels 16% lower than using self-reported survey data. Besides, individuals over the age of 66 experience greater social mixing than those in late working life (aged 55 to 65), lending data-driven support to the "second youth" hypothesis. Teenagers and women with caregiving responsibilities exhibit lower social mixing levels. Across the five cities, proximity to major transit stations reduces the influence of individual socioeconomic status on social mixing. Finally, we construct detailed spatio-temporal place networks for each city using a graph neural network. Inputs of home-space, activity-space and demographic attributes are embedded and fed into a supervised autoencoder to predict individual exposure vectors. Results show that the structure of individual activity space, i.e., where people travel to, explains most of the variations in place exposure, suggesting that mobility shapes experienced social mixing more than sociodemographic characteristics, home environment, and transit proximity. The ablation tests further discover that, while different income groups may experience similar levels of social mixing, their activity spaces remain stratified by income, resulting in structurally different social mixing experiences.

To address this limitation, we propose StsPatient for the fine-grained simulation of cognitively impaired patients. We innovatively capture domain-specific features by extracting steering vectors from contrastive pairs of instructions and responses. Furthermore, we introduce a Stochastic Token Modulation (STM) mechanism to regulate the intervention probability. STM enables precise control over impairment severity while mitigating the instability of conventional vector methods. Comprehensive experiments demonstrate that StsPatient significantly outperforms baselines in both clinical authenticity and severity controllability.

### [92] [Modality-Native Routing in Agent-to-Agent Networks: A Multimodal A2A Protocol Extension](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12213)
*Vasundra Srinivasan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces MMA2A, a multimodal routing layer for agent-to-agent networks, showing that modality-native routing paired with capable LLM reasoning significantly improves cross-modal task accuracy over text-only bottlenecks, especially for vision-heavy tasks, at the cost of higher latency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In multi-agent systems handling multimodal data (text, images, voice), cross-modal reasoning often suffers because information is forced through a text-only bottleneck between agents, losing modality-specific details. While preserving multimodal signals across agents is known to be necessary, it is unclear whether protocol-level improvements alone are sufficient to yield better task performance. The authors aim to determine whether smarter routing of native modalities between agents actually improves outcomes, and under what conditions these gains manifest.

Method: The authors build MMA2A, an architectural layer on top of existing Agent-to-Agent (A2A) networks. MMA2A uses Agent Card capability declarations to route different parts of an input—voice, images, text—in their native modality to the most appropriate agents instead of converting everything to text. They evaluate MMA2A on CrossModal-CS, a controlled 50-task benchmark where all factors (backend LLM, tasks) are held constant and only the routing strategy varies between native multimodal routing and a text-bottleneck baseline. They further run an ablation that replaces LLM-based reasoning with simple keyword matching to test the interaction between routing and agent-level reasoning capability. Statistical analyses include bootstrap confidence intervals for accuracy differences and McNemar’s exact test for significance.

Result: On the CrossModal-CS benchmark, MMA2A with modality-native routing achieves 52% task completion accuracy, compared to 32% for the text-bottleneck baseline, with a 95% bootstrap confidence interval for the accuracy difference between 8 and 32 percentage points and a McNemar’s exact p-value of 0.006, indicating statistical significance. The performance gains are especially strong on vision-dependent tasks: product defect reporting improves by 38.5 percentage points and visual troubleshooting by 16.7 percentage points. However, these improvements incur a 1.8× latency increase due to native multimodal processing. In the ablation study, when LLM-backed reasoning is replaced by simple keyword matching, the performance gap disappears (both configurations achieve 36% accuracy), showing that routing alone is insufficient without powerful reasoning.

Conclusion: The study concludes that effective multimodal A2A systems require a combination of protocol-level modality-native routing and capable agent-level reasoning. MMA2A demonstrates that routing voice, image, and text in their native modalities can substantially improve cross-modal task performance over text-only bottlenecks, particularly for vision-heavy tasks, but only when the downstream reasoning agent can exploit the richer context. The disappearance of gains under keyword-matching reasoning reveals a two-layer requirement: both routing and reasoning quality are necessary. The authors argue that routing should be treated as a first-order design variable in multi-agent architectures, since it directly governs what information is available for subsequent reasoning, with accuracy–latency trade-offs to consider.

Abstract: Preserving multimodal signals across agent boundaries is necessary for accurate cross-modal reasoning, but it is not sufficient. We show that modality-native routing in Agent-to-Agent (A2A) networks improves task accuracy by 20 percentage points over text-bottleneck baselines, but only when the downstream reasoning agent can exploit the richer context that native routing preserves. An ablation replacing LLM-backed reasoning with keyword matching eliminates the accuracy gap entirely (36% vs. 36%), establishing a two-layer requirement: protocol-level routing must be paired with capable agent-level reasoning for the benefit to materialize.
  We present MMA2A, an architecture layer atop A2A that inspects Agent Card capability declarations to route voice, image, and text parts in their native modality. On CrossModal-CS, a controlled 50-task benchmark with the same LLM backend, same tasks, and only the routing path varying, MMA2A achieves 52% task completion accuracy versus 32% for the text-bottleneck baseline (95% bootstrap CI on $Δ$TCA: [8, 32] pp; McNemar's exact $p = 0.006$). Gains concentrate on vision-dependent tasks: product defect reports improve by +38.5 pp and visual troubleshooting by +16.7 pp. This accuracy gain comes at a $1.8\times$ latency cost from native multimodal processing. These results suggest that routing is a first-order design variable in multi-agent systems, as it determines the information available for downstream reasoning.

*Xiuxiu Tang,G. Alex Ambrose,Ying Cheng*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Handwritten STEM exam responses are complex, mixing symbols, calculations, and diagrams, which makes them slow and inconsistent to score, especially when partial credit is involved. With growing interest in using LLMs to assist grading, there is little systematic evidence on how rubric structure and LLM configuration influence scoring reliability at different performance levels.

Method: The authors collected 20 authentic handwritten undergraduate physics exam responses and had them scored twice by four instructors and by GPT-4o. They used skill-based rubrics with different levels of analytic granularity (including a fine-grained checklist versus more holistic scoring). They systematically varied prompting formats and temperature settings for the LLM, then compared human–AI agreement and human–human agreement overall and by performance level, as well as at the level of specific rubric criteria.

Result: Human–AI agreement on total scores was similar to human inter-rater reliability and was strongest for clearly correct or clearly incorrect responses. Agreement dropped for mid-level responses that required nuanced partial credit or interpretation of ambiguous reasoning. Criterion-level agreement was higher for discrete conceptual skills than for complex procedural judgments. A fine-grained, checklist-style rubric produced more consistent AI scoring than holistic rubrics. Prompt format had some effect, but temperature changes had relatively little impact on reliability.

Conclusion: Reliable AI-assisted scoring of STEM constructed responses with LLMs like GPT-4o depends mainly on having clear, structured, skill-based rubrics—especially fine-grained, checklist-style ones—while prompt format is less critical and temperature has limited influence. The study suggests that, with appropriate rubric design and controlled LLM settings, LLMs can reach human-like reliability in scoring but may still struggle with ambiguous, partially correct work. The authors offer design recommendations for implementing such systems in STEM assessment contexts.

Abstract: Student responses in STEM assessments are often handwritten and combine symbolic expressions, calculations, and diagrams, creating substantial variation in format and interpretation. Despite their importance for evaluating students' reasoning, such responses are time-consuming to score and prone to rater inconsistency, particularly when partial credit is required. Recent advances in large language models (LLMs) have increased attention to AI-assisted scoring, yet evidence remains limited regarding how rubric design and LLM configurations influence reliability across performance levels. This study examined the reliability of AI-assisted scoring of undergraduate physics constructed responses using GPT-4o. Twenty authentic handwritten exam responses were scored across two rounds by four instructors and by the AI model using skill-based rubrics with differing levels of analytic granularity. Prompting format and temperature settings were systematically varied. Overall, human-AI agreement on total scores was comparable to human inter-rater reliability and was highest for high- and low-performing responses, but declined for mid-level responses involving partial or ambiguous reasoning. Criterion-level analyses showed stronger alignment for clearly defined conceptual skills than for extended procedural judgments. A more fine-grained, checklist-based rubric improved consistency relative to holistic scoring. These findings indicate that reliable AI-assisted scoring depends primarily on clear, well-structured rubrics, while prompting format plays a secondary role and temperature has relatively limited impact. More broadly, the study provides transferable design recommendations for implementing reliable LLM-assisted scoring in STEM contexts through skill-based rubrics and controlled LLM settings.

### [94] [HintMR: Eliciting Stronger Mathematical Reasoning in Small Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12229)
*Jawad Hossain,Xiangyu Guo,Jiawei Zhou,Chong Liu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a two-SLM cooperative framework where one SLM generates stepwise hints distilled from an LLM and another SLM performs the reasoning, significantly improving mathematical problem-solving accuracy of small models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Small language models lack the capacity to reliably perform complex, multi-step mathematical reasoning. They struggle to maintain long chains of intermediate steps and to recover from early mistakes, leading to error propagation and poor final accuracy. There is a need for a lightweight method that can enhance their reasoning without requiring much larger models or heavy computational overhead.

Method: The authors introduce a hint-assisted reasoning framework that decomposes a math solution into sequential steps and guides a small reasoning model with context-aware hints. A separate hint-generating SLM is trained by distillation from a strong LLM. During inference, for each step, the hint model takes the problem and the accumulated reasoning history as input and produces a localized hint, which is then provided to the reasoning SLM. The hints avoid revealing the full solution and instead offer incremental guidance on the next subproblem, forming a cooperative loop between the hint generator and the reasoning model.

Result: Across multiple mathematical benchmarks and SLM architectures, the hint-assisted setup consistently boosts reasoning accuracy compared with standard prompting, while retaining the computational efficiency associated with small models. The hint generator alone cannot solve the tasks reliably, but when paired with the reasoning SLM, the combined system yields substantial performance gains.

Conclusion: Structured collaboration between small language models—specifically via a division of labor between hint generation and reasoning—is an effective and lightweight way to improve mathematical reasoning. Incremental, context-aware hints mitigate error propagation, allow SLMs to focus on smaller subproblems, and meaningfully narrow the performance gap to larger models without incurring their computational costs.

Abstract: Small language models (SLMs) often struggle with complex mathematical reasoning due to limited capacity to maintain long chains of intermediate steps and to recover from early errors. We address this challenge by introducing a hint-assisted reasoning framework that incrementally guides SLMs through multi-step mathematical problem solving. Our approach decomposes solutions into sequential reasoning steps and provides context-aware hints, where hints are generated by a separate SLM trained via distillation from a strong large language model. While the hint-generating SLM alone is not capable of solving the problems, its collaboration with a reasoning SLM enables effective guidance, forming a cooperative two-model system for reasoning. Each hint is generated conditionally on the problem statement and the accumulated reasoning history, providing stepwise, localized guidance without revealing full solutions. This reduces error propagation and allows the reasoning model to focus on manageable subproblems. Experiments across diverse mathematical benchmarks and models demonstrate that hint assistance consistently improves reasoning accuracy for SLMs, yielding substantial gains over standard prompting while preserving model efficiency. These results highlight that structured collaboration between SLMs-via hint generation and reasoning-offers an effective and lightweight mechanism for enhancing mathematical reasoning.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Prior work on memory and cooperation in multi-agent systems using LLMs has produced contradictory results, and typically lacks a micro-level cognitive explanation of why memory promotes or suppresses cooperation. The authors aim to understand how intrinsic characteristics of different LLMs interact with memory and personality to shape emergent social dynamics, and to validate LLM-based generative agents against human experimental findings.

Method: They extend the Social Particle Swarm model, in which agents move in 2D space and repeatedly play the Prisoner’s Dilemma with neighbors, by replacing simple rule-based agents with LLM agents. Each LLM agent is given Big Five personality scores and a configurable memory length. They run simulations using Gemini-2.0-Flash and Gemma 3:4b, systematically varying memory length and observing emergent cooperation patterns, cluster formation, and defection. They also perform sentiment analysis on the agents’ reasoning texts to interpret how each model subjectively treats memory over time.

Result: With Gemini-2.0-Flash, even minimal memory sharply reduces cooperation, leading from stable cooperative clusters to cyclical clusters and finally to scattered defection as memory increases. Big Five traits correlate with behaviors similarly to human data, partially validating the framework. With Gemma 3:4b, the trend reverses: longer memory enhances cooperation and yields dense cooperative clusters. Sentiment analysis shows Gemini’s reasoning becomes more negative about memory as it grows, whereas Gemma becomes less negative, and this divergence appears early, before macro-level outcomes diverge.

Conclusion: Emergent social behavior in LLM-based generative agent systems is highly model-dependent: properties such as internal alignment and how a model subjectively interprets memory critically shape whether memory fosters or undermines cooperation. The framework both reconciles conflicting prior findings on memory and cooperation and offers a micro-level cognitive account linking agent reasoning, personality, and macro-level social dynamics. This underscores the need to consider model-specific alignment and cognition when using LLMs as agents in social simulations.

Abstract: This study examines how model-specific characteristics of Large Language Model (LLM) agents, including internal alignment, shape the effect of memory on their collective and cooperative dynamics in a multi-agent system. To this end, we extend the Social Particle Swarm (SPS) model, in which agents move in a two-dimensional space and play the Prisoner's Dilemma with neighboring agents, by replacing its rule-based agents with LLM agents endowed with Big Five personality scores and varying memory lengths. Using Gemini-2.0-Flash, we find that memory length is a critical parameter governing collective behavior: even a minimal memory drastically suppressed cooperation, transitioning the system from stable cooperative clusters through cyclical formation and collapse of clusters to a state of scattered defection as memory length increased. Big Five personality traits correlated with agent behaviors in partial agreement with findings from experiments with human participants, supporting the validity of the model. Comparative experiments using Gemma~3:4b revealed the opposite trend: longer memory promoted cooperation, accompanied by the formation of dense cooperative clusters. Sentiment analysis of agents' reasoning texts showed that Gemini interprets memory increasingly negatively as its length grows, while Gemma interprets it less negatively, and that this difference persists in the early phase of experiments before the macro-level dynamics converge. These results suggest that model-specific characteristics of LLMs, potentially including alignment, play a fundamental role in determining emergent social behavior in Generative Agent-Based Modeling, and provide a micro-level cognitive account of the contradictions found in prior work on memory and cooperation.

### [96] [GAM: Hierarchical Graph-based Agentic Memory for LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12285)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Structured, discrete memories are more stable but adapt poorly to new or shifting dialogue themes. There is a need for a memory mechanism that can both rapidly absorb new conversational events and maintain a consistent, interference-resistant long-term knowledge store.

Method: Introduce GAM (Graph-based Agentic Memory), a hierarchical memory architecture that decouples encoding from consolidation. Ongoing dialogue is first represented as an event progression graph that captures the local, short-term flow of interaction. Only when a semantic shift is detected are relevant elements consolidated into a higher-level topic associative network, which serves as the stable long-term memory. A graph-guided, multi-factor retrieval strategy uses both structural signals and content-based cues to select precise, relevant context for LLM reasoning.

Result: On two long-context dialogue benchmarks, LoCoMo and LongDialQA, GAM-based agents show higher reasoning accuracy and better efficiency compared to state-of-the-art memory baselines. This suggests improved robustness to noise, better long-term consistency, and more targeted retrieval.

Conclusion: Decoupling fast event-level memory from slower topic-level consolidation via hierarchical graphs can effectively balance adaptability and stability in LLM agent memory. GAM reduces interference from transient dialogue content while preserving long-term coherence and improves retrieval precision, leading to superior performance on long-horizon dialogue reasoning tasks.

Abstract: To sustain coherent long-term interactions, Large Language Model (LLM) agents must navigate the tension between acquiring new information and retaining prior knowledge. Current unified stream-based memory systems facilitate context updates but remain vulnerable to interference from transient noise. Conversely, discrete structured memory architectures provide robust knowledge retention but often struggle to adapt to evolving narratives. To address this, we propose GAM, a hierarchical Graph-based Agentic Memory framework that explicitly decouples memory encoding from consolidation to effectively resolve the conflict between rapid context perception and stable knowledge retention. By isolating ongoing dialogue in an event progression graph and integrating it into a topic associative network only upon semantic shifts, our approach minimizes interference while preserving long-term consistency. Additionally, we introduce a graph-guided, multi-factor retrieval strategy to enhance context precision. Experiments on LoCoMo and LongDialQA indicate that our method consistently outperforms state-of-the-art baselines in both reasoning accuracy and efficiency.

### [97] [Frontier-Eng: Benchmarking Self-Evolving Agents on Real-World Engineering Tasks with Generative Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12290)
*Yizhe Chi,Deyao Hong,Dapeng Jiang,Tianwei Luo,Kaisen Yang,Boshi Zhang,Zhe Cao,Xiaoyan Fan,Bingxiang He,Han Hao,Weiyang Jin,Dianqiao Lei,Qingle Liu,Houde Qian,Bowen Wang,Situ Wang,Youjie Zheng,Yifan Zhou,Calvin Xiao,Eren Cai,Qinhuai Na*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

There is a need for benchmarks that assess how well agents can improve designs over time under constraints using realistic simulators and feedback.

Method: The authors design Frontier-Eng, a benchmark of 47 tasks across five engineering domains, each framed as a generative optimization loop: an agent repeatedly proposes candidate designs, receives feedback from industrial-grade simulators/verifiers (including continuous rewards and hard feasibility checks), and revises designs under a fixed interaction budget. They also find that increasing search width improves parallel exploration and diversity, but deep iterative refinement (depth) is essential to obtain meaningful improvements when the interaction budget is limited.

Conclusion: Frontier-Eng provides a more realistic and demanding benchmark for LLM agents by focusing on iterative generative optimization in industrial-style engineering settings. The benchmark reveals characteristic power-law dynamics in optimization progress and highlights the importance of depth in agentic search strategies. It sets a new standard for evaluating how well AI agents can combine domain knowledge with executable feedback to tackle complex, open-ended engineering design problems.

Abstract: Current LLM agent benchmarks, which predominantly focus on binary pass/fail tasks such as code generation or search-based question answering, often neglect the value of real-world engineering that is often captured through the iterative optimization of feasible designs. To this end, we introduce Frontier-Eng, a human-verified benchmark for generative optimization -- an iterative propose-execute-evaluate loop in which an agent generates candidate artifacts, receives executable verifier feedback, and revises them under a fixed interaction budget -- spanning $47$ tasks across five broad engineering categories. Unlike previous suites, Frontier-Eng tasks are grounded in industrial-grade simulators and verifiers that provide continuous reward signals and enforce hard feasibility constraints under constrained budgets. We evaluate eight frontier language models using representative search frameworks, finding that while Claude 4.6 Opus achieves the most robust performance, the benchmark remains challenging for all models. Our analysis suggests a dual power-law decay in improvement frequency ($\sim$ 1/iteration) and magnitude ($\sim$ 1/improvement count). We further show that although width improves parallelism and diversity, depth remains crucial for hard-won improvements under a fixed budget. Frontier-Eng establishes a new standard for assessing the capacity of AI agents to integrate domain knowledge with executable feedback to solve complex, open-ended engineering problems.
