### [98] [MultiDocFusion: Hierarchical and Multimodal Chunking Pipeline for Enhanced RAG on Long Industrial Documents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12352)
*Joongmin Shin,Chanjun Park,Jeongbae Park,Jaehyung Seo,Heuiseok Lim*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes MultiDocFusion, a multimodal, structure-aware chunking pipeline that reconstructs document hierarchy to significantly improve RAG-based QA over complex industrial documents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RAG-based QA systems for long industrial documents usually rely on naive text chunking that ignores rich page layouts and hierarchical structures. This leads to information loss, misaligned context, and lower answer quality, especially for complex, multimodal industrial documents where meaning heavily depends on structure and visual organization. There is a need for a chunking method that explicitly models document regions, layout, and hierarchical sectioning to support more faithful retrieval and QA.

Method: The authors design MultiDocFusion, a multimodal chunking pipeline with four main stages: (i) vision-based document parsing to detect structured regions (e.g., sections, tables, figures) on document pages; (ii) OCR-based text extraction from the detected regions; (iii) LLM-based document section hierarchical parsing (DSHP-LLM) to reconstruct the global document structure into a hierarchical tree of sections and subsections; and (iv) DFS-based hierarchical chunk construction, which groups text along the tree to form structure-aware chunks suitable for retrieval and QA. The pipeline integrates visual layout cues, textual content, and LLM reasoning about document organization.

Result: On multiple industrial benchmarks, MultiDocFusion yields substantial improvements over standard chunking baselines: retrieval precision increases by 8–15%, and ANLS (Average Normalized Levenshtein Similarity) QA scores improve by 2–3%. These gains are consistent across datasets, supporting the benefit of explicit document hierarchy modeling for multimodal document QA.

Conclusion: Explicitly modeling document structure and hierarchy during chunking is crucial for high-fidelity RAG-based QA on complex industrial documents. MultiDocFusion, by combining vision-based region detection, OCR, LLM-based structural parsing, and hierarchical chunk construction, significantly improves retrieval and QA metrics over conventional chunking approaches, demonstrating that structure-aware chunking should be a key design principle in multimodal document QA systems.

Abstract: RAG-based QA has emerged as a powerful method for processing long industrial documents. However, conventional text chunking approaches often neglect complex and long industrial document structures, causing information loss and reduced answer quality. To address this, we introduce MultiDocFusion, a multimodal chunking pipeline that integrates: (i) detection of document regions using vision-based document parsing, (ii) text extraction from these regions via OCR, (iii) reconstruction of document structure into a hierarchical tree using large language model (LLM)-based document section hierarchical parsing (DSHP-LLM), and (iv) construction of hierarchical chunks through DFS-based grouping. Extensive experiments across industrial benchmarks demonstrate that MultiDocFusion improves retrieval precision by 8-15% and ANLS QA scores by 2-3% compared to baselines, emphasizing the critical role of explicitly leveraging document hierarchy for multimodal document-based QA. These significant performance gains underscore the necessity of structure-aware chunking in enhancing the fidelity of RAG-based QA systems.

### [99] [ReflectCAP: Detailed Image Captioning with Reflective Memory](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12357)
*Kyungmin Min,Minbeom Kim,Kang-il Lee,Seunghyun Yoon,Kyomin Jung*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: ReflectCAP is a multi-agent, reflection-based guidance method that improves both factuality and fine-grained coverage in detailed image captioning for large vision-language models (LVLMs).


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: ReflectCAP introduces a multi-agent pipeline that first probes an LVLM to discover its systematic hallucination patterns and blind spots in image captioning. The method is applied uniformly across several LVLM families, including GPT-4.1, Qwen, and InternVL variants.

On the CapArena-Auto benchmark, where captions are evaluated head-to-head against strong reference systems, it yields substantial performance gains. Compared to naive model scaling and other multi-agent captioning pipelines, ReflectCAP attains better caption quality with 21–36% lower compute overhead.

Conclusion: By externalizing model-specific error patterns into Structured Reflection Notes and reusing them at inference, ReflectCAP delivers more factual and comprehensive image captions with superior quality–cost trade-offs. This reflection-guided approach makes state-of-the-art detailed captioning achievable under realistic computational and latency budgets, and demonstrates that targeted guidance can be more efficient than simply scaling models or stacking heavy multi-agent systems.

Abstract: Detailed image captioning demands both factual grounding and fine-grained coverage, yet existing methods have struggled to achieve them simultaneously. We address this tension with Reflective Note-Guided Captioning (ReflectCAP), where a multi-agent pipeline analyzes what the target large vision-language model (LVLM) consistently hallucinates and what it systematically overlooks, distilling these patterns into reusable guidelines called Structured Reflection Notes. At inference time, these notes steer the captioning model along both axes -- what to avoid and what to attend to -- yielding detailed captions that jointly improve factuality and coverage. Applying this method to 8 LVLMs spanning the GPT-4.1 family, Qwen series, and InternVL variants, ReflectCAP reaches the Pareto frontier of the trade-off between factuality and coverage, and delivers substantial gains on CapArena-Auto, where generated captions are judged head-to-head against strong reference models. Moreover, ReflectCAP offers a more favorable trade-off between caption quality and compute cost than model scaling or existing multi-agent pipelines, which incur 21--36\% greater overhead. This makes high-quality detailed captioning viable under real-world cost and latency constraints.

### [100] [Preventing Safety Drift in Large Language Models via Coupled Weight and Activation Constraints](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12384)
*Songping Peng,Zhiheng Zhang,Daojian Zeng,Lincheng Jiang,Xieping Gao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They study why LLM safety alignment is fragile during fine-tuning and propose CWAC, a method that constrains both weight updates and activations in a learned safety subspace, preserving refusals while maintaining task performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Fine-tuning can unintentionally break the safety alignment of LLMs, causing models to stop refusing harmful queries. Existing methods usually regularize either weights or activations alone, which theory and practice suggest is not enough. The authors want a principled way to preserve pre-trained safety behaviors even when downstream data contains harmful content or strong distribution shifts.

Method: 1) Theoretically analyze how fine-tuning affects safety-related behaviors and show that constraining only weights or only activations is insufficient.
2) Define a "safety subspace" in parameter space derived from the pre-trained model’s alignment behavior.
3) Introduce Coupled Weight and Activation Constraints (CWAC): (a) project or regularize weight updates to stay within or orthogonal to this safety subspace; (b) use sparse autoencoders to identify safety-critical latent features and apply targeted activation regularization on them during fine-tuning.
4) Evaluate CWAC on four popular LLMs across diverse downstream tasks and different ratios of harmful data, comparing to strong baselines such as LoRA regularization, activation steering, or alignment-preserving fine-tuning methods.

Result: Across four standard LLMs and various fine-tuning tasks, CWAC yields the lowest harmfulness scores while preserving—or nearly preserving—task accuracy. The benefits hold even when a large fraction of the fine-tuning data is harmful, and CWAC outperforms other weight-only or activation-only defenses in both robustness and utility trade-offs.

Conclusion: Safety alignment in LLMs can be more reliably preserved during fine-tuning by jointly constraining both weight updates and activations in a safety-aware manner. CWAC’s coupled constraints and use of safety-critical features identified by sparse autoencoders provide a practical, model-agnostic defense that substantially improves robustness to harmful fine-tuning data over existing approaches.

Abstract: Safety alignment in Large Language Models (LLMs) remains highly fragile during fine-tuning, where even benign adaptation can degrade pre-trained refusal behaviors and enable harmful responses. Existing defenses typically constrain either weights or activations in isolation, without considering their coupled effects on safety. In this paper, we first theoretically demonstrate that constraining either weights or activations alone is insufficient for safety preservation. To robustly preserve safety alignment, we propose Coupled Weight and Activation Constraints (CWAC), a novel approach that simultaneously enforces a precomputed safety subspace on weight updates and applies targeted regularization to safety-critical features identified by sparse autoencoders. Extensive experiments across four widely used LLMs and diverse downstream tasks show that CWAC consistently achieves the lowest harmful scores with minimal impact on fine-tuning accuracy, substantially outperforming strong baselines even under high harmful data ratios.

### [101] [Heuristic Classification of Thoughts Prompting (HCoT): Integrating Expert System Heuristics for Structured Reasoning into Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12390)
*Lei Lin,Jizhao Zhu,Yong Liu,Donghong Sun,Hongbo He,Yihua Du*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes HCoT, a Heuristic-Classification-of-Thoughts prompting method that guides and structures LLM reasoning, improving accuracy and token efficiency over existing CoT/ToT approaches on complex reasoning tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs currently generate reasoning token-by-token in a stochastic, Bayesian-like way, which leads to random, non-deterministic reasoning trajectories without strategic planning. Additionally, reasoning and decision-making are decoupled: retrieved knowledge does not dynamically shape the reasoning strategy. As a result, LLMs often fail to converge to correct solutions on complex problems and lack mechanisms to correct or optimize reasoning trajectories during generation. The paper is motivated by the need for a more strategically anchored, controllable, and efficient reasoning process in LLMs.

Method: The authors introduce HCoT (Heuristic-Classification-of-Thoughts), a prompting schema integrated into the LLM’s generation process. HCoT uses a heuristic classification model to structure the problem space and classify intermediate thoughts/steps, thereby controlling the reasoning trajectory. It aligns the LLM’s reasoning with a higher-level problem representation and produces abstract, reusable solution patterns. The method is model-agnostic and can be applied on top of various LLMs. During inference, HCoT iteratively guides which type of reasoning step to take next, based on heuristic assessments of the partial solution state.

Result: HCoT is evaluated on two complex inductive reasoning tasks with large, ill-defined search spaces and on the well-defined 24 Game task. On the inductive reasoning benchmarks, HCoT outperforms existing prompting and search-based methods such as Chain-of-Thought and Tree-of-Thoughts in accuracy. On the 24 Game, HCoT achieves significantly better token efficiency than Tree-of-Thoughts with breadth-first search while maintaining strong performance. Overall, it defines a Pareto frontier in the accuracy–token usage trade-off, surpassing prior methods on that joint metric.

Conclusion: By coupling a heuristic classification controller with LLM reasoning, HCoT transforms stochastic token-by-token generation into a more strategically guided process. This integration of structured problem-space modeling and reasoning control leads to more reliable convergence to correct solutions and more efficient use of tokens. The paper concludes that HCoT offers a strong balance between performance and computational cost, is broadly compatible with many LLMs, and that reusing abstract solution templates can further enhance generalization and efficiency on complex reasoning tasks.

Abstract: This paper addresses two limitations of large language models (LLMs) in solving complex problems: (1) their reasoning processes exhibit Bayesian-like stochastic generation, where each token is sampled from a context-dependent probability distribution, leading to inherently random decision trajectories rather than deterministic planning; (2) the reasoning and decision-making mechanisms are statically decoupled, meaning dynamically retrieved domain knowledge fails to dynamically adjust the underlying reasoning strategy. These dual deficiencies result in initial decisions lacking strategic anchoring and reasoning chains often failing to converge on correct solutions, as stochastic generation lacks mechanisms for trajectory correction or knowledge-guided optimization during sequential reasoning. To resolve these issues, we propose a problem-solving method integrated into the LLM's generation process to guide reasoning. This method, compatible with numerous LLMs and featuring reusable solutions, is grounded in a novel Heuristic-Classification-of-Thoughts prompting schema (HCoT). HCoT synergizes the LLM's reasoning ability with a structured problem space via a heuristic classification model that controls the reasoning process and provides reusable abstract solutions. Evaluated on two complex inductive reasoning tasks with ill-defined search spaces, HCoT outperforms existing approaches (e.g., Tree-of-Thoughts and Chain-of-Thoughts prompting) in performance. On the well-structured 24 Game task, HCoT demonstrates significantly higher token efficiency compared to the state-of-the-art Tree-of-Thoughts-Breadth-First-Search. In terms of both accuracy and token usage, HCoT achieves a Pareto frontier balance, offering a strong trade-off between performance and computational cost.

Conclusion: Sequential unlearning—first reinforcing benign behavior, then applying layer-limited negative fine-tuning—offers a practical, reproducible path to implement data erasure requirements in LLMs used in politically sensitive settings. Model capacity influences robustness to unlearning, and the approach strikes a favorable balance between privacy-driven behavioral suppression and preservation of general language capabilities.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) are increasingly deployed in politically sensitive environments, where memorisation of personal data or confidential content raises regulatory concerns under frameworks such as the GDPR and its Right to be Forgotten. Translating such legal principles into large-scale generative systems presents significant technical challenges.
  We introduce a lightweight sequential unlearning framework that explicitly separates retention and suppression objectives. The method first stabilises benign capabilities through positive fine-tuning, then applies layer-restricted negative fine-tuning to suppress designated sensitive patterns while preserving general language competence.
  Experiments on the SemEval-2025 LLM Unlearning benchmark demonstrate effective behavioural suppression with minimal impact on factual accuracy and fluency. GPT-2 exhibits greater robustness than DistilGPT-2, highlighting the role of model capacity in privacy-aligned adaptation. We position sequential unlearning as a practical and reproducible mechanism for operationalising data erasure requirements in politically deployed LLMs.

### [103] [Enhancing Clustering: An Explainable Approach via Filtered Patterns](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12460)
*Motaz Ben Hassine,Saïd Jabbour*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper improves explainable clustering by removing redundant k-relaxed frequent patterns that generate identical k-covers, thus shrinking the search space and speeding up clustering without harming – and sometimes improving – cluster quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Explainable (conceptual) clustering needs symbolic cluster descriptions that are both high-quality and computationally feasible. The existing k-relaxed frequent pattern (k-RFP) framework yields good clusters but generates many distinct patterns that actually cover the same data instances (identical k-covers). This redundancy inflates the search space and computational cost in the SAT-based pattern generation and ILP-based cluster selection steps, motivating a principled method to detect and remove redundant patterns while preserving interpretability and clustering performance.

Method: The authors first provide a theoretical characterization of when two distinct k-relaxed frequent patterns induce the same k-cover, giving formal conditions for redundancy. Based on this, they design a pattern reduction framework that groups k-RFPs by their induced k-cover and retains only one representative pattern per group. This optimization is integrated into the existing SAT + ILP pipeline: SAT solvers still generate candidate patterns, but redundant ones are filtered using the new theory, and the ILP model then operates on the reduced, non-redundant pattern set. They also analyze the robustness and interpretability of the patterns selected by the ILP by studying how stable their induced clusters are.

Result: On several real-world datasets, the reduced pattern set is substantially smaller than the original k-RFP collection, leading to notable reductions in computational time and resource usage during clustering. The resulting clusters maintain or improve standard clustering quality measures compared to the unreduced k-RFP baseline, indicating that the filtered pattern set is sufficient and sometimes better for representing the structure of the data.

Conclusion: Redundant k-relaxed frequent patterns that share the same k-cover are a major source of inefficiency in explainable clustering. By formally characterizing such redundancies and keeping only one representative pattern per k-cover, the proposed framework significantly shrinks the pattern search space and improves computational efficiency without sacrificing—and occasionally enhancing—cluster quality and interpretability. This yields a more practical and robust pipeline for knowledge-driven, explainable clustering.

Abstract: Machine learning has become a central research area, with increasing attention devoted to explainable clustering, also known as conceptual clustering, which is a knowledge-driven unsupervised learning paradigm that partitions data into $θ$ disjoint clusters, where each cluster is described by an explicit symbolic representation, typically expressed as a closed pattern or itemset. By providing human-interpretable cluster descriptions, explainable clustering plays an important role in explainable artificial intelligence and knowledge discovery. Recent work improved clustering quality by introducing k-relaxed frequent patterns (k-RFPs), a pattern model that relaxes strict coverage constraints through a generalized kcover definition. This framework integrates constraint-based reasoning, using SAT solvers for pattern generation, with combinatorial optimization, using Integer Linear Programming (ILP) for cluster selection. Despite its effectiveness, this approach suffers from a critical limitation: multiple distinct k-RFPs may induce identical k-covers, leading to redundant symbolic representations that unnecessarily enlarge the search space and increase computational complexity during cluster construction. In this paper, we address this redundancy through a pattern reduction framework. Our contributions are threefold. First, we formally characterize the conditions under which distinct k-RFPs induce identical kcovers, providing theoretical foundations for redundancy detection. Second, we propose an optimization strategy that removes redundant patterns by retaining a single representative pattern for each distinct k-cover. Third, we investigate the interpretability and representativeness of the patterns selected by the ILP model by analyzing their robustness with respect to their induced clusters. Extensive experiments conducted on several real-world datasets demonstrate that the proposed approach significantly reduces the pattern search space, improves computational efficiency, preserves and enhances in some cases the quality of the resulting clusters.

### [104] [CIA: Inferring the Communication Topology from LLM-based Multi-Agent Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12461)
*Yongxuan Wu,Xixun Lin,He Zhang,Nan Sun,Kun Wang,Chuan Zhou,Shirui Pan,Yanan Cao*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

If an adversary can infer these topologies just by querying the system as a black box, it could expose security vulnerabilities, enable system replication, and compromise privacy. However, the extent to which such inference is possible and effective has not been systematically studied.

Method: The authors propose Communication Inference Attack (CIA), an attack method that crafts adversarial queries to elicit intermediate agents’ reasoning outputs. They then model the semantic correlations among these outputs to reconstruct the underlying communication topology. The approach uses a global bias disentanglement technique to separate signal from systemic biases and employs LLM-guided weak supervision to help learn topology information with limited or no explicit topology labels.

Result: In extensive experiments on LLM-based multi-agent systems whose communication topologies have been optimized for performance, CIA can infer the underlying topologies with high accuracy. The method achieves an average AUC of 0.87 and up to 0.99 in the best cases, showing that even under restrictive black-box access, the attacker can almost perfectly recover the communication structure.

Conclusion: The study demonstrates that communication topologies in LLM-based multi-agent systems are highly vulnerable to inference attacks, even in black-box settings. This poses significant privacy and intellectual property risks and suggests that current MAS designs need new defenses or obfuscation mechanisms to protect their internal communication structures. The work highlights the urgency of considering topology privacy as a core security objective in MAS design and deployment.

Abstract: LLM-based Multi-Agent Systems (MAS) have demonstrated remarkable capabilities in solving complex tasks. Central to MAS is the communication topology which governs how agents exchange information internally. Consequently, the security of communication topologies has attracted increasing attention. In this paper, we investigate a critical privacy risk: MAS communication topologies can be inferred under a restrictive black-box setting, exposing system vulnerabilities and posing significant intellectual property threats. To explore this risk, we propose Communication Inference Attack (CIA), a novel attack that constructs new adversarial queries to induce intermediate agents' reasoning outputs and models their semantic correlations through the proposed global bias disentanglement and LLM-guided weak supervision. Extensive experiments on MAS with optimized communication topologies demonstrate the effectiveness of CIA, achieving an average AUC of 0.87 and a peak AUC of up to 0.99, thereby revealing the substantial privacy risk in MAS.

It also outperforms prior techniques, particularly in complex multi-road scenes.

Conclusion: Automatically learning an optimal ROI based on detection, tracking, and density cues enables vehicle counting that is both highly accurate and computationally efficient. The proposed framework is detector/tracker-agnostic, robust in complex multi-road scenarios, and suitable for real-time traffic monitoring, outperforming existing vehicle counting methods.

Abstract: Accurate vehicle counting through video surveillance is crucial for efficient traffic management. However, achieving high counting accuracy while ensuring computational efficiency remains a challenge. To address this, we propose a fully automated, video-based vehicle counting framework designed to optimize both computational efficiency and counting accuracy. Our framework operates in two distinct phases: \textit{estimation} and \textit{prediction}. In the estimation phase, the optimal region of interest (ROI) is automatically determined using a novel combination of three models based on detection scores, tracking scores, and vehicle density. This adaptive approach ensures compatibility with any detection and tracking method, enhancing the framework's versatility. In the prediction phase, vehicle counting is efficiently performed within the estimated ROI. We evaluated our framework on benchmark datasets like UA-DETRAC, GRAM, CDnet 2014, and ATON. They also formally specify constraints that the similarity measures must obey to ensure desirable mathematical and interpretative properties.

Result: They obtain a unified framework capable of computing similarity scores between FOL arguments at multiple structural levels. The two model families demonstrate how to plug in either language-model-based syntactic sensitivity or more abstract measures, while still respecting the axioms and constraints. The use of contextual weights yields similarity assessments that are both fine-grained and explainable, as one can trace which components contributed most to the score.

Conclusion: The work generalizes argument similarity from propositional to First-Order Logic in a principled way, offering a flexible, multi-level, and explainable framework. This lays groundwork for more accurate and interpretable applications in argument aggregation, enthymeme decoding, and other tasks requiring similarity judgments over complex structured arguments.

Abstract: Similarity in formal argumentation has recently gained attention due to its significance in problems such as argument aggregation in semantics and enthymeme decoding. While existing approaches focus on propositional logic, we address the richer setting of First-Order Logic (FOL), where similarity must account for structured content. We introduce a comprehensive framework for FOL argument similarity, built upon: (1) an extended axiomatic foundation; (2) a four-level parametric model covering predicates, literals, clauses, and formulae similarity; (3) two model families, one syntax-sensitive via language models, both integrating contextual weights for nuanced and explainable similarity; and (4) formal constraints enforcing desirable properties.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Evaluation is often subjective or post-hoc, offering no mechanism to prevent flawed explanations from reaching users. There is a need for an automated, systematic way to verify and refine LLM-generated explanations so that XAI becomes more trustworthy and broadly usable.

Method: The authors introduce a Two-Stage LLM Meta-Verification Framework. Stage 1 uses an Explainer LLM to convert raw XAI outputs (from multiple XAI methods and datasets) into natural-language narratives. Stage 2 employs a Verifier LLM that evaluates these narratives along four dimensions: faithfulness to the underlying XAI output, coherence, completeness, and hallucination risk. An iterative refeed mechanism then feeds the Verifier’s feedback back into the Explainer to refine the explanations over multiple rounds. The framework is tested across five XAI techniques, several datasets, and three families of open-weight LLMs. Additionally, they analyze Entropy Production Rate (EPR) during refinement to study how the reasoning dynamics evolve.

Result: Experiments demonstrate that the verification stage effectively filters out unreliable explanations and improves the quality of the remaining ones. Compared with directly presenting raw XAI outputs, the framework yields explanations that are more linguistically accessible while maintaining or improving trustworthiness. The EPR analysis suggests that the Verifier’s feedback nudges the Explainer toward more stable, consistent, and coherent reasoning over iterations.

Conclusion: The proposed two-stage meta-verification framework offers a practical and efficient path to more trustworthy, user-friendly XAI. By pairing an Explainer LLM with a Verifier LLM and an iterative refinement loop, the system can automatically improve explanation quality and reduce hallucinations. The EPR-based analysis further indicates that this process stabilizes the Explainer’s reasoning, supporting the claim that such verification mechanisms are key to democratizing and safely deploying XAI in real-world settings.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) are increasingly used to translate the technical outputs of eXplainable Artificial Intelligence (XAI) methods into accessible natural-language explanations. However, existing approaches often lack guarantees of accuracy, faithfulness, and completeness. At the same time, current efforts to evaluate such narratives remain largely subjective or confined to post-hoc scoring, offering no safeguards to prevent flawed explanations from reaching end-users. To address these limitations, this paper proposes a Two-Stage LLM Meta-Verification Framework that consists of (i) an Explainer LLM that converts raw XAI outputs into natural-language narratives, (ii) a Verifier LLM that assesses them in terms of faithfulness, coherence, completeness, and hallucination risk, and (iii) an iterative refeed mechanism that uses the Verifier's feedback to refine and improve them. Experiments across five XAI techniques and datasets, using three families of open-weight LLMs, show that verification is crucial for filtering unreliable explanations while improving linguistic accessibility compared with raw XAI outputs. In addition, the analysis of the Entropy Production Rate (EPR) during the refinement process indicates that the Verifier's feedback progressively guides the Explainer toward more stable and coherent reasoning. Overall, the proposed framework provides an efficient pathway toward more trustworthy and democratized XAI systems.

### [108] [Cross-Cultural Simulation of Citizen Emotional Responses to Bureaucratic Red Tape Using LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12545)

  <summary>Details</summary>

To address this gap, we propose an evaluation framework for assessing LLMs' emotional responses to red tape across diverse cultural contexts. As a pilot study, we apply this framework to a single red-tape scenario. Our results show that all models exhibit limited alignment with human emotional responses, with notably weaker performance in Eastern cultures. Cultural prompting strategies prove largely ineffective in improving alignment. We further introduce \textbf{RAMO}, an interactive interface for simulating citizens' emotional responses to red tape and for collecting human data to improve models. The interface is publicly available at https://ramo-chi.ivia.ch.

### [109] [IDEA: An Interpretable and Editable Decision-Making Framework for LLMs via Verbal-to-Numeric Calibration](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12573)
*Yanji He,Yuxin Jiang,Yiwen Wu,Bo Huang,Jiaheng Wei,Wei Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: IDEA is a framework that distills an LLM’s decision behavior into an interpretable parametric model over human-meaningful factors, yielding calibrated probabilities and editable decision logic for human-AI collaboration.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although LLMs are used for decision-making, they suffer from miscalibrated probabilities, unfaithful or opaque explanations, and lack precise mechanisms to incorporate expert knowledge. Existing prompting or explanation-based approaches cannot guarantee calibration, factor control, or editability, which is critical in high-stakes settings. The paper aims to create a principled way to extract, understand, and reliably modify an LLM’s decision process over interpretable factors.

Method: The authors introduce IDEA, which (1) defines a set of semantically meaningful decision factors and represents decisions via a parametric probabilistic model over these factors; (2) jointly learns mappings from verbal factor descriptions to numerical parameters and the decision parameters themselves using an EM algorithm; (3) uses correlated sampling to preserve dependencies among factors when simulating decisions; and (4) allows direct mathematical editing of parameters to inject expert knowledge or enforce constraints (e.g., factor exclusion) while maintaining calibration guarantees.

Result: On five datasets, using Qwen-3-32B as the underlying LLM, IDEA attains higher overall performance (78.6%) than DeepSeek R1 (68.1%) and slightly better than GPT-5.2 (77.9%). It also demonstrates the ability to achieve perfect exclusion of specified factors and exact probability calibration, capabilities that cannot be matched by prompt-engineering approaches alone.

Conclusion: IDEA provides a structured way to distill LLM decision-making into an interpretable, calibrated parametric model over meaningful factors. This enables more reliable probabilities, controllable use of information (e.g., excluding factors), and mathematically principled editing to incorporate expert knowledge. As a result, IDEA supports more trustworthy, quantitative human-AI collaboration in decision-making tasks and sets a new standard compared to using raw LLM prompting.

Abstract: Large Language Models are increasingly deployed for decision-making, yet their adoption in high-stakes domains remains limited by miscalibrated probabilities, unfaithful explanations, and inability to incorporate expert knowledge precisely. We propose IDEA, a framework that extracts LLM decision knowledge into an interpretable parametric model over semantically meaningful factors. Through joint learning of verbal-to-numerical mappings and decision parameters via EM, correlated sampling that preserves factor dependencies, and direct parameter editing with mathematical guarantees, IDEA produces calibrated probabilities while enabling quantitative human-AI collaboration. Experiments across five datasets show IDEA with Qwen-3-32B (78.6%) outperforms DeepSeek R1 (68.1%) and GPT-5.2 (77.9%), achieving perfect factor exclusion and exact calibration -- precision unattainable through prompting alone. The implementation is publicly available at https://github.com/leonbig/IDEA.

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multimodal jailbreak attacks focus on surface-level perturbations (pixels, typography, or overtly harmful images) and largely ignore the deep semantic structure in natural images, leaving a broad semantic attack surface unexplored. There is a need to systematically uncover and study these semantic vulnerabilities to better understand and defend vision-language models.

Method: The paper proposes MemJack, a memory-augmented multi-agent jailbreak framework. Multiple coordinated agents inspect visual entities in original images, map them to malicious intents, and generate adversarial prompts using visual-semantic camouflage from multiple perspectives. An Iterative Nullspace Projection (INLP) geometric filter is used to steer generations and bypass early refusals in the model’s latent space. A persistent Multimodal Experience Memory stores successful attack strategies and is reused across images and multi-turn dialogues to maintain coherence and improve attack effectiveness. The authors also construct MemJack-Bench, a dataset of over 113,000 recorded multimodal jailbreak interaction trajectories.

Result: On full, unmodified COCO val2017 images, MemJack attains a 71.48% attack success rate against Qwen3-VL-Plus, which can be increased to 90% given a larger query/interaction budget. The framework shows that semantics-driven attacks on natural images are both practical and highly effective, and the MemJack-Bench dataset provides a large-scale empirical base of such attacks.

Conclusion: MemJack demonstrates that deeply semantic, memory-augmented multi-agent jailbreak attacks can reliably bypass current safety mechanisms in vision-language models using only natural images. The strong empirical performance reveals substantial unaddressed vulnerabilities in the semantic space of VLMs. By releasing MemJack-Bench, the authors aim to support the development and evaluation of more robust and alignment-aware defenses for future multimodal systems.

Abstract: The rapid evolution of Vision-Language Models (VLMs) has catalyzed unprecedented capabilities in artificial intelligence; however, this continuous modal expansion has inadvertently exposed a vastly broadened and unconstrained adversarial attack surface. Current multimodal jailbreak strategies primarily focus on surface-level pixel perturbations and typographic attacks or harmful images; however, they fail to engage with the complex semantic structures intrinsic to visual data. This leaves the vast semantic attack surface of original, natural images largely unscrutinized. Driven by the need to expose these deep-seated semantic vulnerabilities, we introduce \textbf{MemJack}, a \textbf{MEM}ory-augmented multi-agent \textbf{JA}ilbreak atta\textbf{CK} framework that explicitly leverages visual semantics to orchestrate automated jailbreak attacks. MemJack employs coordinated multi-agent cooperation to dynamically map visual entities to malicious intents, generate adversarial prompts via multi-angle visual-semantic camouflage, and utilize an Iterative Nullspace Projection (INLP) geometric filter to bypass premature latent space refusals. By accumulating and transferring successful strategies through a persistent Multimodal Experience Memory, MemJack maintains highly coherent extended multi-turn jailbreak attack interactions across different images, thereby improving the attack success rate (ASR) on new images. Extensive empirical evaluations across full, unmodified COCO val2017 images demonstrate that MemJack achieves a 71.48\% ASR against Qwen3-VL-Plus, scaling to 90\% under extended budgets. Furthermore, to catalyze future defensive alignment research, we will release \textbf{MemJack-Bench}, a comprehensive dataset comprising over 113,000 interactive multimodal jailbreak attack trajectories, establishing a vital foundation for developing inherently robust VLMs.

### [112] [KnowRL: Boosting LLM Reasoning via Reinforcement Learning with Minimal-Sufficient Knowledge Guidance](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12627)
*Linhao Yu,Tianmeng Yang,Siyu Ding,Renren Jin,Naibin Gu,Xiangzhao Hao,Shuaiyi Nie,Deyi Xiong,Weichong Yin,Yu Sun,Hua Wu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: KnowRL is a hint-efficient RL framework for improving LLM reasoning by selecting minimal, interaction-aware knowledge subsets instead of long hints.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: RL for LLM reasoning suffers from sparse rewards on hard problems. Hint-based RL partly fixes this but typically relies on long, token-heavy hints that are redundant, inconsistent, and expensive to train on. The authors want a way to inject guidance that is both effective and compact, and that better captures how different pieces of guidance interact.

Method: KnowRL decomposes hints into fine-grained atomic knowledge points (KPs). During RL training it uses a Constrained Subset Search (CSS) procedure to pick compact subsets of KPs that are sufficient for solving instances while accounting for interactions between KPs. They study and address a pruning interaction paradox where removing one KP can help but removing multiple similar KPs can hurt, and design the subset selection to be robust under such dependencies. They then train a 1.5B-parameter model, KnowRL-Nemotron-1.5B, starting from OpenMath-Nemotron-1.5B using this framework.

Result: On eight reasoning benchmarks at the 1.5B scale, KnowRL-Nemotron-1.5B consistently beats strong RL and hint-based baselines. Without any KP hints at inference, it achieves 70.08 average accuracy, outperforming the base Nemotron-1.5B by 9.63 points. When allowed to use selected KPs at inference, performance further rises to 74.16, reaching state-of-the-art results for models of this size.

Conclusion: Fine-grained, knowledge-guided RL with compact, interaction-aware hint subsets can significantly enhance LLM reasoning while avoiding the overhead and issues of long hints. KnowRL provides an effective framework for designing minimally sufficient guidance and yields state-of-the-art 1.5B-scale reasoning performance, with resources released for the community.

Abstract: RLVR improves reasoning in large language models, but its effectiveness is often limited by severe reward sparsity on hard problems. Recent hint-based RL methods mitigate sparsity by injecting partial solutions or abstract templates, yet they typically scale guidance by adding more tokens, which introduce redundancy, inconsistency, and extra training overhead. We propose \textbf{KnowRL} (Knowledge-Guided Reinforcement Learning), an RL training framework that treats hint design as a minimal-sufficient guidance problem. During RL training, KnowRL decomposes guidance into atomic knowledge points (KPs) and uses Constrained Subset Search (CSS) to construct compact, interaction-aware subsets for training. We further identify a pruning interaction paradox -- removing one KP may help while removing multiple such KPs can hurt -- and explicitly optimize for robust subset curation under this dependency structure. We train KnowRL-Nemotron-1.5B from OpenMath-Nemotron-1.5B. Across eight reasoning benchmarks at the 1.5B scale, KnowRL-Nemotron-1.5B consistently outperforms strong RL and hinting baselines. Without KP hints at inference, KnowRL-Nemotron-1.5B reaches 70.08 average accuracy, already surpassing Nemotron-1.5B by +9.63 points; with selected KPs, performance improves to 74.16, establishing a new state of the art at this scale. The model, curated training data, and code are publicly available at https://github.com/Hasuer/KnowRL.

### [113] [RPRA: Predicting an LLM-Judge for Efficient but Performant Inference](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12634)
*Dylan R. Ashley,Gaël Le Lan,Changsheng Zhao,Naina Dhingra,Zhipeng Cai,Ernie Chang,Mingchen Zhuge,Yangyang Shi,Vikas Chandra,Jürgen Schmidhuber*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper explores how smaller language models can decide when to answer a query themselves and when to defer to a larger model, by predicting how well an LLM judge would rate their answer.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large language models deliver high-quality outputs but are computationally expensive, which makes them hard to deploy on resource-limited devices. Smaller models are cheaper but less capable. The authors aim to mitigate this quality–efficiency trade-off by enabling smaller models to recognize when they are likely to perform poorly and should instead defer to a stronger model.

Method: The authors define two paradigms: Predict-Answer/Act (PA) and Reason-Predict-Reason-Answer/Act (RPRA). In both, a model first predicts, before answering, how an LLM judge would score its eventual output. They compare three prediction strategies: (1) zero-shot prediction, where models estimate judge scores without extra training, (2) prediction aided by an in-context “report card” of past queries, answers, and judge scores, and (3) supervised fine-tuning on data of model outputs paired with LLM-judge scores. They test these on models of various sizes, including reasoning-optimized models, against generic LLM judges across multiple datasets.

Result: Larger, especially reasoning-oriented, models accurately forecast the scores that generic LLM judges would assign to their answers even in a zero-shot setting. Smaller models initially struggle, but their prediction accuracy improves substantially when equipped with in-context report cards or after supervised fine-tuning. Across datasets, report cards and fine-tuning yield mean prediction accuracy improvements of up to 55% and 52%, respectively, for smaller models.

Conclusion: Models can be trained or prompted to anticipate the quality of their own answers as judged by an external LLM, effectively learning their own performance boundaries. This self-assessment ability enables a practical scheme where small models handle easy queries and defer difficult ones to larger models, improving overall computational efficiency while preserving answer quality, and pointing toward more efficient and self-aware AI systems.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) face a fundamental trade-off between computational efficiency (e.g., number of parameters) and output quality, especially when deployed on computationally limited devices such as phones or laptops. One way to address this challenge is by following the example of humans and have models ask for help when they believe they are incapable of solving a problem on their own; we can overcome this trade-off by allowing smaller models to respond to queries when they believe they can provide good responses, and deferring to larger models when they do not believe they can. To this end, in this paper, we investigate the viability of Predict-Answer/Act (PA) and Reason-Predict-Reason-Answer/Act (RPRA) paradigms where models predict -- prior to responding -- how an LLM judge would score their output. We evaluate three approaches: zero-shot prediction, prediction using an in-context report card, and supervised fine-tuning. Our results show that larger models (particularly reasoning models) perform well when predicting generic LLM judges zero-shot, while smaller models can reliably predict such judges well after being fine-tuned or provided with an in-context report card. Altogether, both approaches can substantially improve the prediction accuracy of smaller models, with report cards and fine-tuning achieving mean improvements of up to 55% and 52% across datasets, respectively. These findings suggest that models can learn to predict their own performance limitations, paving the way for more efficient and self-aware AI systems.

### [114] [Broadening the Applicability of Conditional Syntax Splitting for Reasoning from Conditional Belief Bases](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12660)
*Lars-Phillip Spiegel,Jonas Haldimann,Jesse Heyninck,Gabriele Kern-Isberner,Christoph Beierle*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper generalizes syntax splitting for nonmonotonic reasoning from conditional belief bases to allow overlapping atoms and nontrivial conditionals, and studies which inference operators satisfy the new postulates.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Classical syntax splitting only works when a belief base can be partitioned into subbases over disjoint signatures, which is uncommon in realistic knowledge bases. Safe conditional syntax splitting tried to relax this by allowing some overlap, but was later shown to only permit trivial, self-fulfilling conditionals in the overlap, making it of limited practical use. There is thus a need for a more general notion of splitting that allows meaningful overlap (including nontrivial conditionals) while still supporting principled nonmonotonic inference.

Method: The authors define a generalized form of conditional syntax splitting for belief bases of conditionals that permits overlapping signatures and nontrivial shared conditionals. They distinguish between ‘genuine’ splittings, which truly help decompose inference, and ‘simple’ splittings, which offer no real advantage. Based on the generalized splitting notion, they formulate adjusted inference postulates and then systematically evaluate several well-known inductive nonmonotonic inference operators against these new postulates. They also carry out a formal comparison between generalized conditional syntax splitting and the older notion of conditional syntax splitting.

Result: The authors obtain a more permissive splitting framework in which subbases may share atoms and contain nontrivial conditionals while still enabling sound inductive inference. They classify which belief base decompositions count as genuine splittings and which are merely simple. They show that standard inductive inference operators can be characterized in terms of their compliance or noncompliance with the new generalized splitting postulates, and they prove that every operator satisfying generalized conditional syntax splitting also satisfies (ordinary) conditional syntax splitting, but not conversely.

Conclusion: Generalized conditional syntax splitting significantly broadens the practical applicability of splitting postulates in nonmonotonic reasoning from conditional belief bases. It formally captures more realistic decompositions of knowledge that involve overlapping and nontrivial conditionals, while distinguishing useful (genuine) from unhelpful (simple) splittings. The new postulates refine our understanding of inductive inference operators: generalized splitting implies classical conditional syntax splitting but is strictly stronger, so it serves as a sharper criterion for designing and evaluating nonmonotonic inference systems.

Abstract: In nonmonotonic reasoning from conditional belief bases, an inference operator satisfying syntax splitting postulates allows for taking only the relevant parts of a belief base into account, provided that the belief base splits into subbases based on disjoint signatures. Because such disjointness is rare in practice, safe conditional syntax splitting has been proposed as a generalization of syntax splitting, allowing the conditionals in the subbases to share some atoms. Recently this overlap of conditionals has been shown to be limited to trivial, self-fulfilling conditionals. In this article, we propose a generalization of safe conditional syntax splittings that broadens the applicability of splitting postulates. In contrast to safe conditional syntax splitting, our generalized notion supports syntax splittings of a belief base Δ where the subbases of Δ may share atoms and nontrivial conditionals. We illustrate how this new notion overcomes limitations of previous splitting concepts, and we identify genuine splittings, separating them from simple splittings that do not provide benefits for inductive inference from Δ. We introduce adjusted inference postulates based on our generalization of conditional syntax splitting, and we evaluate several popular inductive inference operators with respect to these postulates. Furthermore, we show that, while every inductive inference operator satisfying generalized conditional syntax splitting also satisfies conditional syntax splitting, the reverse does not hold.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: They define a new task called Human-centric Topic Modeling (Human-TM), where a human-provided goal is integrated into topic discovery. They propose GCTM-OT (Goal-prompted Contrastive Topic Model with Optimal Transport). The pipeline: (1) use LLM prompting on the document collection to extract candidate goals; (2) incorporate these goal candidates into a semantic contrastive learning framework that uses optimal transport to align documents and goals while discovering topic representations; (3) train a topic model whose topics are both coherent/diverse and aligned with these goals. The optimal transport component provides a principled way to match document semantics with goal representations during contrastive learning.

Result: On three public subreddit datasets, GCTM-OT outperforms state-of-the-art topic modeling baselines on standard metrics of topic coherence and diversity, and it also shows significantly better alignment between discovered topics and human-provided goals. This demonstrates that explicitly modeling goals improves both traditional quantitative measures and human-centric alignment.

Conclusion: Incorporating human goals directly into topic modeling (Human-TM) leads to more interpretable, diverse, and user-aligned topics than conventional methods focused only on statistical coherence. The proposed GCTM-OT model, combining LLM-based goal extraction with optimal-transport-driven contrastive learning, is an effective approach and points toward a broader class of human-centric topic discovery systems that better reflect user intent.

Abstract: Existing topic modeling methods, from LDA to recent neural and LLM-based approaches, which focus mainly on statistical coherence, often produce redundant or off-target topics that miss the user's underlying intent. We introduce Human-centric Topic Modeling, \emph{Human-TM}), a novel task formulation that integrates a human-provided goal directly into the topic modeling process to produce interpretable, diverse and goal-oriented topics. To tackle this challenge, we propose the \textbf{G}oal-prompted \textbf{C}ontrastive \textbf{T}opic \textbf{M}odel with \textbf{O}ptimal \textbf{T}ransport (GCTM-OT), which first uses LLM-based prompting to extract goal candidates from documents, then incorporates these into semantic-aware contrastive learning via optimal transport for topic discovery. Experimental results on three public subreddit datasets show that GCTM-OT outperforms state-of-the-art baselines in topic coherence and diversity while significantly improving alignment with human-provided goals, paving the way for more human-centric topic discovery systems.

### [116] [Safe reinforcement learning with online filtering for fatigue-predictive human-robot task planning and allocation in production](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12667)

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a safe reinforcement learning approach (PF-CD3Q) for dynamic human-robot task planning and allocation that respects human fatigue limits in real time.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In human-robot collaborative manufacturing, it is crucial to improve efficiency while ensuring worker well-being. Existing human-robot task planning methods often assume fixed fatigue model parameters, but real workers’ fatigue sensitivity changes over time due to variable conditions like sleep and workload. This mismatch can lead to unsafe or suboptimal task allocations. The paper is motivated by the need for a planning and allocation method that can adapt online to uncertain and time-varying human fatigue while still guaranteeing safety constraints.

Method: The authors formulate the dynamic human-robot task planning and allocation (HRTPA) problem as a constrained Markov decision process (CMDP) where constraints come from human fatigue safety limits. They design PF-CD3Q, which combines a particle filter (PF) with constrained dueling double deep Q-learning (CD3Q). First, PF-based estimators track workers’ fatigue states and online-update the parameters of a fatigue model using observed fatigue progression. Then, these real-time fatigue estimates and predictions are embedded into the CD3Q decision-making: the agent predicts task-level fatigue effects for candidate actions and removes those that would exceed fatigue limits, effectively constraining the action space during learning and execution.

Result: The approach enables real-time, fatigue-aware task planning and allocation that explicitly enforces fatigue safety limits while optimizing efficiency. By online-estimating uncertain fatigue parameters and integrating them into safe RL, PF-CD3Q can adapt to day-to-day variations in human fatigue sensitivity, handle production dynamics, and maintain safety through CMDP-based constraints. (Concrete numerical results are not provided in the abstract.)

Conclusion: The paper concludes that integrating particle-filter-based online fatigue estimation with constrained deep RL is an effective way to solve the complex HRTPA problem under uncertain human fatigue. PF-CD3Q can dynamically allocate tasks between humans and robots while keeping human fatigue within safe bounds, supporting the ergonomic and efficiency goals of Industry 5.0 human-robot collaborative manufacturing.

Abstract: Human-robot collaborative manufacturing, a core aspect of Industry 5.0, emphasizes ergonomics to enhance worker well-being. This paper addresses the dynamic human-robot task planning and allocation (HRTPA) problem, which involves determining when to perform tasks and who should execute them to maximize efficiency while ensuring workers' physical fatigue remains within safe limits. The inclusion of fatigue constraints, combined with production dynamics, significantly increases the complexity of the HRTPA problem. Traditional fatigue-recovery models in HRTPA often rely on static, predefined hyperparameters. However, in practice, human fatigue sensitivity varies daily due to factors such as changed work conditions and insufficient sleep. To better capture this uncertainty, we treat fatigue-related parameters as inaccurate and estimate them online based on observed fatigue progression during production. To address these challenges, we propose PF-CD3Q, a safe reinforcement learning (safe RL) approach that integrates the particle filter with constrained dueling double deep Q-learning for real-time fatigue-predictive HRTPA. Specifically, we first develop PF-based estimators to track human fatigue and update fatigue model parameters in real-time. These estimators are then integrated into CD3Q by making task-level fatigue predictions during decision-making and excluding tasks that exceed fatigue limits, thereby constraining the action space and formulating the problem as a constrained Markov decision process (CMDP).

The approach is implemented and tested in a 3D real-time simulator that mimics a complex production process.

The results demonstrate that our proposed EBQ&SAP method effectively addresses human-robot TPA problems in complex and dynamic production processes.

### [118] [MISID: A Multimodal Multi-turn Dataset for Complex Intent Recognition in Strategic Deception Games](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12700)
*Shufang Lin,Muyang Chen,Xiabing Zhou,Rongrong Zhang,Dayou Zhang,Fangxin Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces MISID, a new multimodal, multi-turn benchmark for detecting hidden intent in complex strategic interactions, and FRACTAM, a baseline framework that improves MLLMs’ ability to recognize and reason about such intent.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing intent recognition datasets and models mostly target single utterances or simple dialogues, which do not reflect real-world situations where people maintain complex, often deceptive, strategies across long multi-party interactions. There is a need for a benchmark and methods that capture fine-grained, long-context, multimodal and strategic intent, and expose where current Multimodal LLMs fail.

Method: 1) Build MISID, a benchmark sourced from high-stakes social strategy games, with multimodal (text + vision), multi-turn, multi-participant interactions and a two-tier, multi-dimensional annotation scheme designed for long-context discourse and causal evidence tracking. 2) Systematically evaluate state-of-the-art MLLMs on this benchmark to diagnose failure modes such as text-prior visual hallucinations, weak cross-modal fusion, and difficulty chaining causal cues. 3) Propose FRACTAM, a baseline framework following a “Decouple-Anchor-Reason” paradigm: (a) decouple modalities to get unbiased unimodal factual representations and reduce text bias; (b) use two-stage retrieval to anchor long-range relevant facts; and (c) explicitly construct cross-modal evidence chains for reasoning about hidden intent.

Result: Experiments on MISID show that existing MLLMs perform poorly on complex strategic, long-horizon intent recognition, confirming issues like hallucinations and weak cross-modal reasoning. FRACTAM, when applied to mainstream models, significantly improves detection and inference of hidden intent while keeping strong basic perceptual performance.

Conclusion: MISID exposes substantial gaps in current MLLMs’ ability to understand and reason about human intent in complex, deceptive, multimodal interactions. The proposed FRACTAM framework mitigates these issues by separating modality processing, better anchoring long-range evidence, and explicitly chaining multimodal cues, leading to better hidden intent recognition without sacrificing perceptual accuracy. MISID and FRACTAM together provide a new benchmark and baseline for future research on strategic intent understanding in multimodal, multi-party, long-context settings.

Abstract: Understanding human intent in complex multi-turn interactions remains a fundamental challenge in human-computer interaction and behavioral analysis. While existing intent recognition datasets focus mainly on single utterances or simple dialogues, real-world scenarios often involve sophisticated strategic interactions where participants must maintain complex deceptive narratives over extended periods. To address this gap, we introduce MISID, a comprehensive multimodal, multi-turn, and multi-participant benchmark for intent recognition. Sourced from high-stakes social strategy games, MISID features a fine-grained, two-tier multi-dimensional annotation scheme tailored for long-context discourse analysis and evidence-based causal tracking. Our systematic evaluation of state-of-the-art Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) on MISID reveals critical deficiencies in complex scenarios, including text-prior visual hallucination, impaired cross-modal synergy, and limited capacity in chaining causal cues. Consequently, we propose FRACTAM as a baseline framework. Using a ``Decouple-Anchor-Reason'' paradigm, FRACTAM reduces text bias by extracting pure unimodal factual representations, employs two-stage retrieval for long-range factual anchoring, and constructs explicit cross-modal evidence chains. Extensive experiments demonstrate that FRACTAM enhances mainstream models' performance in complex strategic tasks, improving hidden intent detection and inference while maintaining robust perceptual accuracy. Our dataset is available at https://naislab.cn/datasets/MISID.

</details>

These assets are stored as cases that can be retrieved and adapted when solving new tasks. Abstract: LLM-based autonomous agents perform well on general reasoning tasks but still struggle to reliably use task structure, key constraints, and prior experience in complex real-world settings. We propose a case-based learning framework that converts experience from past tasks into reusable knowledge assets, allowing agents to transfer prior case experience to new tasks and perform more structured analysis. Unlike methods based mainly on pretrained knowledge or static prompts, our framework emphasizes extracting and reusing task-relevant knowledge, analytical prompts, and operational skills from real cases. We evaluate the method on a unified benchmark of six complex task categories and compare it with Zero-Shot, Few-Shot, Checklist Prompt, and Rule Memory baselines. Results show that our method achieves consistently strong performance across all tasks and matches or outperforms the best baseline in every case, with especially clear gains on more complex tasks. Further analysis shows that the advantage of case-based learning increases with task complexity, and that practical knowledge acquired by one agent can be reused by others. These findings suggest that case-based learning offers a promising path for building professional agents for real-world work.

### [120] [Can AI Tools Transform Low-Demand Math Tasks? An Evaluation of Task Modification Capabilities](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12743)
*Danielle S. Fox,Brenda L. Robles,Elizabeth DiPietro Brovey,Christian D. Schunn*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper evaluates how well various AI tools can upgrade low-level math tasks into higher-cognitive-demand tasks and finds only moderate, inconsistent success.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although prior work shows AI can classify the quality of math tasks, it is unclear whether AI can also *improve* such tasks. Since teachers often need to adapt low-cognitive-demand problems into richer ones, understanding AI’s generative support potential is important for curriculum design and teacher assistance.

Method: The authors selected 11 AI tools, including 6 general-purpose systems (e.g., ChatGPT, Claude) and 5 math-teacher-focused systems (e.g., Khanmigo, coteach.ai). Using the Task Analysis Guide framework by Stein & Smith (1998), they designed prompts that mimic how knowledgeable teachers might ask AIs to upgrade low-demand math tasks into higher-demand ones, without heavy prompt engineering. They applied these prompts to two types of low-demand tasks and coded the AI outputs for whether the cognitive demand level had been correctly upgraded, remained too low (“undershooting”), or overshot into an unrealistically demanding category for classroom use.

Result: Across tools, AI successfully upgraded tasks to the intended higher cognitive-demand level only 64% of the time. Performance varied widely by tool, from 33% (weak) to 88% (strong). Specialized math-teacher tools were only somewhat better than general-purpose tools. Common failure patterns included leaving the task essentially low-level (undershooting) or pushing it into too advanced or impractical forms (overshooting). There was a small negative correlation (r = -0.35) between an AI’s accuracy in classifying task demand and its success in upgrading tasks, indicating these are distinct capabilities.

Conclusion: AI tools can sometimes help teachers upgrade low-cognitive-demand math tasks but are far from reliably effective, even when prompts resemble what skilled teachers might naturally use. Specialized education-focused AIs offer only modest advantages over general-purpose systems. Because the ability to generate higher-demand tasks is distinct from the ability to classify tasks, future work must develop targeted methods and designs to better support teachers in adapting curriculum rather than assuming that good classifiers are also good generators.

Abstract: While recent research has explored AI tools' ability to classify the quality of mathematical tasks (arXiv:2603.03512), little is known about their capacity to increase the quality of existing tasks. This study investigated whether AI tools could successfully upgrade low-cognitive-demand mathematics tasks. Eleven tools were tested, including six broadly available, general-purpose AI tools (e.g., ChatGPT and Claude) and five tools specialized for mathematics teachers (e.g., Khanmigo, coteach.ai). Using the Task Analysis Guide framework (Stein & Smith, 1998), we prompted AI tools to modify two different types of low-demand mathematical tasks. The prompting strategy aimed to represent likely approaches taken by knowledgeable teachers, rather than extensive optimization to find a more effective prompt (i.e., an optimistic typical outcome). On average, AI tools were only moderately successful: tasks were accurately upgraded only 64% of the time, with different AI tool performance ranging from quite weak (33%) to broadly successful (88%). Specialized tools were only moderately more successful than general-purpose tools. Failure modes included both "undershooting" (maintaining low cognitive demand) and "overshooting" (elevating tasks to an overly ambitious target category that likely would be rejected by teachers). Interestingly, there was a small negative correlation (r = -.35) between whether a given AI tool was able to correctly classify the cognitive demand of tasks and whether the AI was able to upgrade tasks, showing that the ability to modify tasks (i.e., a generative task) represents a distinct capability from the ability to classify them (i.e., judgement using a rubric). These findings have important implications for understanding AI's potential role in curriculum adaptation and highlight the need for specialized approaches to support teachers in modifying instructional materials.

### [121] [DocSeeker: Structured Visual Reasoning with Evidence Grounding for Long Document Understanding](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12812)
*Hao Yan,Yuliang Liu,Xingchen Liu,Yuyi Zhang,Minghui Liao,Jihao Wu,Wei Chen,Xiang Bai*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: DocSeeker is a multimodal LLM framework for long-document understanding that improves evidence localization and reasoning via a structured workflow and specialized training.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

There is a need for models that can systematically analyze, locate, and reason over relevant parts of very long, multi-page documents while handling memory limits.

Method: The authors propose DocSeeker, which enforces an "Analysis, Localization, Reasoning" workflow. They use a two-stage training pipeline: (1) Supervised fine-tuning on high-quality signals generated via an efficient knowledge distillation process, so the model learns explicit analysis and evidence-localization behaviors; (2) Evidence-aware Group Relative Policy Optimization, a reinforcement learning strategy that jointly optimizes evidence localization and answer correctness. They also add an Evidence-Guided Resolution Allocation mechanism to decide how to allocate visual resolution across multi-page documents under memory constraints.

Result: In experiments, DocSeeker outperforms prior MLLMs on long-document understanding benchmarks, including both in-domain and out-of-domain tasks. It shows strong generalization from training on short documents to handling ultra-long documents, and integrates well with visual Retrieval-Augmented Generation systems.

Conclusion: Enforcing a structured analysis-localization-reasoning pipeline, combined with evidence-aware training and resolution allocation, substantially improves MLLMs on long-document understanding. DocSeeker provides a strong, generalizable foundation for long-document question answering and works naturally with visual RAG architectures.

Abstract: Existing Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) suffer from significant performance degradation on the long document understanding task as document length increases. This stems from two fundamental challenges: 1) a low Signal-to-Noise Ratio (SNR), with crucial evidence buried in irrelevant pages; and 2) supervision scarcity, as datasets offering only final short answers provide a weak learning signal. In this paper, we address these challenges by proposing a paradigm that requires the model to execute a structured ``\textbf{Analysis}, \textbf{Localization} and \textbf{Reasoning}'' workflow. To instill this capability, we design a two-stage training framework: we first perform Supervised Fine-Tuning on high-quality data generated via an efficient knowledge distillation strategy. Subsequently, we employ an Evidence-aware Group Relative Policy Optimization which jointly optimizes for both evidence localization and answer accuracy. Additionally, we introduce a Evidence-Guided Resolution Allocation strategy to mitigate memory constraints of training on multi-pages documents. Extensive experiments demonstrate that DocSeeker achieves superior performance on both in-domain and out-of-domain tasks. We show it robustly generalizes from short-page training to ultra-long documents and is naturally synergistic with visual Retrieval-Augmented Generation systems, serving as a solid foundation for their implementation.

### [122] [RePAIR: Interactive Machine Unlearning through Prompt-Aware Model Repair](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12820)
*Jagadeesh Rachapudi,Pranav Singh,Ritali Vatsi,Praful Hambarde,Amit Shukla*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose an interactive, user-driven way to make LLMs forget specific pieces of knowledge at inference time, without retraining, via a new framework called RePAIR and a training-free activation-editing method STAMP.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs trained on large web corpora inevitably learn harmful content, misinformation, and personal data, and current machine unlearning methods require model provider intervention, extensive retraining, and curated datasets. This leaves end users without a practical, fine-grained way to remove or correct knowledge from the models they interact with.

Method: They introduce Interactive Machine Unlearning (IMU), where users issue natural language instructions for forgetting at inference time. To realize this, they propose RePAIR, consisting of: (i) a watchdog model that detects user intent to unlearn; (ii) a surgeon model that generates a repair procedure; and (iii) a patient model whose parameters are automatically updated. The core technical component is STAMP (Steering Through Activation Manipulation with PseudoInverse), a training-free, single-sample unlearning technique that uses closed-form pseudoinverse-based updates to redirect MLP activations into a refusal subspace. A low-rank version of STAMP reduces complexity from O(d^3) to O(r^3 + r^2 d) for efficient, on-device editing.

Result: On benchmarks involving harmful knowledge suppression, misinformation correction, and personal data erasure, RePAIR attains near-zero forgetting metrics (Acc_f = 0.00, F-RL = 0.00) while preserving high retained-task performance (Acc_r up to 84.47, R-RL up to 0.88). It outperforms six state-of-the-art machine unlearning and model editing baselines and achieves up to ~3x speedup relative to training-based methods.

Conclusion: RePAIR provides an effective, efficient, and practical framework for user-driven model editing and unlearning directly at inference time, enabling transparent and on-device control over what LLMs remember. Its training-free activation-manipulation approach makes it suitable for deployment beyond centralized providers and suggests natural extensions to multimodal foundation models.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) inherently absorb harmful knowledge, misinformation, and personal data during pretraining on large-scale web corpora, with no native mechanism for selective removal. While machine unlearning offers a principled solution, existing approaches are provider-centric, requiring retraining pipelines, curated retain datasets, and direct intervention by model service providers (MSPs), thereby excluding end users from controlling their own data. We introduce Interactive Machine Unlearning (IMU), a new paradigm in which users can instruct LLMs to forget targeted knowledge through natural language at inference time. To realize IMU, we propose RePAIR, a prompt-aware model repair framework comprising (i) a watchdog model for unlearning intent detection, (ii) a surgeon model for generating repair procedures, and (iii) a patient model whose parameters are updated autonomously. At the core of RePAIR, we develop Steering Through Activation Manipulation with PseudoInverse (STAMP), a training-free, single-sample unlearning method that redirects MLP activations toward a refusal subspace via closed-form pseudoinverse updates. Its low-rank variant reduces computational complexity from O(d^3) to O(r^3 + r^2 * d), enabling efficient on-device unlearning with up to ~3x speedup over training-based baselines. Extensive experiments across harmful knowledge suppression, misinformation correction, and personal data erasure demonstrate that RePAIR achieves near-zero forget scores (Acc_f = 0.00, F-RL = 0.00) while preserving model utility (Acc_r up to 84.47, R-RL up to 0.88), outperforming six state-of-the-art baselines. These results establish RePAIR as an effective and practical framework for user-driven model editing, advancing transparent and on-device control over learned knowledge, with potential extensions to multimodal foundation models.

  <summary>Details</summary>

The authors aim to explain the neuro-computational origin of pictographic writing and how internal percepts become external symbols.

Method: They design a biologically inspired ‘digital twin’ of the visual hierarchy that mimics feedforward and recurrent processing in human visual cortex. The system: (1) encodes images into low-level visual features; (2) generates an initial contour sketch; and (3) iteratively refines this sketch via top-down feedback driven by semantic representations, progressively compressing the image into stable, boundary-based abstractions. Result: The model produces symbolic contour sketches that show strong structural similarity to early pictographic characters across distant cultures, including Egyptian hieroglyphs, Chinese oracle-bone script, and proto-cuneiform. In some cases, the generated symbols offer plausible candidate readings or interpretations for undeciphered scripts, suggesting that the model recapitulates aspects of human symbol formation.

Conclusion: The findings support the view that pictographic writing emerges naturally from the brain’s intrinsic visual-compression and abstraction mechanisms, rather than from purely arbitrary cultural conventions. The proposed neuro-inspired model offers a concrete computational framework showing how high-level semantic knowledge can guide the generation of low-level visual symbols, and how AI can be used to reconstruct the cognitive processes by which humans first turned perception into written signs.

Abstract: Humans readily recognize objects from sparse line drawings, a capacity that appears early in development and persists across cultures, suggesting neural rather than purely learned origins. Yet the computational mechanism by which the brain transforms high-level semantic knowledge into low-level visual symbols remains poorly understood. Here we propose that ancient pictographic writing emerged from the brain's intrinsic tendency to compress visual input into stable, boundary-based abstractions. We construct a biologically inspired digital twin of the visual hierarchy that encodes an image into low-level features, generates a contour sketch, and iteratively refines it through top-down feedback guided by semantic representations, mirroring the feedforward and recurrent architecture of the human visual cortex. The resulting symbols bear striking structural resemblance to early pictographs across culturally distant writing systems, including Egyptian hieroglyphs, Chinese oracle bone characters, and proto-cuneiform, and offer candidate interpretations for undeciphered scripts. Our findings support a neuro-computational origin of pictographic writing and establish a framework in which AI can recapitulate the cognitive processes by which humans first externalized perception into symbols.
