<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: AI safety research has many benchmarks but no coherent, standardized way to measure and compare safety across them. This fragmentation makes it hard to understand what is actually being evaluated, how, and with which assumptions, and to choose or maintain benchmarks responsibly.

Method: The authors compile and organize 195 AI safety benchmarks (2018–2026) into a multi-sheet workbook that encodes benchmark metadata, metric definitions, associated papers and venues, and repository activity status. They introduce a controlled metadata schema and a complexity taxonomy to classify benchmarks, and then perform descriptive meta-analysis over this structured catalogue.

Result: The analysis shows that benchmark creation has outpaced systematic measurement design. Most benchmarks are of medium complexity; very few are widely popular. Evaluations are heavily English-only, mostly limited to evaluation rather than interventions, and frequently rely on inactive code/data repositories and preprint venues. Metric labels like accuracy or safety score hide substantial variation in judges, aggregation rules, and underlying threat models.

Conclusion: The field’s main problem is not a lack of benchmarks but a fragmented, inconsistent measurement ecosystem. AISafetyBenchExplorer offers a traceable catalogue, standardized metadata, and a complexity taxonomy that together can support more principled benchmark selection, clearer operationalizations of safety, better comparability across studies, and improved long-term stewardship of safety benchmarks.

Abstract: The rapid expansion of large language model (LLM) safety evaluation has produced a substantial benchmark ecosystem, but not a correspondingly coherent measurement ecosystem. We present AISafetyBenchExplorer, a structured catalogue of 195 AI safety benchmarks released between 2018 and 2026, organized through a multi-sheet schema that records benchmark-level metadata, metric-level definitions, benchmark-paper metadata, and repository activity. This design enables meta-analysis not only of what benchmarks exist, but also of how safety is operationalized, aggregated, and judged across the literature. Using the updated catalogue, we identify a central structural problem: benchmark proliferation has outpaced measurement standardization. The current landscape is dominated by medium-complexity benchmarks (94/195), while only 7 benchmarks occupy the Popular tier. The workbook further reports strong concentration around English-only evaluation (165/195), evaluation-only resources (170/195), stale GitHub repositories (137/195), stale Hugging Face datasets (96/195), and heavy reliance on arXiv preprints among benchmarks with known venue metadata. At the metric level, the catalogue shows that familiar labels such as accuracy, F1 score, safety score, and aggregate benchmark scores often conceal materially different judges, aggregation rules, and threat models. We argue that the field's main failure mode is fragmentation rather than scarcity. Researchers now have many benchmark artifacts, but they often lack a shared measurement language, a principled basis for benchmark selection, and durable stewardship norms for post publication maintenance. AISafetyBenchExplorer addresses this gap by providing a traceable benchmark catalogue, a controlled metadata schema, and a complexity taxonomy that together support more rigorous benchmark discovery, comparison, and meta-evaluation.

*Chuyang Xiang,Yichen Wei,Jiale Ma,Handing Wang,Junchi Yan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper presents BEAM, a bi-level optimization framework that uses genetic algorithms and Monte Carlo Tree Search, enhanced with adaptive memory and knowledge augmentation, to automatically design high-performing heuristics and full solvers, outperforming prior LLM-based hyper-heuristics and some human-designed solvers.

  <summary>Details</summary>

They struggle to design complete, competitive solvers, and even variants with hyperparameter tuning or iterative code modification lack high-level algorithmic modeling. This leads to poor exploration efficiency and limited ability to generate complex solver code. There is a need for a framework that can explore high-level algorithmic structures and realize them as concrete heuristics in a structured, efficient way.

Method: The authors reformulate heuristic design as a bi-level optimization problem and propose BEAM (Bi-level Memory-adaptive Algorithmic Evolution). In BEAM, the outer (exterior) level uses a genetic algorithm (GA) to evolve high-level algorithmic structures represented with function placeholders. The inner (interior) level uses Monte Carlo Tree Search (MCTS) to fill in these placeholders with concrete implementations (heuristics/code). An Adaptive Memory module is introduced to support and stabilize complex code generation over time. To ensure meaningful evaluation of complex code, they also propose a Knowledge Augmentation (KA) Pipeline that addresses the limitations of starting from scratch or from rigid code templates, enriching the initial knowledge and search space for LHH-based solver design.

Result: On several optimization problems, BEAM outperforms existing LLM-based hyper-heuristics. In particular, for CVRP (Capacitated Vehicle Routing Problem) hybrid algorithm design, BEAM reduces the optimality gap by 37.84% on aggregate compared to prior LHH methods. Moreover, BEAM automatically designs a heuristic for the Maximum Independent Set (MIS) problem that surpasses the performance of the state-of-the-art human-crafted solver KaMIS.

Conclusion: Bi-level modeling with explicit separation between high-level algorithmic structure search (via GA) and low-level heuristic realization (via MCTS), combined with adaptive memory and knowledge augmentation, substantially improves the effectiveness of LLM-based hyper-heuristics. BEAM can automatically design complex, competitive solvers that not only outperform previous LHH approaches but can even exceed top human-engineered solvers on challenging combinatorial optimization tasks.

Abstract: Large Language Model-based Hyper Heuristic (LHH) has recently emerged as an efficient way for automatic heuristic design. However, most existing LHHs just perform well in optimizing a single function within a pre-defined solver. Their single-layer evolution makes them not effective enough to write a competent complete solver. While some variants incorporate hyperparameter tuning or attempt to generate complex code through iterative local modifications, they still lack a high-level algorithmic modeling, leading to limited exploration efficiency. To address this, we reformulate heuristic design as a Bi-level Optimization problem and propose \textbf{BEAM} (Bi-level Memory-adaptive Algorithmic Evolution). BEAM's exterior layer evolves high-level algorithmic structures with function placeholders through genetic algorithm (GA), while the interior layer realizes these placeholders via Monte Carlo Tree Search (MCTS). We further introduce an Adaptive Memory module to facilitate complex code generation. To support the evaluation for complex code generation, we point out the limitations of starting LHHs from scratch or from code templates and introduce a Knowledge Augmentation (KA) Pipeline. Experimental results on several optimization problems demonstrate that BEAM significantly outperforms existing LHHs, notably reducing the optimality gap by 37.84\% on aggregate in CVRP hybrid algorithm design. BEAM also designs a heuristic that outperforms SOTA Maximum Independent Set (MIS) solver KaMIS.

</details>


### [129] [Drawing on Memory: Dual-Trace Encoding Improves Cross-Session Recall in LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12948)
*Benjamin Stern,Peter Nadel*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes dual-trace memory encoding for LLM agents, where each fact is stored together with a contextual narrative scene, leading to substantially better long-term, cross-session recall and temporal reasoning than fact-only memory, without extra token cost.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM agents with persistent memory typically store information as isolated factual records. This flat representation lacks temporal and contextual structure, making it hard for agents to perform temporal reasoning, track updates over time, or integrate information across many sessions. The authors are motivated by cognitive psychology findings such as the drawing effect and encoding specificity, suggesting that richer, context-laden memory traces improve recall. They aim to bring similar benefits to LLM memory systems.

Method: The authors introduce dual-trace memory encoding, where each fact stored in memory is accompanied by a concrete scene trace: a short narrative reconstruction of the situation and context in which the fact was learned. The agent is required to generate and store both the abstract fact and its contextual scene at encoding time. They evaluate this method on the LongMemEval-S benchmark comprising 4,575 sessions and 100 recall questions. They compare dual-trace encoding to a fact-only control that has matched coverage and format over 99 shared questions. They also analyze token usage and sketch how the approach could be extended to coding agents, including preliminary pilot validation.

Result: On LongMemEval-S, dual-trace memory attains 73.7% overall accuracy compared to 53.5% for the fact-only baseline, a 20.2 percentage point improvement with a 95% confidence interval of [+12.1, +29.3] and bootstrap p < 0.0001. The performance gains are especially strong for tasks requiring temporal reasoning (+40pp), tracking knowledge updates over time (+25pp), and aggregating information across multiple sessions (+30pp). There is no observed benefit for single-session retrieval tasks. Token analysis indicates that these substantial accuracy gains come with no additional token cost relative to the control condition. The authors also report preliminary, qualitative validation of an architectural design to apply dual-trace encoding to coding agents.

Conclusion: Dual-trace memory encoding, which pairs abstract facts with concrete contextual scene traces, significantly improves long-term, cross-session recall and temporal reasoning in LLM agents compared to fact-only memory representations, without increasing token usage. The pattern of improvements aligns with encoding specificity theory, suggesting that richer, context-bound memory traces are especially helpful when retrieval conditions match the original encoding context. The approach appears promising for more complex agent settings such as coding assistants, though further validation beyond preliminary pilots is needed.

Abstract: LLM agents with persistent memory store information as flat factual records, providing little context for temporal reasoning, change tracking, or cross-session aggregation. Inspired by the drawing effect [3], we introduce dual-trace memory encoding. In this method, each stored fact is paired with a concrete scene trace, a narrative reconstruction of the moment and context in which the information was learned. The agent is forced to commit to specific contextual details during encoding, creating richer, more distinctive memory traces. Using the LongMemEval-S benchmark (4,575 sessions, 100 recall questions), we compare dual-trace encoding against a fact-only control with matched coverage and format over 99 shared questions. Dual-trace achieves 73.7% overall accuracy versus 53.5%, a +20.2 percentage point (pp) gain (95% CI: [+12.1, +29.3], bootstrap p < 0.0001). Gains concentrate in temporal reasoning (+40pp), knowledge-update tracking (+25pp), and multi-session aggregation (+30pp), with no benefit for single-session retrieval, consistent with encoding specificity theory [8]. Token analysis shows dual-trace encoding achieves this gain at no additional cost. We additionally sketch an architectural design for adapting dual-trace encoding to coding agents, with preliminary pilot validation.

</details>


### [130] [Modeling Co-Pilots for Text-to-Model Translation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12955)
*Serdar Kadioglu,Karthik Uppuluri,Akash Singirikonda*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Introduces Text2Model (LLM-based co-pilots plus leaderboard) and Text2Zinc (cross-domain dataset and editor) for translating natural language descriptions of satisfaction and optimization problems into MiniZinc models, showing LLMs are promising but not yet reliable push-button tools for combinatorial modeling.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Leverage LLMs to automatically translate natural-language descriptions of combinatorial optimization and satisfaction problems into formal models, addressing the lack of unified, solver-agnostic benchmarks and tools that cover both optimization and satisfaction in a single architecture and dataset.

Method: Design Text2Model as a family of LLM-based co-pilot strategies (single- and multi-call) for text-to-model translation, including zero-shot prompting, chain-of-thought, knowledge-graph intermediate representations, grammar-constrained decoding, and agentic decompositions; build Text2Zinc as a MiniZinc-based, cross-domain dataset plus interactive editor; run comprehensive experiments measuring execution and solution accuracy across strategies and against prior work.

Result: The proposed LLM co-pilot strategies are competitive with, and sometimes outperform, recent methods for text-to-combinatorial-model translation, but still fall short of reliable fully automatic modeling; they demonstrate strengths and weaknesses across different strategies and problem types on the Text2Zinc benchmark.

Conclusion: LLMs show substantial promise for assisting with combinatorial modeling but are not yet robust, push-button solutions; the open-sourced Text2Model co-pilots, leaderboard, Text2Zinc dataset, and interactive editor provide a standardized, solver-agnostic foundation for future research to close this gap.

Abstract: There is growing interest in leveraging large language models (LLMs) for text-to-model translation and optimization tasks. This paper aims to advance this line of research by introducing \textsc{Text2Model} and \textsc{Text2Zinc}. \textsc{Text2Model} is a suite of co-pilots based on several LLM strategies with varying complexity, along with an online leaderboard. \textsc{Text2Zinc} is a cross-domain dataset for capturing optimization and satisfaction problems specified in natural language, along with an interactive editor with built-in AI assistant. While there is an emerging literature on using LLMs for translating combinatorial problems into formal models, our work is the first attempt to integrate \textit{both} satisfaction and optimization problems within a \textit{unified architecture} and \textit{dataset}. Moreover, our approach is \textit{solver-agnostic} unlike existing work that focuses on translation to a solver-specific model. To achieve this, we leverage \textsc{MiniZinc}'s solver-and-paradigm-agnostic modeling capabilities to formulate combinatorial problems. We conduct comprehensive experiments to compare execution and solution accuracy across several single- and multi-call strategies, including; zero-shot prompting, chain-of-thought reasoning, intermediate representations via knowledge-graphs, grammar-based syntax encoding, and agentic approaches that decompose the model into sequential sub-tasks. Our co-pilot strategies are competitive, and in parts improve, recent research in this domain. Our findings indicate that while LLMs are promising they are not yet a push-button technology for combinatorial modeling. We contribute \textsc{Text2Model} co-pilots and leaderboard, and \textsc{Text2Zinc} and interactive editor to open-source to support closing this performance gap.

### [131] [Cycle-Consistent Search: Question Reconstructability as a Proxy Reward for Search Agent Training](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12967)
*Sohyun An,Shuibenyang Yuan,Hayeon Lee,Cho-Jui Hsieh,Alexander Min*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Cycle-Consistent Search (CCS), a reinforcement learning framework that trains search agents for question answering without gold supervision by using cycle-consistency with information bottlenecks as a reward signal.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RL-based search agents in information retrieval depend heavily on gold supervision like ground-truth answers, which is expensive and hard to scale. The authors want a scalable way to train effective search agents when labeled data is scarce or unavailable.

Method: They propose CCS, which treats an optimal search trajectory as a lossless encoding of the question. The agent generates a search trajectory, and a separate model attempts to reconstruct the original question from that trajectory. The reconstruction quality provides a reward for the RL policy. To avoid trivial solutions that exploit lexical overlap (information leakage), they introduce information bottlenecks: removing the final answer from the trajectory and masking named entities in search queries. This pushes the model to rely on retrieved documents and the structural pattern of the search process rather than surface forms.

Result: On standard question-answering benchmarks, CCS achieves performance close to fully supervised RL baselines that use ground-truth answers, and it outperforms other methods that also avoid gold supervision.

Conclusion: Cycle-consistency with carefully designed information bottlenecks can provide a viable, scalable reward signal for training search agents without gold supervision, enabling effective question-answering performance in low- or no-label settings.

Abstract: Reinforcement Learning (RL) has shown strong potential for optimizing search agents in complex information retrieval tasks. However, existing approaches predominantly rely on gold supervision, such as ground-truth answers, which is difficult to scale. To address this limitation, we propose Cycle-Consistent Search (CCS), a gold-supervision-free framework for training search agents, inspired by cycle-consistency techniques from unsupervised machine translation and image-to-image translation. Our key hypothesis is that an optimal search trajectory, unlike insufficient or irrelevant ones, serves as a lossless encoding of the question's intent. Consequently, a high-quality trajectory should preserve the information required to accurately reconstruct the original question, thereby inducing a reward signal for policy optimization. However, naive cycle-consistency objectives are vulnerable to information leakage, as reconstruction may rely on superficial lexical cues rather than the underlying search process. To reduce this effect, we apply information bottlenecks, including exclusion of the final response and named entity recognition (NER) masking of search queries. These constraints force reconstruction to rely on retrieved observations together with the structural scaffold, ensuring that the resulting reward signal reflects informational adequacy rather than linguistic redundancy. Experiments on question-answering benchmarks show that CCS achieves performance comparable to supervised baselines while outperforming prior methods that do not rely on gold supervision. These results suggest that CCS provides a scalable training paradigm for training search agents in settings where gold supervision is unavailable.

</details>


### [132] [Bilevel Late Acceptance Hill Climbing for the Electric Capacitated Vehicle Routing Problem](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.13013)
*Yinghao Qin,Mosab Bazargani,Edmund K. Burke,Carlos A. Coello Coello,Zhongmin Song,Jun Chen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Proposes a bilevel optimization with a Late Acceptance Hill Climbing algorithm for the Electric Capacitated Vehicle Routing Problem, using a surrogate objective to guide search and showing state-of-the-art results on benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Efficiently solving the Electric Capacitated Vehicle Routing Problem requires simultaneously handling routing and charging decisions, which is challenging at large scale. Existing methods can struggle with computational complexity and parameter tuning, especially on large benchmark instances, motivating a simpler yet effective framework that can exploit problem structure, scale well, and provide high-quality solutions under limited evaluation budgets.

Method: Introduce a bilevel optimization framework for E-CVRP where routing and charging are modeled at two levels. Define a surrogate upper-level objective capturing the interaction between routing and charging to guide search. Develop a bilevel Late Acceptance Hill Climbing (b-LAHC) algorithm with three phases: greedy descent for rapid improvement, neighborhood exploration for diversification, and final refinement for intensification. Use fixed algorithm parameters to avoid complex adaptation and maintain algorithmic simplicity. Evaluate the method on the IEEE WCCI-2020 benchmark against eight state-of-the-art algorithms under a fixed evaluation budget.

Result: On the IEEE WCCI-2020 benchmark, the proposed b-LAHC algorithm delivers superior or competitive performance versus eight state-of-the-art methods. It finds near-optimal solutions on small-scale instances and establishes 9 out of 10 new best-known results on large-scale instances, achieving an average improvement of 1.07% over previous records.

Conclusion: The bilevel framework with a surrogate upper-level objective effectively speeds up convergence while preserving solution quality, and the simple, parameter-free b-LAHC algorithm is highly competitive for E-CVRP. The observed strong—though not perfect—correlation between the surrogate objective and the full cost validates using the surrogate as a search guide but also shows that a joint solution of both levels remains necessary. Overall, the approach is promising for efficiently solving large-scale hierarchical routing problems.

Abstract: This paper tackles the Electric Capacitated Vehicle Routing Problem (E-CVRP) through a bilevel optimization framework that handles routing and charging decisions separately or jointly depending on the search stage. By analyzing their interaction, we introduce a surrogate objective at the upper level to guide the search and accelerate convergence. A bilevel Late Acceptance Hill Climbing algorithm (b-LAHC) is introduced that operates through three phases: greedy descent, neighborhood exploration, and final solution refinement. b-LAHC operates with fixed parameters, eliminating the need for complex adaptation while remaining lightweight and effective. Extensive experiments on the IEEE WCCI-2020 benchmark show that b-LAHC achieves superior or competitive performance against eight state-of-the-art algorithms. Under a fixed evaluation budget, it attains near-optimal solutions on small-scale instances and sets 9/10 new best-known results on large-scale benchmarks, improving existing records by an average of 1.07%. Moreover, the strong correlation (though not universal) observed between the surrogate objective and the complete cost justifies the use of the surrogate objective while still necessitating a joint solution of both levels, thereby validating the effectiveness of the proposed bilevel framework and highlighting its potential for efficiently solving large-scale routing problems with a hierarchical structure.

</details>


### [133] [PAL: Personal Adaptive Learner](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.13017)
*Megha Chakraborty,Darssan L. Eswaramoorthi,Madhur Thareja,Het Riteshkumar Shah,Finlay Palmer,Aryaman Bahl,Michelle A Ihetu,Amit Sheth*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper presents PAL, an AI platform that converts lecture videos into interactive, real-time adaptive learning experiences with personalized summaries.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing AI education platforms offer limited, static personalization, such as fixed quizzes, constant pacing, and generic feedback, which cannot adequately respond to a learner's changing understanding during a lesson. There is a need for educational systems that can interpret rich lecture content and adapt in real time to support individual learners more effectively.

Method: The authors design PAL (Personal Adaptive Learner), an AI platform that performs multimodal analysis of lecture videos, then interactively engages learners with questions of varying difficulty during the lesson. The system monitors learner responses and adjusts question difficulty and engagement strategy in real time. After the session, PAL generates a personalized summary emphasizing key concepts and incorporating examples aligned with the learner's interests.

Result: PAL successfully converts traditional lecture videos into interactive sessions where content, questions, and difficulty are adapted on the fly based on learner performance. The system is able to produce individualized summaries and interest-tailored examples at the end of each learning session, demonstrating the feasibility of real-time adaptive support using multimodal lecture analysis.

Conclusion: By combining multimodal content analysis with adaptive decision-making, PAL offers a novel framework for responsive digital learning that goes beyond static personalization. The work shows that AI can deliver real-time, individualized support in video-based education, addressing a central limitation of current AI-driven learning platforms.

Abstract: AI-driven education platforms have made some progress in personalisation, yet most remain constrained to static adaptation--predefined quizzes, uniform pacing, or generic feedback--limiting their ability to respond to learners' evolving understanding. This shortfall highlights the need for systems that are both context-aware and adaptive in real time. We introduce PAL (Personal Adaptive Learner), an AI-powered platform that transforms lecture videos into interactive learning experiences. PAL continuously analyzes multimodal lecture content and dynamically engages learners through questions of varying difficulty, adjusting to their responses as the lesson unfolds. At the end of a session, PAL generates a personalized summary that reinforces key concepts while tailoring examples to the learner's interests. By uniting multimodal content analysis with adaptive decision-making, PAL contributes a novel framework for responsive digital learning. Our work demonstrates how AI can move beyond static personalization toward real-time, individualized support, addressing a core challenge in AI-enabled education.
